==PAGE:2==
Preface
This study explores the environmental impacts of producing 1,4-butanediol and adipic acid from
residual biomass and through biochemical processes. Life cycle assessment (LCA) is used to high-
light hot-spots during the production of these bio-based chemicals, and to compare them with their
conventional counterparts.
The study constitutes the thesis and concluding element of the master of science in environmental
engineering at the Department of Environmental Engineering at the Technical University of Den-
mark (DTU Environment). The thesis has been undertaken from August 2020 through January
2021 and corresponds to 30 ECTS points per author. The work has been split equally between the
two authors, while the LCA of 1,4-butanediol was carried out by Julie, and the LCA of adipic acid
was carried out by Soﬁe.
We would like to thank our supervisors, Senior Researcher at DTU Environment, Anders Damgaard,
and Professor at DTU Chemical Engineering, John Woodley, for their support through the process.
John Woodley has been a great help when we were mapping the biochemical production processes
- a task that proved fairly challenging to us. Anders Damgaard has provided us with very valuable
guidance for the execution of our LCA.
Main supervisor: Anders Damgaard, Senior Researcher at DTU Environment
Co-supervisor: John Woodley, Professor at DTU Chemical Engineering
Date: Kgs. Lyngby, 2021-01-24
Soﬁe Anthonisen
Julie Kathrine Lyager

I
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
In recent years the motivation to produce chemicals less reliant on fossil fuels has increased, due to a
general concern for climate change and depletion of fossil resources (Cespi et al., 2020; Fantke and
Ernstoff, 2018; Dunn et al., 2016). Although some studies have suggested that bio-based chemicals
may emit less greenhouse gases than fossil-based, it has also been demonstrated that trade-offs with
other environmental impacts can occur (Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018; Cespi et al., 2020).
Life cycle assessment (LCA) is a valuable tool accounting for an array of environmental impacts,
which can quantify both the positive and negative environmental consequences of a product. In
the present study LCA was used to explore the cradle-to-gate impacts of producing 1,4-butanediol
(BDO) and adipic acid (ADA) from second generation (2G) biomass feedstocks and through bio-
chemical conversion methods. The environmental impacts of the conventional counterparts of both
chemicals were drawn in to compare with the bio-based chemicals.
Ideally, the inventory of an LCA relies on primary data. In the present study it was not possible
to obtain this, and instead the inventories were based on literature sources. For bio-based BDO
the inventory data was largely based on simulation data from techno-economic analyses, while for
bio-based ADA the inventory was to a large extent based on laboratory scale data, since bio-based
ADA is not produced commercially to date. Both inventories included many rough assumptions.
The data quality and use of assumptions introduced large uncertainties to the results.
Considering the uncertainty in the results it could not be concluded if the bio-based or conven-
tional chemicals were environmentally preferable. For bio-based BDO the results suggested that a
decrease in fossil resource scarcity impact could be obtained. For bio-based ADA the results indi-
cated considerably higher freshwater and marine eutrophication impacts. For both BDO and ADA
the bio-based alternatives entailed much higher land use impacts due to the biomass feedstock.
Some major contributors to the life cycle impacts of the chemicals were identiﬁed. For both BDO
and ADA the procedure used to model the environmental burden of the 2G biomass feedstock was
crucial for their environmental performance. Furthermore, it was evident for both chemicals that
the handling of by-product biomass had a determining inﬂuence on the climate change and fossil
resource scarcity impacts due to crediting of heat produced in the combustion of these by-products.
Additionally, for bio-based BDO some processes contributing considerably to one or more impact
categories were the drying of harvested biomass, the enzymatic hydrolysis of corn stover and the
fermentation process. For bio-based ADA the lignin ﬁrst pretreatment, and methanol as well as
catalyst recovery contributed signiﬁcantly to the environmental impacts.
To improve the conﬁdence and usability of the results it was deemed essential to improve the
quality of the inventory data and to assess more scenarios including different biomass feedstocks
and different biomass pretreatment methods.

==PAGE:8==
1
Introduction
General
The climate of the Earth is changing at an unprecedented pace and the changes are occurring in
response to human activities (Wuebbles, 2018; Amini, 2019). Climate change due to increased
greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions has even been deemed the biggest global health threat in the 21st
century (WHO, 2019; Watts et al., 2018). The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC)
reports with high conﬁdence that fossil fuel combustion and industrial processes contributed with
78% of the increased emissions observed from 1970 to 2010 (IPCC, 2014). Hence, in order to
reduce the impacts of climate change it is necessary to reduce the dependency on fossil fuels (Skoog
et al., 2018).
According to Bennet and Page (2017) and IPCC (2014) the chemical industry is one of the single
industrial sectors with the highest emissions worldwide, and is heavily dependent on fossil re-
sources, which more than 97% of all chemical products are derived from. The International Energy
Agency (IEA) estimates that approximately half of the energy input to the chemical industry is used
as feedstock rather than a source of energy (IEA, 2020a). This leaves the chemical industry as the
industrial subsector with the largest consumption of oil and gas and with the third largest emissions
of GHGs (880 million tonnes CO2 equivalents (eq) directly emitted from primary chemical produc-
tion in 2018 (IEA, 2020a)). Increasing demand for plastics, fertilisers and other chemical products
is expected to increase the future demand for energy in the chemical industry IEA (2020a). In
Figure 1.1 the amounts of various fossil resources used for chemical production are outlined.
Figure 1.1: The chemical sector is the largest industrial energy consumer, but only the third largest
source of industrial CO2 emissions (IEA, 2020b). Figure from IEA (2020b).
1
==PAGE:9==
In the light of this, there has been an increasing interest in transitioning towards a chemical industry
less reliant on fossil resources (Lee et al., 2019). The European Commission (EC) states that a gen-
eral shift to biological raw materials and processing methods has an emission reduction potential
of 2.5 billion tonnes CO2-eq per year by 2030 (EC, 2020b). This shift in food, energy and material
production is often referred to as the bioeconomy (EC, 2018, 2020a), while the transition to bio-
logical feedstocks within chemical production is often related to the concept of Green Chemistry
(US EPA, 2020; Lewandowski, 2014; Isac-García et al., 2016).
Bulk chemicals are generally produced in large volumes and with low prices (Bennet and Page,
2017). These include platform chemicals, which are deﬁned as forming the base of more complex
products (EC, 2004). The annual production of fossil-based platform chemicals is estimated to be
330 million tonnes (Pachapur et al., 2016). The scale of production makes the potential emission
reductions from bulk and platform chemicals an area of great interest.
Examples of important platform chemicals produced in high volumes are 1,4-butanediol (BDO)
and adipic acid (ADA) (Straathof and Bampouli, 2017). The major applications of BDO are in
the production of raw materials for spandex ﬁbres and in engineering plastics (Silva et al., 2020),
whereas ADA is a monomer primarily used in the production of 6,6 polyamide, commonly known
as nylon (Wu, 2016). Several conventional production methods exist for both the production of
BDO and ADA from fossil feedstocks. On the contrary, only one method for producing BDO
from biological feedstocks (bio-BDO) has reached industrial scale, while the production of ADA
from biological feedstocks (bio-ADA) has only reached pilot-scale (Ingram and Le, 2013; Bern-
hard et al., 2014). Bio-BDO is currently being produced by Novamont-owned Mater Biotech in
Italy, which uses a Genomatica engineered Escherichia coli (E. coli) to convert glucose in a direct
fermentation process. The companies previously operating pilot-scale production of bio-ADA are
no longer on the market, however a lot of efforts are given to develop biological processes from
both glucose and residual biomass such as lignin (Bernhard et al., 2014).
In the transition from fossil to bio-based chemicals it is important to avoid burden shifting, which
refers to decreasing one environmental burden at the expense of increasing another. Holistic en-
vironmental assessments can help avoiding this while supporting sound decision making (Fantke
and Ernstoff, 2018; Tufvesson et al., 2013). Life cycle assessment (LCA) is a strong quantitative
tool to evaluate the potential impacts of a deﬁned system across a range of environmental impact
categories for its entire life cycle (Bjørn et al., 2018d). LCA enables the comparison of multiple
pathways for producing the same functional product and detecting the most environmentally sig-
niﬁcant stages of the product life cycle (Bjørn et al., 2018d; Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018; DS/EN ISO
14044, 2006). In this study LCA will be used to compare the conventional production of BDO and
ADA from fossil feedstocks with the biochemical production of bio-BDO and bio-ADA from bio-
logical feedstocks. Furthermore, the main contributing life cycle stages of bio-BDO and bio-ADA
production will be explored.
2
==PAGE:10==
2
Aim
General
The overall aim of the present study is to provide a holistic picture of the environmental impact
of the biochemical production of the platform chemicals BDO and ADA, produced from residual
biomass, also known as 2nd generation (2G) biomass. The aim will be achieved by conducting a
literature study which will be the basis of two full cradle-to-gate LCA studies. The results will be
examined in the light of the environmental impacts of the corresponding conventional petrochemi-
cal production.
A secondary aim is to identify hot-spots of the biochemical alternatives conducting contribution
analysis which will shed light on the most contributing unit processes and production stages. The
results of the present study will be discussed in the light of results of LCA studies found in literature
and areas of improvements will be identiﬁed.
3
Background
General
The following section provides background information on the two chemicals of focus, BDO and
ADA. It consists of clariﬁcation of terms and units of measure, the argumentation for choosing the
above mentioned chemicals, brief descriptions of the chemicals and their market size and applica-
tions. Furthermore, the conventional and biochemical production routes are outlined.
3.1
Clariﬁcation of terms and units of measure
General
Bio-based, fossil-based, biochemical and petrochemical
Confusion may arise when dealing with terms related to the production of chemicals from various
feedstocks and through various processes. Chemicals derived from biological feedstocks are re-
ferred to as "bio-based" chemicals, which is in contrast to "fossil-based" chemicals derived from
fossil feedstocks (USDA, 2016; DS/EN 16760, 2015). Fossil-based chemicals are the conventional
type of chemicals produced through petrochemical processes. In the production of "bio-based"
chemicals there are no requirements to the conversion process being biological, as long as the feed-
stock is of biological origin. Conversion processes that are biological, such as the microbial fer-
mentation using living cells (e.g bacteria or yeast) or components of living cells (e.g. enzymes), are
referred to as bio-processes (Singh, 2015). Production of chemicals using at least one bio-process
is referred to as a "biochemical" production. A chemical obtained from biochemical production
does not require a biological feedstock.
In the present study, "bio-BDO" and "bio-ADA" are referring to bio-based BDO or bio-based ADA
(produced from biological feedstock) and through a biochemical production process (containing at
least one bio-process).
3
==PAGE:11==
Units of measure
The present study generally uses metric units of measure. One "tonne" refers to the mass equal to
1000 kg. This means that other ton units (short tons, imperial tons) have been converted to metric
tonnes, and have been assumed metric when not speciﬁed otherwise in literature sources.
3.2
Selection of chemicals, production methods and feedstock for case study
General
Chemicals
Within the manufacturing of chemicals one speak of bulk chemicals or speciality chemicals, where
the latter describes chemicals providing greater added-value to their products and are produced
in relatively small volumes. Bulk chemicals are generally produced in larger volumes and with
lower prices than speciality chemicals (Bennet and Page, 2017). Bulk chemicals include platform
chemicals, which are deﬁned as forming the base of more complex products (EC, 2004). Due to
the large production volumes, reducing the relative environmental impacts from production of bulk
chemicals, will potentially lead to high absolute reductions globally.
In the present study two chemicals, BDO and ADA, are selected as case subjects. Both BDO
and ADA are considered bulk, platform chemicals underlying the production of large variety of
consumer products. The selection of BDO and ADA was based on (1) their large global production
volumes, (2) the potential of producing them biochemically and from biological feedstocks, (3)
available data for their conventional production to facilitate comparison, and (4) they both have
a petrochemical counterpart. Furthermore, their alternative bio-based and biochemical production
pathways are well-studied research topics. Both BDO and ADA are considered bulk chemicals
used in a large variety of consumer products.
Production methods
The selection of which production methods to compare for both BDO and ADA is based on a re-
view of the literature. Petrochemical production methods are mapped to reveal the most applied
conventional production methods for both BDO and ADA (Reppe process followed by hydrogena-
tion for BDO and two-step oxidation of cyclohexane for ADA). Biochemical production methods
are also mapped for BDO and ADA. For BDO the most relevant method is deemed the direct fer-
mentation of sugars, since an industrial scale production plant applying this method exist. For
ADA the fermentation of lignin-derivatives to cis,cis-muconic acid and further hydrogenation is
deemed most relevant due to the level of development compared to alternatives. In Section 3.3 and
3.4 the selected processes will be described, while an overview of the underlying mapping of both
petrochemical and alternative biochemical production methods is found in Appendix A.3 and A.4.
Feedstocks
In relation to biomass one talks about ﬁrst and second generation (1G and 2G) biomass. 1G biomass
comes from cereal or other food crops, which means that the use of 1G biomass for bioenergy or
chemical feedstock purposes will entail competition with the food market (Attard et al., 2015). 2G
4
==PAGE:12==
biomass is usually obtained as a by-product or residual from 1G biomass production, typically of
lignocellulosic composition, and does not compete with the food market (Attard et al., 2015). The
selection of biological feedstock for bio-BDO and bio-ADA production is based on two main crite-
ria, namely, that (1) the biomass is of 2G origin, and (2) that data is available for the pretreatment to
obtain the constituents needed for the selected biochemical production methods. The most relevant
2G biomass proved to be corn stover for both BDO and ADA.
3.3
1,4-butanediol: Market, applications and production
BDO
1,4-butanediol (BDO), CAS 110-63-4, is a four-carbon, primary alcohol with the molecular for-
mula C4H10O2 and a molecular weight of 90.122 g/mol (ECHA, 2020b). Its molecule has an
alkane structure with two terminal hydroxyl groups, see Figure 3.1 (Gräfje et al., 2019). BDO is
a colour- and odourless liquid, which is readily soluble in water, alcohols, ketones, glycol ethers,
and glycol ether acetates (Gräfje et al., 2019). Its properties makes BDO a versatile chemical in-
termediate (Gräfje et al., 2019). BDO does not occur naturally in the environment and there are
no known microorganisms producing it naturally (Yim et al., 2011; Andreozzi et al., 2016). It is
conventionally produced through petrochemical pathways, however, in recent decades technologies
to derive BDO from non-fossil feedstocks and through biological pathways have emerged (Gräfje
et al., 2019; Ingram and Le, 2013; Silva et al., 2020).
1,4-butanediol
Properties:
Primary alcohol
Color- and odorless liquid
Readily soluble in water
Molecular weight 90.122 g/mol
Figure 3.1: Chemical structure and summarised properties of 1,4-butanediol.
3.3.1
Market size and price of 1,4-butanediol
BDO is considered a major commodity chemical with an estimated global market of 1.5 million
tonnes per year in 2012, and is used to make more than 2.5 million tonnes of plastic and span-
dex ﬁbers anually (Yim et al., 2011; Ingram and Le, 2013). According to Gräfje et al. (2019)
around 435,000 tonnes/year are produced in Europe, 350,000 tonnes/year in the United States and
1,000,0000 tonnes/year in Asia (mainly China). In 2025 the global market of BDO is projected to
reach a value of USD 12.6 billion according to private market research and consulting company,
Grand View Research (2017). According to Straathof and Bampouli (2017) the BDO market price
was 2000 C/ton in 2014.
5
==PAGE:13==
3.3.2
Applications of 1,4-butanediol
BDO is used as an organic solvent and a chemical intermediate in the production of adhesives,
ﬁbres and polyurethanes (Silva et al., 2020; Gräfje et al., 2019). A major part of the BDO produced
globally is used to produce raw materials for the spandex ﬁbre industry (Silva et al., 2020; Simola
et al., 2017). In this process BDO commonly undergoes acid-catalysed dehydration to produce
tetrahydrofuran (THF), which further undergoes acid-catalysed polymerisation to produce polyte-
tramethylene ether glycol (PTMEG), a key material used in spandex ﬁbres. Also the production
of polybutylene terephthalate (PBT) resins for engineering plastics account for a large part of the
BDO consumption (Silva et al., 2020; Simola et al., 2017). In this process PBT is produced either
by transesteriﬁcation of terephtalic acid dimethyl ester (DMT) and BDO or polycondensation of
puriﬁed terephtalic acid (PTA) and BDO (Eipper, 2007).
3.3.3
Conventional production of 1,4-butanediol
The majority of BDO consumed globally is currently being produced from fossil resources. Ac-
cording to Gräfje et al. (2019), Ingram and Le (2013) and Silva et al. (2020) the major petrochem-
ical production pathways for BDO include:
(1) Hydrogenation of 2-butyne-1,4-diol derived from acetylene (Reppe process)
(2) Hydrogenation of maleic anhydride derived from butane (Davy process)
(3) Acetoxylation of butadiene
(4) Isomerisation of propylene oxide
The Reppe process was the ﬁrst pathway used to produce 1,4-butanediol in the 1930s and is still
the most widely used production process today (Ingram and Le, 2013; MarkorChem, 2020; Gräfje
et al., 2019). According to Ingram and Le (2013) the Reppe process pathway accounted for 40%
of the production of BDO in 2013. The key producers of BDO through the Reppe process pathway
are BASF, Ashland, DuPont and Invista (Ingram and Le, 2013; Izawa and Yokoyama, 2016). Due
to its large share in the BDO production the Reppe process has been selected as the petrochemical
production pathway used for comparison in the present study. In the following section the Reppe
process will be described in detail and the other listed petrochemical production methods will be
outlined in Appendix A.3.
3.3.3.1
Reppe process
The production of BDO through the Reppe process requires two key steps; (1) the production of
2-butyne-1,4-diol and (2) the hydrogenation of 2-butyne-1,4-diol to BDO. The feedstock for the
process is acetylene, which is derived from mainly natural gas, shalegas, naphtha or coal (Gräfje
et al., 2019; Ingram and Le, 2013). The formation of 2-butyne-1,4-diol in the ﬁrst step proceeds
6
==PAGE:14==
with the following reaction (Copenhaver and Bigelow, 1949):
HC CH+2 HCHO −→HOCH2C CCH2OH
(1)
Acetylene
Formaldehyde
2−butyne−1,4−diol
The formation of BDO through hydrogenation in the second step proceeds as follows (Izawa and
Yokoyama, 2016):
HOCH2C CCH2OH
Raney Ni, H2
−−−−−−−→HOCH2CH CHCH2OH
(2)
1,4−butynediol
1,4−butanediol (BDO)
The majority of chemical producers keep their industrial processes and operating conditions pro-
prietary, which is why the best available knowledge on detailed BDO production through the Reppe
process relies on old literature (Copenhaver and Bigelow (1949)) or more recent, derived literature
(Haas et al. (2005), Izawa and Yokoyama (2016), and Teh et al. (2019)). The detailed description
of BDO production through these sources is presented in the following, and is assumed to roughly
represent current production of BDO with the Reppe process. The process diagram as adapted from
these literature sources can be seen in Figure 3.2.
(1) In the ﬁrst production step gaseous acetylene (C2H2) is introduced into a mix of an aqueous
solution of formaldehyde (CH2O) and recycled liquid from downstream distillation to obtain a
maximum of 12% of formaldehyde. The catalyst in the reaction is copper-bismuth-acetylide (Haas
et al., 2005; Copenhaver and Bigelow, 1949; Izawa and Yokoyama, 2016). 10-15% copper (II)
oxide and 3-6% bismuth oxide is supported on a granular silica gel. The copper (II) oxide forms
copper (I) acetylide in the process which complexes with acetylene to function as the active catalyst.
The oligomeric acetylene complexes on the catalyst may form water soluble cuprenes, which build
up on the catalyst, but this formation is inhibited by the bismuth oxide in the catalyst (Haas et al.,
2005). According to Haas et al. (2005) both ﬁxed bed and ﬂuidised bed reactor conﬁgurations have
been used for this production step. The ﬁxed bed conﬁguration requires higher operating pressure
than the ﬂuidised bed conﬁguration, which may be undesired due to the highly explosive nature
of acetylene at increased pressure. According to Copenhaver and Bigelow (1949) the reaction is
operated at 5 atm and 90-110°C, whereas Haas et al. (2005) reports 1-6 bar and 70-100°C. The pH
should be held between 5-8 (Teh et al., 2019; Haas et al., 2005).
Aside from the major product 2-butyne-1,4-diol (Equation 1) propargyl alcohol is formed as a
by-product in the following reaction:
HC CH+HCHO −→HC C−CH2OH

After reaction acetylene is removed from the product and recycled (Figure 3.2). This leaves the
concentration of 2-butyne-1,4-diol in the product at 30-33% (Copenhaver and Bigelow, 1949).
The product is then distilled to remove methanol, unreacted formaldehyde, and propargyl alcohol.
Formaldehyde and propargyl alcohol are recirculated to the reactor, which helps to keep formalde-
hyde in excess and to suppress the formation of propargyl alcohol in the reaction (Figure 3.2). The
percentage of butynediol then reaches 35% (Copenhaver and Bigelow, 1949).
(2) In the second step the 35% butynediol solution is hydrogenated in a tower over a nickel or
palladium catalyst (Copenhaver and Bigelow, 1949; Haas et al., 2005). According to Gräfje et al.
(2019) nickel catalysts are the most widely used, such as nickel supported on oxides of zirconium
or aluminium, or Raney nickel catalysts. The yield of BDO from 2-butyne-1,4-diol is reported to
8
==PAGE:16==
be around 90% with respect to acetylene (Izawa and Yokoyama, 2016). The temperature is held
constant in the reaction at a point between 70-140°C depending on the efﬁciency of the catalyst.
The operating pressure is 300 atm (Copenhaver and Bigelow, 1949).
The reactions related to the formation of BDO through high-pressure hydrogenation of 2-butyne-
1,4-diol can be seen in Figure 3.3. It is important that the temperature is held constant, and that the
butenediol is rapidly reacted to BDO. If the pH is low the butenediol may rearrange to 4-hydroxy-
butanal, which requires higher temperature to react towards BDO. As represented in Figure 3.3
small amounts of furan, THF and n-butanol are also formed.

3.3.4
Biochemical production of 1,4-butanediol
In the last decade technologies to derive BDO from non-fossil feedstocks and through biological
pathways have emerged (Gräfje et al., 2019; Ingram and Le, 2013). These include:
(1) Direct fermentation of sugars using genetically engineered microorganisms
(2) Catalytic hydrogenation of biosuccinic acid
(3) Using furfural or furan as feedstock
(4) Esteriﬁcation followed by hydrogenolysis of succinic acid
In 2011 the biotechnology and chemical company Genomatica was granted the patent to the inven-
tion of a metabolically engineered Escherichia coli (E. coli) organism with biosynthetic production
capabilities for BDO (Burk et al., 2011). As BDO is not produced naturally in any known or-
ganism, metabolic engineering of E. coli was necessary to produce BDO through direct microbial
fermentation (Yim et al., 2011). To the best knowledge of the author commercial production of
bio-BDO has only been achieved this far using the E. coli engineered by Genomatica. Hence, the
direct fermentation of sugars to BDO using E. coli has been selected as the biochemical pathway
used for comparison in the current study. In the following sections this pathway will be described
in detail and other listed biochemical pathways are outlined in Appendix A.3.
9
==PAGE:17==
3.3.4.1
Direct fermentation of sugars
Genomatica’s desire to metabolically engineer a microorganism to produce BDO was inspired by
successful production of biochemicals such as 1,3-propanediol, 1,2-propanediol, isobutanol, iso-
prene and putrescine (Yim et al., 2011). Their metabolically engineered BDO-producing E. coli
was obtained by using a genome-scale metabolic model of E. coli and biopathway prediction al-
gorithms (Yim et al., 2011). Using their own SimPheny Biopathway Predictor software 10,000
potential BDO pathways were revealed, and subsequently prioritised to select the BDO pathways
that were most likely to lead to high performance. The prioritisation attributes included the path-
way length, the number of non-native pathway steps, the number of novel pathway steps, thermo-
dynamic feasibility and maximum theoretical BDO yield (Yim et al., 2011; Barton et al., 2014).
Genomatica used the gained knowledge in the laboratory to introduce two heterologous BDO path-
ways in E. coli. Furthermore, the E. coli host organism was engineered to optimise its BDO pro-
duction performance.
Since 2011 when Genomatica was granted the patent to the invention of the BDO-producing engi-
neered E. coli they have continued to optimise its performance and prepare it for commercialisation
(Burgard et al., 2016; Andreozzi et al., 2016). According to Genomatica (2015a) the ﬁrst commer-
cial volumes of bio-BDO were produced in 2012/2013. According to a joint press release in 2015
BASF and Genomatica expanded their license agreement for bio-BDO production by BASF us-
ing Genomatica’s engineered E. coli, also stating that BASF had already been producing bio-BDO
under an existing license agreement (BASF, 2015). However, in 2016 Mater-Biotech, owned by
Novamont in Italy, opened the ﬁrst commercial plant producing 30,000 tonnes/year of bio-BDO
relying on Genomatica’s engineered E. coli (Novamont, 2020; Genomatica, 2015a). This is to
the best knowledge of the author also the only commercially running bio-BDO production plant.
Novamont claims that their Mater-Biotech bio-BDO has a 50% lower carbon footprint than BDO
derived from fossil sources. Furthermore, Novament claims, that the environmental sustainability
of their Mater-Biotech bio-BDO production has been enhanced by considering energy efﬁciency
and utilisation of by-products in the process Novamont (2016).
The fermentation of sugars to produce BDO requires two main steps: (1) the biological conversion
of sugars in direct fermentation relying on E. coli engineered by Genomatica, and (2) the down-
stream concentration and puriﬁcation of the fermentation broth to obtain the pure BDO product.
(1) Genomatica divides their biological pathway into two main steps: (a) the biosynthesis of 4-
hydroxybutyrate and (b) the conversion of 4-hydroxybutyrate to BDO (Yim et al., 2011). (a) In
the upstream pathway in a series of enzymatic reactions 4-hydroxybutyrate is bio-synthesised from
succinate. In the ﬁrst step succinate is activated by succinyl-CoA synthetase to succinyl CoA.
Succinyl CoA is then reduced by CoA-dependent succinate semialdehyde dehydrogenase to suc-
cinyl semialdehyde. Succinyl semialdehyde is then reduced by 4-hydroxybutyrate dehydrogenase
to 4-hydroxybutyrate. (Yim et al., 2011) (Figure 3.4). Alternatively, α-ketoglutarate is activated
10
==PAGE:18==
by 2-oxoglutarate decarboxylase to succinyl semialdehyde, thus skipping the succinyl CoA step
when starting from succinate. These two alternatives represent the difference between the two
heterologous pathways (Yim et al., 2011).
(b) In the downstream pathway 4-hydroxybutyrate
is activated by 4-hydroxybutyryl-CoA transferase to 4-hydroxybutyryl CoA, which is ﬁnally ac-
tivated by 4-hydroxybutyryl-coA reductase to 4-hydroxybutyraldehyde. The ﬁnal step from 4-
hydroxybutyraldehyde to BDO is activated by alcohol dehydrogenase and occurs naturally in E.
coli (Yim et al., 2011) (Figure 3.4).
Figure 3.4: Biological pathway for the synthesis of BDO in metabolically engineered E. coli from
Genomatica. Graphic from from Yim et al. (2011).
(2) In the downstream pathway the obtained BDO-containing fermentation broth needs to undergo
concentration and puriﬁcation steps to obtain the pure BDO product. The downstream steps used
at the Novamont-owned Mater Biotech’s bio-BDO producing plant in Italy are not publicly known.
However, the same year as Mater Biotech started producing bio-BDO using Genomatica’s engi-
neered E. coli, Genomatica published a scientiﬁc article about their development of a commercial
scale process for production of bio-BDO (Burgard et al., 2016). The article describes the work of
Yim et al. (2011) to engineer the BDO-producing E. coli strain and Genomatica’s further optimisa-
tion of the strain performance to obtain commercially relevant titers, productivity and yield of BDO
from sugar. Burgard et al. (2016) describes the downstream steps as: sterilization of fermentation
broth, removal of cells by centrifugation or microﬁltration, removal of large biomolecules and some
salts by nano- and ultraﬁltration, removal of remaining salts by ion exchange, evaporation of water,
and ﬁnally distillation to obtain the BDO product.
The type of sugar used as substrate for biosynthesis of BDO was initially the six-carbon sugar,
glucose, but also the disaccharide, sucrose, was employed (Yim et al., 2011). The ﬁve-carbon
sugar, xylose, and mixed sugar streams were also tested as substrates and showed more or less
"equal speciﬁc production rates" as when using glucose (Yim et al., 2011). Mixed sugar streams
11
==PAGE:19==
are typically obtained from pretreatment of 2G biomass of lignocellulosic origin. However, the
derivation of sugars from lignocellulose requires more intensive pretreatment than the derivation of
glucose from 1G biomass.
3.4
Adipic acid: Market, applications and production
ADA
ADA, CAS 124-04-9, also commonly known as hexanedioic acid or 1,4-butanedicarboxylic acid. It
is a colour- and odourless crystal having an acidic taste (Musser, 2005; Oppenheim and Dickerson,
2014; Althaus et al., 2007). The molecular formula of ADA is C6H10O4 (see Figure 3.5) and it has
a molecular weight of 146.14 g/mol (Musser, 2005; Luttrell and Klaassen, 2016; Oppenheim and
Dickerson, 2014; Althaus et al., 2007). ADA is classiﬁed as very soluble in methanol and ethanol,
soluble in acetone and ethyl acetate and very slightly soluble in benzene and cyclohexane (Musser,
2005; ECHA, 2020a; Luttrell and Klaassen, 2016). ADA is stable to most oxidising agents (Musser,
2005). Natural appearance of ADA is very rare (Musser, 2005; Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014),
hence it does not occur in known natural biosynthetic pathways (Wu, 2016).
Adipic acid
Properties:
Dicarboxylic acid
Water solubility: 1.42 g/100g water (15°C)
Soluble in methanol and ethanol
Molecular weight 146.14 g/mol
Figure 3.5: Chemical structure and summarised properties of adipic acid (Musser, 2005).
3.4.1
Market size and price of adipic acid
The market size is typically estimated based on the production of end-use application, thus, the
numbers come with some uncertainty. According to the market analysis ﬁrm BCC Research (2019)
report ’Global Market for Adipic Acid’ the market size of ADA was 3.8 million tonnes/year in 2017
of which more than 50% was produced in the Asian-Paciﬁc region. Another market intelligence
and advisory ﬁrm, Modor Intelligence, state that the production of ADA in 2016 was 3.15 million
tonnes (Mordor Intelligence, 2020). Other estimates of the market size range from 2.3 million
tonnes/year in 2016 (Wu, 2016), over 2.6 million tonnes/year in 2015 (Bart and Cavallaro, 2015b),
to 2.7 million tonnes/year in 2017 (Gunukula and Anex, 2017). In Bernhard et al. (2014) the total
demand in 2017 was about 2.5 million tonnes/year with a price of 1500 e/tonnes.
3.4.2
Applications of adipic acid
ADA is the most, commercially, important aliphatic dicarboxylic acid (Musser, 2005; Oppenheim
and Dickerson, 2014; Althaus et al., 2007). It is manufactured globally in large scale and the
12
==PAGE:20==
primary application, around 80% (Luttrell and Klaassen, 2016; Musser, 2005) is as monomer within
the production of nylon 6,6-polyamide ﬁbers and resins (Wu, 2016; Musser, 2005), in which ADA
and 1,6-hexanediamide are the main constituents undergoing a condensation reaction forming the
polymer (AGS Technology, 2020; Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014). Due to stringent requirements
of the 6,6-polyamide production, ADA is manufactured as one of the purest large volume chemicals
(Musser, 2005). Other areas of application are plasticisers, lubricants, and polyurethane resins,
pharmaceuticals (Luttrell and Klaassen, 2016; Musser, 2005) as well as neutraliser or acidulant in
the food industry under the E-number E355 (Musser, 2005; Luttrell and Klaassen, 2016).
3.4.3
Conventional production of adipic acid
There exist several production routes for ADA. However, all are based on petroleum derived feed-
stock such as cyclohexane, benzene, phenol, and butadiene (Bart and Cavallaro, 2015a). Cyclo-
hexane based production accounts for ~93% of the global production of ADA (Bart and Cavallaro,
2015a). Different production methods are listed below.
1. Two-step (Air and HNO3) oxidation of cyclohexane developed by DuPont
2. Hydrogenation of phenol followed by oxidation of cyclohexanol
3. HNO3 oxidation of cyclohexanol from cyclohexene
4. Butadiene and carbon monoxide based production of ADA (not commercialised)
The main difference within the ﬁrst three processes above is the use of feedstock. 1 and 3 are based
on benzene, whereas 2 is based on phenol. All three processes produce KA-oil (ketone-alcohol
oil), which is further oxidised to ADA via nitric acid oxidation (Musser, 2005; Oppenheim and
Dickerson, 2014; Bart and Cavallaro, 2015a).
The by far most applied process to produce ADA is the two-step oxidation of cyclohexane, which
was developed by DuPont in the 1930’s - 1940’s (Bart and Cavallaro, 2015a; Musser, 2005; Oppen-
heim and Dickerson, 2014). Therefore, this process was chosen for the LCA of the present study.
The production process is described below. Other petrochemical production pathways are brieﬂy
described in Appendix A.4.
3.4.3.1
Two-step oxidation of cyclohexane
The two steps of this ADA production process are (1) liquid-phase oxidation of cyclohexane with
air, producing KA-oil followed by (2) nitric acid oxidation of KA-oil into ADA (Bart and Cavallaro,
2015a; Musser, 2005; Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014; Castellan et al., 1991).
Three main routes exist for the ﬁrst oxidation step of cyclohexane. The oldest route still in use is
the metal-catalysed oxidation. The other two routes are derived from this route either maximising
the selectivity towards cyclohexylhydroperoxide (the ﬁrst intermediate produced during the oxi-
13
==PAGE:21==
dation process) or adding boric acid to the cyclohexane (Bart and Cavallaro, 2015a; Oppenheim
and Dickerson, 2014). The conversion rate of this process lies between 3-8 mol%, which is very
low for a commercial process (Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014; Bart and Cavallaro, 2015a). As a
result of the low conversion the unreacted cyclohexane must be recovered via distillation, which is
an energy intensive process and much effort has been given to energy conservation techniques such
as process-process heat interchange, high energy packed distillation columns, and puriﬁcation of
the KA-oil using caustic soda to avoid a ﬁnal steam stripping process. (Oppenheim and Dickerson,
2014).
In the second step, nitric acid oxidation, the produced KA-oil is oxidised into ADA using nitric acid
obtaining yields up to 92-96%. Nitric acid is typically derived from ammonia (Bart and Cavallaro,
2015a) or natural gas (Bernhard et al., 2014). This reaction runs in a continuous loop of a nitric
acid mother liquor (NML), passing through the entire system (Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014;
Musser, 2005). A process ﬂow sheet is displayed in Figure 3.6

Figure 3.6: Process diagram of the two-step oxidation process using KA-oil as feedstock. The
diagram is adapted from Oppenheim and Dickerson (2014).
A reactor conisting of a big heat ecchanger kept at 60-90°C and 0.1-0.4 MPa is fed with KA oil,
NML, make-up acid containing 45-60% nitric acid (Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014; Musser,
2005) and copper and vanadium catalyst. The residence time in the reactor is less than 5 minutes
14
==PAGE:22==
(Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014). The reaction is completed and the amount of impurities reduced
in an additional reactor with elevated temperatures This process is very exothermic (e.g. 6280
kJ/kg) (Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014; Musser, 2005). To control the reaction and increase the
yield, a ratio of excess of recycled NML to KA-oil is maintained at 3:1 or up to 1000:1 (Musser,
2005). Excess dissolved nitrogen oxides are removed with air and the off-gas is combined with air
and absorbed with water, generating nitric acid, which can be recycled in the process (Oppenheim
and Dickerson, 2014; Musser, 2005).
The major by-products from the nitric acid oxidation process are succinic and glutaric acid. Today,
these acids are recovered during the process as dibasic acids or converted to dibasic esters by adding
methyl esters after the recovering of the catalysts. Another procedure is to produce a mixture of
acids and anhydrides through distillation. Separation of the the acids is ensured by crystallisation
or extraction using organic solvents (Musser, 2005). Another major by-product, which cannot be
recovered as a valuable product is nitrous oxide (Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014).
The production of ADA has a high global warming potential compared to a wide range of other
polymers (Flederbach et al., 2019). The commercial synthesis of ADA accounts for almost 80%
of the industrial nitrous oxide emissions (Aryapratama and Janssen, 2017), which corresponds to
approximately 10% of the global anthropogenic nitrous oxide (Kruyer and Peralta-Yahya, 2017).
Nitrous oxide is a very potent greenhouse gas 265 times worse than carbon dioxide (IPCC, 2014).
Several nitrous oxide abatement technologies exists and are implemented at ADA plants.
3.4.4
Biobased adipic acid
Due to environmental concerns regarding the conventional production of ADA, companies seek to
an alternative and more sustainable source of ADA. There is an interest in changing the feedstock of
the conventional ADA process since it is based on petrochemical benzene (Bernhard et al., 2014).
At the time of writing, no commercial biochemical ADA process exists. However, much research
is made within this ﬁeld to develop sustainable and economically feasible processes. Some of the
processes under investigation are:
1. Chemo-catalytic production of ADA via two chemical catalytic steps (Rennovia)
2. Direct fermentation of fats and alkanes (Verdezyne)
3. Direct fermentation of glucose
4. Semi-chemical production of ADA from lignin combining fermentation and hydrogenation
The Rennovia and Verdezyne process both entered the market with a pilot plant (Bernhard et al.,
2014), however, they were not economically feasible, and thereby forced to leave the market
(De Guzman, 2010). The 4th process is of interest since it aims to use lignin as feedstock, which
is an abundant yet unexploited source of biomass. It furthermore, opens up for the opportunity of
developing bio-reﬁneries using both the holocellulose and lignin in biomass. Moreover, research
15
==PAGE:23==
has come far in engineering a microorganism capable of converting lignin derived monomers into
cis,cis-muconic acid (MA), which can be hydrogenated into ADA (Vardon et al., 2015; van Duuren
et al., 2011b; Salvachúa et al., 2018; Vardon et al., 2016; van Duuren et al., 2020; Kohlstedt et al.,
2018). Based on the above the semi-chemical production of ADA from lignin was chosen for the
LCA of the present study. Alternative bio-based ADA production processes are brieﬂy outlined in
Appendix A.4.
3.4.4.1
Semi-chemical production of ADA from lignin combining fermentation and hydrogenation
A semi-chemical process entails both a biological and a chemical conversion step (Draths et al.,
1994; Bernhard et al., 2014; Deng et al., 2016; Gunukula and Anex, 2017). The advantage of a
semi-chemical conversion process includes operation at lower pH, and the option to use unpuriﬁed
sugar in the bio-conversion step (Bernhard et al., 2014; Gunukula and Anex, 2017). The bio-
conversion in the semi-chemical production of bio-ADA can consist of fermentation of glucose or
lignocellulosic derived aromatics (e.g. p-coumarate and benzoate) to the precursors glucaric acid or
MA, which after separation and puriﬁcation is converted to ADA by catalytic hydrogenation (Bart
and Cavallaro, 2015b). The ﬁrst semi-chemical route towards ADA was reported by Draths et al.
(1994) using glucose as feedstock.
The fermentation of glucose uses the reverse β-oxidation pathway (Gunukula and Anex, 2017),
whereas the fermentation of lignocellulosic aromatics uses the β-ketoadipate pathway (Harwood
and Parales, 1996). Using lignocellulosic biomass as feedstock is an interesting alternative, since it
has low economic value compared to glucose not competing with food production (Bernhard et al.,
2014). It is, moreover, the major source of aromatics in nature (Deng et al., 2016). Lignin is a
very abundant and unexploited resource and is the second most common bio-polymer accounting
for 15-40% of dry weight of plants. Currently, lignin is a by-product of the sugar production from
biomass and is producing heat and power through incineration (Vardon et al., 2015).
HO

An effective microorganism is required for the fermentation process. The β-ketoadipate pathway
is naturally present in Pseudomonas putida, Acinetobacter calcoaceticus, Agrobacterium tume-
faciens, and Rodococcus erythropolis (Harwood and Parales, 1996). A widely studied and used
16
==PAGE:24==
microorganism is P. putida (Vardon et al., 2015, 2016; van Duuren et al., 2020; Corona et al.,
2018b). It is amenable to genetic manipulation and tolerates a wide range of chemical as well
as physical stress. Moreover, it is capable of utilising several aromatics derived from lignocellu-
losic biomass (Vardon et al., 2015; Salvachúa et al., 2018). Depending on the aromatic compound
the β-ketoadipate pathway converges at catechol or protocatechuic acid as the central metabolite.
(Bernhard et al., 2014; Vardon et al., 2015). The metabolic pathway is displayed in Figure 3.7.
None of these processes have reached commercialisation but are still under development at labora-
tory scale; van Duuren et al. (2020) reported a fermentation process conducted in a 30 L reactor.
Metabolic engineering of microorganism such as P. putida is still ongoing. The biological process
of the present study is based on literature. It is a best guess of a potential process. This speciﬁc
process consists of a fermentation step along with the necessary downstream process producing
MA, which is further converted into ADA via catalytic hydrogenation.
The speciﬁc feedstock of the biochemical production process in the present study is corn stover,
from which lignin is extracted through the novel lignin ﬁrst technology also known as reductive
catalytic fractionation, which is an organosolv process (Anderson et al., 2016a; Xu and Ferdosian,
2017). An organosolv process results in high purity lignin, which is in contrary to other lignin
extraction methods focusing on the holocellulose fraction used for the production of glucose from
2G biomass (Patel et al., 2018).
The lignin ﬁrst method was adapted from Anderson et al. (2016a) and uses methanol as solvent
in a 1:1 ratio methanol to substrate. The catalyst used is a nickel catalyst and phosphoric acid
was added to optimise the conversion. The process operated at 250°C and hydrogen was added
at 30 bar. Other lignin extraction methods are developed with the purpose to remove lignin from
cellulose and hemicellulose, which is used to produce glucose from 2G biomass.
The lignin extract is the fed into a fermentor, containing a variation of M9 medium. Glucose
is added for cell maintenance, but could be replaced by acetic acid (Vardon et al., 2015). The
engineered microorganism P. putida converts the aromatic compound p-coumaric acid into MA
(Salvachúa et al., 2018). The fermentation takes place at 32°C, 1 bar pressure, and at pH 7. For
each mol of MA produced 2 mol sodium hydroxide is added to maintain constant pH (Corona et al.,
2018b). Air is sparged through the fermentor at a rate of 20 L/min (van Duuren et al., 2020).
After fermentation separation and puriﬁcation steps follow including centrifugation, microﬁltration
and ﬁltration using activated carbon at a load of 5 g/L (Vardon et al., 2016) followed by crystallisa-
tion using the physico-chemical properties of MA. 2 M sulphuric acid is added and the temperature
was reduced to 5°C (Vardon et al., 2016; Kruyer and Peralta-Yahya, 2017; Corona et al., 2018b;
van Duuren et al., 2020, 2011b; Kruyer and Peralta-Yahya, 2017). To obtain polymer grade MA
is dissolved in ethanol removing further impurities (Vardon et al., 2016). The puriﬁed MA is ﬁ-
nally catalytically hydrogenated into ADA using a Rh/C catalyst at 75°C and 33 bar (Corona et al.,
2018b; Vardon et al., 2015, 2016).
17
==PAGE:25==
3.5
Shifting from fossil-based towards bio-based products
General
In recent years the motivation to produce chemicals from biomass rather than fossil feedstock has
increased, due to general concern for the changing climate and the depletion of fossil resources
(Cespi et al., 2020; Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018; Dunn et al., 2016). Furhermore, consumer aware-
ness on the environmental performance of products is driving the development of so-called "green"
chemicals (Dunn et al., 2016). Studies have shown that bio-based chemicals may emit less green-
house gases throughout their life cycle than fossil-based chemicals, thus leading to lower climate
change impacts (Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018). Climate change impact is also often the category
(represented by the carbon footprint) used for the purpose of marketing "green" chemicals. For in-
stance, Novamont-owned Mater Biotech claims that their bio-BDO production entails only 50% of
the greenhouse gas emissions compared to its conventional counterpart (Novamont, 2020). How-
ever, a major pitfall is that when basing management decisions on single indicators, such as the
carbon footprint, the burdens removed from this indicator may be shifted onto another indicator,
also referred to as burden shifting (Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018). In the case of comparing bio-based
chemicals to fossil-based chemicals, the carbon footprint of the biochemical may in some cases be
lower, while environmental impacts related to the growing, harvesting and pretreatment of biomass
may be higher for the biochemical (Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018).
The nature of burden-shifting means that it is not an inherent characteristic of bio-based chemicals
that they are better for the environment than fossil-based chemicals. In table 3.1 an excerpt of LCA
studies concluding that there are environmental trade-offs between bio- and fossil-based products
are listed. These studies represent a small fraction of available literature, but are meant as examples
to underline the complex dynamics of shifting production towards biomass feedstocks.
In summary, three of the articles listed in Table 3.1 ﬁnd that there exist trade-offs between decreased
climate change impact and increases in other impact categories for the bio-based alternative (Urban
and Bakshi, 2009; Chen et al., 2016; Belboom and Léonard, 2016). Lammens et al. (2011) ﬁnds
that the ranking between fossil- or bio-based alternative depends on which chemical is tested. For
two chemicals tested (N-methylpyrrolidone and N-vinylpyrrolidone) the climate change impact is
lower for the bio-based alternative, while for two other chemicals (Acrylonitrile and Succinonitrile)
the bio-based alternatives had higher climate change impacts.
Finally, it is highlighted that the methodological choices (scope, modelling framework, includ-
ed/excluded processes and life cycle impact assessment (LCIA) method) vary greatly among avail-
able literature studies including the four articles used as examples here. Methodological choices
may have a large impact on the conclusions and ranking of alternatives in comparative LCAs. This
is backed up by a recent literature review on LCA comparisons of fossil- and bio-based polymers
by Walker and Rothman (2020). They conclude that the impacts vary in many cases with a factor
2-4 among studies evaluating the same polymer type. Methodological choices when performing
comparative LCAs of fossil- and bio-based chemicals will be further explored in Section 4.2.

3.6
Life cycle assessments of 1,4-butanediol and adipic acid
General
As a part of the present study literature was scrutinised for other LCA’s assessing BDO and ADA
with the aim to ﬁnd inspiration and to discuss the results of the present study. Only few LCA’s have
been performed on the biochemical production of BDO and ADA revealing the relevance of further
LCA studies. The results of a LCA are highly depending on choices and assumptions made by the
LCA practitioner.
The literature revealed LCA studies of BDO and ADA. For BDO the authors managed to ﬁnd four
available LCA studies the ﬁrst one being from 1996 and the newest from 2018. For ADA four
LCA studies are found and one assessment combining economic, ecological and social impacts of
the ADA production. The LCA studies found in literature are of different nature, they cover both
attributional and consequential LCA as well as cradle-to-gate and cradle-to-grave studies. The
focus of this section is to investigate the different bio-based BDO and ADA production processes
as well as identifying strength and weaknesses of the LCA studies affecting the reproducibility.
3.6.1
1,4-butanediol
BDO
The literature search on LCA’s of BDO resulted in the ﬁnding of four studies assessing the environ-
mental impact of the bio-BDO production as well as the production of bio-based plastics, in which
BDO is encompassed. Differences and similarities of the LCA studies will be discussed in the fol-
lowing. Table 3.2 reveals the composition of the bio-BDO production processes as well as climate
change results and information regarding data quality and uncertainty assessments. The three of
the four LCA’s perform direct fermentation to produce bio-BDO whereas Adom et al. (2014) is a
semi-chemical production route.
Vigon et al. (1996) is the ﬁrst LCA study on the production of bio-BDO. This study assesses LCA
as a decision-making tool and only assesses energy resource consumption and qualitatively relates
them to climate change effects. It is the only of the four studies including a data quality assessment,
however, it is referred to in a separate study and not directly included in the article. It is very scarce
on information regarding the process description and only states the feedstock for the biochemical
production consists of corn derived glucose. As a result of the scarce information regarding the
processes of assessment no LCI tables are included leading to low reproducibility of the study.
Furthermore, no uncertainty assessment is conducted reducing the reliability of the study.

The most conservative results are obtained by Adom et al. (2014), which is possibly due to the
cradle-to-grave scope of the study, since it includes CO2-eq emissions related to the degradation of
the products, but do not chose a speciﬁc product assessing the use and end-of-life stages of those.
It evaluates multiple bio-products using either corn stover or algae as feedstock. This process
produces succinic acid via fermentation followed by separation and subsequently hydrogenation to
BDO. The quality of the LCA appears to be high since the study includes detailed LCI tables and
an uncertainty assessment (qualitatively). Relating uncertainty to the found results increases the
quality of the study. The reproducibility of this study is hence considered high.
The LCA’s of Forte et al. (2016) is an attributional LCA study assessing a bio-BDO process based
on wheat straw and discloses the most optimistic results regarding climate change impacts. It is the
only study performing a quantitative uncertainty assessment revealing 95% conﬁdence intervals
of each impact category, however, no LCI tables are included in the study. The reproducibility,
hence is reduced and is considered to be low-medium since the report entails relatively detailed
information on the assessed process. This study is the only of the four assessed LCA’s, which
performs a full LCA looking at several impact categories using ReCiPe 2016 as LCIA method. The
remaining LCA’s only include climate change and fossil energy consumption. Which is often the
case for LCA’s assessing emerging biochemical processes, since these are the categories in focus
described in Section 4.2. However, excluding other impact categories might lead to unintended
burden shifting as presented in Section 3.5.
The most recent LCA made on this topic is the cradle-to-grave study from Patel et al. (2018)
assessing several bioplastics, one of them including the production of BDO. The results of the
report reveals the impacts of the production, use and end-of-life stages of the plastics. Nevertheless,
the supplementary information reveals the cradle-to-gate impacts of the production of bio-BDO
and conventionally produced BDO. Interestingly, this report seems to be made on primary data
from Novamont, which operates a bio-BDO plant in Italy. Nevertheless, the information obtained
from Novamont is most likely conﬁdential, hence no detailed LCI tables are included, reducing
the reproducibility of the study. This is a general issue when performing LCA studies based on
conﬁdential data. However, the data quality of the study can be considered high, since the amount
of estimations and expert judgements made is reduced. This study further compares the impacts of
bio-BDO derived from gluces (1G biomass) and corn stover (2G biomass) revealing lower impact
on the 2G production process. Unfortunately, no uncertainty assessment is included in this study.
22
==PAGE:30==
3.6.2
Adipic acid
ADA
Scrutinising the literature revealed four LCA studies and an economic, ecological and social im-
pact assessment on the biochemical ADA production. The parallel assessment of Colodel et al.
(2009) does not describe the biochemical ADA production process and data collection very well,
however, the use of corn as feedstock is stated. The global warming and eutrophication potential of
the assessed process was reported. Colodel et al. (2009) concludes that there is no evident differ-
ence between the conventional and the biochemical ADA production. Regarding the eutrophication
potential Colodel et al. (2009) reports considerably higher potential of the biochemical production
process. Overall this assessment concludes that the conventional production route outperforms
the biochemical production route when assessing economic, ecological and social aspects of the
ADA production. The level of data collection and process description is very limited in this pa-
per regarding the ecological assessment and reproducibility is not possible based on the available
information.
The four LCA’s found in literature are assessed and difference within the studies are pointed out in
the following. Table 3.3 reveals information on the different LCA studies and the composition of
the production processes. All LCA’s discussed are cradle-to-gate. All of them, except van Duuren
et al. (2011a), start with the pretreatment of feedstock and ending at the production of polymer
grade ADA. It can be seen that Aryapratama and Janssen (2017) is the only study assessing a
direct fermentation process, hence the pretreatment method includes an enzymatic hydrolysis step
to release the fermentable sugars. The results of the study ﬁnd a signiﬁcant reduction in climate
change impacts in their bio-ADA production with a potential reduction of 71-84% depending on
the pretreatment method. This study is found to be the most well documented LCA of including
detailed LCI tables and description of the collected data and model assumptions made leading to
a high transparency and reproducibility of the study, which is important in an LCA since small
changes in the process can lead to signiﬁcant changes in the ﬁnal results.
The three remaining LCA’s are assessing a semi-chemical biochemical ADA production process.
van Duuren et al. (2020) seems to be a further developed version of van Duuren et al. (2011a). The
aim of both studies is to evaluate the performance of the biochemical ADA production process in
regards to climate change and cumulative energy demand during the process development using the
LCA as a tool for process optimisation. Both of the studies do not include LCI tables, however, van
Duuren et al. (2020) includes a detailed supplementary information describing experimental data
used and calculations used for the assessment of the climate change impact and cumulative energy
demand. van Duuren et al. (2011a) gives the impression of being a "back-of-the-envelope" LCA,
which might be implied by the limited nature of the assessment. A limitation of van Duuren et al.
(2011a) is the presentation of the results relative to the fossil production process, not stating the im-
pacts of it, which is deﬁnitely clearer in (van Duuren et al., 2020). The transparency of both studies
are considered moderate due to the lack of detailed LCI tables also affecting the reproducibility of
the studies.

Finally, a distinct similarity is disclosed between van Duuren et al. (2020) and Corona et al. (2018b).
The fermentation and downstream processes are identical and the main difference lies within the
feedstock and feedstock pretreatment method, which seems to have a distinct impact on the climate
change results of the studies, where the scenario obtaining the lowest impact in van Duuren et al.
(2020) is twice as high as as the impact of Corona et al. (2018b). The difference might be due to
the difference in data, where Corona et al. (2018b) is based on process simulations and van Duuren
et al. (2020) is based on self made data. The transparency of Corona et al. (2018b) is considered
low, since the LCI tables included have low level of detail and the descriptions in the supplementary
information are insufﬁcient leading to confusion.
Common for the four LCA studies are the lack of quantitative data quality assessment. The data
collection is described and the quality is somewhat assessed qualitatively, however, a more detailed
assessment of the data would increase the reliability and transparency of the LCA results further-
more, none of the above studies have included an uncertainty assessment. Including uncertainties
in LCA results increases the reliability of the results and might decrease the numerical difference
between the biochemical and fossil ADA production leading to more conservative conclusion based
on the result, however, the results might also be more accurate. Performing data quality and uncer-
tainty assessments increases the quality of an LCA.

==PAGE:33==
4
Methodology of life cycle assessment
General
Two major standards for LCA exist: ISO 14040:2006 and ISO 14044:2006. In ISO14040:2006 the
principles and frameworks of LCA are described, while the detailed requirements and guidelines
are laid out in ISO14044:2006. Both standards are adopted by Dansk Standard and the European
Committee for Standardization (DS/EN ISO 14040, 2008; DS/EN ISO 14044, 2006). Furthermore,
extensive guidelines by the European Comission Joint Research Center (EC-JRC) and International
Reference Life Cycle Data System (ILCD) exist (EC-JRC, 2010). These three documents have been
used to prepare the present LCA study. In the following sections the major phases in performing
an LCA are outlined and the most important aspects to consider when assessing chemical products
and comparing bio-based to fossil-based chemicals will be described.
4.1
Phases of life cycle assessment
General
LCA is a tool designed to address environmental impacts throughout the life cycle of a product or a
system (DS/EN ISO 14044, 2006). The entire life cycle includes the raw material acquisition stage,
the production stage, the use stage and the end-of-life (EOL) stage, and studies including these four
stages are referred to as cradle-to-grave studies (DS/EN ISO 14044, 2006). Studies only taking into
account the ﬁrst two life cycle stages (raw material acquisition and production) are referred to as
cradle-to-gate studies (Bjørn et al., 2018d; Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018).
LCA requires the practitioner to go through four phases, namely (1) goal and scope deﬁnition, (2)
life cycle inventory (LCI) analysis, (3) life cycle impact assessment (LCIA), and (4) interpretation
(DS/EN ISO 14044, 2006) (See Figure 4.1). LCA is not performed by undertaking each LCA phase
individually and in sequence, but by revisiting all preceding steps regularly during the process,
meaning that LCA is an iterative tool (See Figure 4.1. The phases are brieﬂy introduced in the
following.
Goal and scope definition
Life cycle inventory (LCI) analysis
Life cycle impact assessment (LCIA)
Interpretation
Figure 4.1: Phases of performing an LCA study. The arrows indicate that LCA is an iterative tool,
meaning that each step is revisited multiple times throughout the process.
26
==PAGE:34==
In the goal and scope deﬁnition phase the frame of the LCA is deﬁned, i.e. the intended applications
of the study, the limitations of the study, the system boundaries, the modelling framework to be used
in the LCI and the method to be used in the LCIA (DS/EN ISO 14044, 2006). Important aspects
also include deﬁning a functional unit, which is the basis of the assessment. In comparative LCA
the functional unit must be equal for the compared objects.
In the LCI phase data is collected to shed light on the product life cycle (DS/EN ISO 14044, 2006).
The life cycle is divided into the four aforementioned life cycle stages, each of which is further
divided into a number of processes. These processes should each have inputs of materials, energy
and direct emissions deﬁned. Some data is collected speciﬁcally for the LCA study (foreground
system), while other data already exist in databases (background system). The results of an LCI
will be an aggregated list of all the elementary ﬂows (inputs and outputs) of all the processes in the
life cycle (DS/EN ISO 14044, 2006). This list forms the basis for the LCIA phase.
In the LCIA phase the LCI elementary ﬂows are ﬁrst classiﬁed, meaning they are assigned to
the relevant environmental impact categories, where they contribute. Subsequently, the classiﬁed
elementary ﬂows are characterised, meaning that deﬁned characterisation factors relate them to
impact indicators. The impact indicators are quantitative measures that represent the environmental
impact categories. Hence, the results of an LCIA will be an LCIA proﬁle presenting the quantitative
contribution of the product system to each environmental impact category (DS/EN ISO 14044,
2006). Depending on the selected LCIA method the number of impact categories may vary.
In the interpretation phase the LCIA results are evaluated in order to make conclusions and recom-
mendations for the LCA. In this process signiﬁcant issues in the LCA have to be identiﬁed, such
as major uncertainties or lacks in the inventory data, high contributions of single processes to the
LCIA results, unexpected results or anomalies. Furthermore, the results have to be considered in
regards to completeness, sensitivity, and consistency. These evaluation steps are applied to im-
prove the conﬁdence and reliability of the results of the LCA (DS/EN ISO 14044, 2006). Finally,
uncertainty and data quality analysis should be performed (DS/EN ISO 14044, 2006).
4.2
Important aspects when assessing fossil- and bio-based chemicals
General
According to Fantke and Ernstoff (2018) LCA is a helpful tool to identify environmental impacts
associated with chemical production, use and disposal, especially in the comparison of chemicals
with the same function, i.e. chemicals derived from alternative feedstocks or produced with alter-
native production methods (Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018). Furthermore, LCA can be used to identify
the hotspots within the life cycle of chemicals (Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018). In the following, the
major topics and challenges to be aware of when performing LCA of chemicals are identiﬁed.
27
==PAGE:35==
4.2.1
Functional unit and reference ﬂow
In LCAs comparing two or more chemicals it is important that the functionality of all the chemicals
is the same, which must be captured in the functional unit (Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018; Tufvesson
et al., 2013). If one chemical alternative has multiple functions it is important to include these in the
functional unit and apply system expansion for the other product to fulﬁll the equivalent functions
(Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018). Furthermore, the amount of chemical needed to fullﬁll the functional
unit, the reference ﬂow, must take into account if one chemical alternative is more or less efﬁcient
in providing the functional unit than another (Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018). This could for instance
be that one chemical alternative has higher or lower purity.
4.2.2
System boundaries
According to Fantke and Ernstoff (2018) a cradle-to-gate study may be appropriate when compar-
ing for example bio-based chemicals with fossil-based chemicals, where the products are function-
ally identical, but only differ in their feedstock and/or production methods. However, the system
must account for all crop production processes, raw material acquisition and reﬁning processes for
the two alternative chemicals. On the contrary, some chemicals can be different, but still com-
pared based on the function they provide. In these cases it is important to include all life cycle
stages (Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018). Tufvesson et al. (2013) highlights the need to include the grave
stage since EOL treatment may entail CO2 emissions that contribute to climate change impact
when originating from fossil feedstock, whereas they are considered biogenic when originating
from biomass feedstock (not contributing to the greenhouse gas effect). According to a review of
LCAs of biopolymers by Hottle et al. (2013) the inclusion of the EOL stage may have a signiﬁcant
impact on the environmental impacts of biopolymers, while it simultaneously introduces greater
uncertainty and variability. The reasons for carrying out a cradle-to-gate LCA study must be well
founded in the intended applications of the study (Bjørn et al., 2018d).
4.2.3
Framework and multifunctionality
Two different modelling frameworks exist in LCA, namely, attributional and consequential (See
Table 4.1). The attributional framework is a "book keeping" approach used to quantify environmen-
tal impacts, which can be assigned to the product system. The consequential framework focuses
on the potential effects of decisions based on the LCA results. This framework involves mod-
elling of broader economic systems, which are affected by the decision. The frameworks differ in
the recommended ways to handle multifunctional processes and how the background systems are
modelled (EC-JRC, 2010). In the attributional framework average data is used for all background
processes within the life cycle (Bjørn et al., 2018c), meaning the product system does not affect the
surrounding economy (Bjørn et al., 2018b). The consequential framework uses marginal data for
background processes. Marginal processes are those which are taken out of use or employed as a
response to a given decrease or increase in the demand of a product (Bjørn et al., 2018b).
28
==PAGE:36==
Table 4.1: Frameworks used in LCA and the respective procedures to handle multifunctionality and
modelling of background systems as recommended by EC-JRC (2010).
Framework

System expansion
Marginal processes
When assessing the production of chemicals from 2G biomass it is important to consider which
modelling framework to use, since this also indicates the appropriate procedure to handle multi-
functional processes. The 2G biomass is a by-product of a multifunctional process. Using corn
cultivation as an example, corn grain and corn stover are the co-products, while the demand for
grain is typically driving the supply. The cultivation of corn grain and corn stover cannot be sub-
divided, meaning that the environmental impacts cannot either. In an attributional framework the
impacts from corn grain and corn stover may be assigned to the grain and stover based on allocation
procedures based on mass or economic value of the co-products. In Tufvesson et al. (2013) some
issues related to allocation based on mass and economic value are addressed; that mass allocation
does not account for the quality of the co-products, which could vary signiﬁcantly, and that eco-
nomic allocation does not consider the ﬂuctuations in prices over time. Therefore Tufvesson et al.
(2013) suggest using system expansion when making LCA of bulk chemicals, even though this
requires more extensive data collection and potentially entails larger uncertainties in the results.
System expansion means that the studied product system is expanded to include the additional
functions related to the co-products, or is credited with avoided production of secondary functions,
the latter referred to as substitution (Bjørn et al., 2018a). System expansion is the recommended
procedure for handling multifunctionality in the consequential framework.
Lammens et al. (2011) provides an example of applying system expansion to model the biomass
used as feedstock for chemical production. The bio-based chemical production described in Lam-
mens et al. (2011) requires glutamic acid derived from sugar beet vinasse. Under the assumption
that glutamic acid will not be the driver of sugar beet production, Lammens et al. (2011) excluded
the impact related to sugar beet production, and instead included the impact related to producing
the substitutes for the glutamic acid removed from the sugar beet vinasse.
29
==PAGE:37==
4.2.4
LCI data availability and quality
The data availability can be a roadblock when performing LCA of chemicals and biochemicals
(Tufvesson et al., 2013). LCI database processes typically rely on average emission data for a single
industry or can be outdated, and hence, it is preferable to collect primary data for the LCA (Fantke
and Ernstoff, 2018). However, chemical producers will rarely disclose detailed information about
the amounts of materials, water, energy, direct emissions and chemicals used in their production.
Therefore, it is necessary to gather secondary data, and to transparently report which data sources
and assumptions have been applied.
4.2.5
Impact assessment limitations
Given the vast amount of chemicals currently on the market (tens of thousands) and the largest
available list of characterisation factors covering only around 3000 chemicals (USEtox), there is a
gap in the method (Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018). This should especially be considered when inter-
preting toxicity impact results of LCAs focusing on chemicals.
4.2.6
Comparing different levels of technological development
According to (Fantke and Ernstoff, 2018) when evaluating the prospects of moving towards bio-
based products, these may prove to have similar or even higher impacts at the current level of
technology, even though it was not the anticipated outcome. In these cases it is important to not
discredit the bio-based product as a goal of future sustainability, but rather to focus on the chal-
lenges that need to be addressed for the bio-based product to avoid burden shifting. This is under-
lined by Tufvesson et al. (2013) stating that it is important to do environmental evaluations on early
planning stages of emerging technologies. To avoid discrediting emerging technologies based on
their lower level of optimisation when comparing with conventional technologies, Tufvesson et al.
(2013) suggests evaluating multiple scenarios for the emerging technology, including potential op-
timised processes.
30
==PAGE:38==
5
Goal and scope
General
The goal and scope of the present study are presented according to the DS/EN ISO 14044 (2006)
requirements in Appendix A.5 and A.6, respectively, to ensure transparency. Relevant aspects will
be outlined in the following paragraphs.
The present study aims to explore the potential environmental impacts associated with the pro-
duction of bio-BDO and bio-ADA from 2G biomass. The life cycle stages and unit processes
which have the highest contributions to the environmental impacts will be identiﬁed in a hot spot
analysis. Furthermore, the impacts of the bio-based chemicals will be compared with their con-
ventional counterparts to illuminate prospective environmental trade-offs when transitioning from
fossil-based to bio-based chemicals. As the study uses two speciﬁc chemicals to exemplify the
transition, the conclusions and recommendations must be principally applied to these two chemi-
cals, and not to chemical production in general. The environmental impacts of the two chemicals,
BDO and ADA, will not be compared with each other, while any observed patterns among the two
chemicals may be highlighted. The intended application of the LCA is to guide the further work
with evaluating environmental impacts of bio-based chemicals by identifying major challenges.
The work may also provide early stage support for decision makers.

Figure 5.1: System boundary deﬁned for production of bio-based 1,4-butanediol and adipic acid.
The scope of the present study is cradle-to-gate, meaning that raw material and production stages
are included, while the use and EOL stages are excluded. The selection of the scope is based on
a desire to perform a process-oriented evaluation of the chemicals. Furthermore, the bio-based
and conventional chemical counterparts are considered functionally equivalent when leaving the
factory gate. Finally, the evaluated chemicals have wide spectres of utilisation and disposal options,
hence, excluding these stages will make the results more generic and focused on the optimisation
of production.

==PAGE:39==
The system boundaries for the bio-based chemicals are outlined in Figure 5.1. The production of
bio-based chemicals uses corn stover as the biomass feedstock (See Table 5.1). In Figure 5.1 it is
seen that the impacts related to corn cultivation are excluded, while the impacts related to producing
the substitutes for corn stover have been included (straw and soybean meal). This procedure will
be elaborated in Section 6.1. The corn stover ﬁrst undergoes mechanical pretreatment before it is
transported to the production site. At the production site it is chemically pretreated, bioconverted
and processed in order to obtain the ﬁnal chemical product.

The system boundaries for the conventional chemicals are outlined in Figure 5.2. The production
of the conventional chemicals uses fossil intermediates as feedstock (See Table 5.1).
The functional unit of the current study is the production of 1 kg polymer grade BDO/ADA in
the state of Iowa, US, in 2020. The geographical location of Iowa is selected based on its large
production of corn, and hence, large availability of corn stover (USDA, 2020). The data used for
the LCI is to the extent possible collected for the state of Iowa, while in most cases it is only
possible to obtain data for all of the United States (US). Where data is not available for the US,
global data sets are used.
