The LCI of the present study has been modelled using the software EASETECH v3.3.7. Ideally,
the data used for the inventory of production of bio-based BDO and ADA would be primary data
from industrial producers of these two chemicals. However, for bio-BDO, which is currently pro-
duced commercially, the production data from commercial producers is proprietary, and is instead
replaced by literature process simulations to fulﬁl the LCI analysis. For bio-ADA, which is not a
32
==PAGE:40==
commercial product to this date, industrial scale data is not available, but laboratory scale literature
data is instead adapted to fulﬁl the LCI analysis. For both bio-based BDO and ADA extensive
literature research has been conducted to obtain the best available data per unit process in the pro-
duction chain. Background processes such as production of used energy and materials are obtained
from ecoinvent v3.6 and adapted to the present study.
As the biochemical processes are both novel it is unknown, whether the processes are currently or
will be like the suggested processes in the present study. This is especially prevalent for bio-ADA.
For biochemical production of BDO the data used is considered representative of the current BDO
production, however, since the commercialisation is still recent, it is likely to be optimised over the
coming decades. For biochemical production of ADA the data is considered representative for the
current level of research, and is still likely to be signiﬁcantly optimised as a part of the up-scaling
in the maturing of the technology.
The inventories for the conventional production of BDO and ADA are obtained from ecoinvent
v3.6. This leads to a lower detail in unit processes in the production chain, however, the com-
pleteness of the data sets are considered comparable. This means that hotspot analysis is mainly
performed for the bio-based chemicals, whereas the net environmental impacts are compared be-
tween the conventional and bio-based chemicals. The most important database processes used are
compared to literature for validation. The database processes for conventional production are as-
sumed to be representative for the temporal and technological scope of this study, as they are well
established and optimised, and therefore, not likely to develop rapidly.
The applied LCIA method is ReCiPe 2016 from a hierarchical perspective with long-term effects
included. The method is summarised in Appendix A.7. In the present study the following im-
pact categories have been selected: climate change (CC), freshwater eutrophication (FEU), ma-
rine eutrophication (MEU), fossil resource scarcity (FRS), terrestrial ecotoxicity (TET), freshwa-
ter ecotoxicity (FET), marine ecotoxicity (MET), human toxicity: cancer (HTC), human toxicity:
non-cancer (HTNC), land use (LU), and water use (WU). The categories are selected due to their
relevance to the deﬁned scope and importance as determined based on early stage results of the
LCA. CC and FRS are included since reductions in these categories are commonly the motivation
for transitioning to bio-based chemicals. LU is included because it is one of the apparent strains on
the environment related to the use of biomass. WU is included due to its high importance in chem-
ical processing. LU and WU impacts are highly dependent on the spatial scope of the LCA, since
the effects vary locally. This topic will be elaborated in Section 7.1. The remaining categories are
included due to substantial person equivalent impacts obtained in early stage results. The toxicity
categories are, however, subject to high uncertainties, which will be discussed further in Section
7.1. The normalisation to person equivalents is performed using normalisation factors afﬁliated to
the ReCiPe 2016 method, which normalises the results to the global population in 2010 (RIVM,
2020).
33
==PAGE:41==
6
Life cycle inventory
General
The scope of the present study is cradle-to-gate meaning that raw material acquisition and produc-
tion are the two overarching life cycles stages. For the production of bio-based BDO and ADA the
stages are divided into ﬁve sub-stages, namely, (1) feedstock provision, (2) mechanical pretreat-
ment, (3) chemical pretreatment, (4) fermentation, and (5) downstream processing. For bio-ADA
the downstream processing includes an additional catalytic hydrogenation step to obtain ADA from
the cis,cis-muconic acid (MA) obtained from fermentation. Step (1) corresponds to the raw mate-
rial acquisition stage, while step (2), (3), (4), and (5) are sub-stages to the production stage. For the
production of conventional BDO and ADA the stages are not subdivided.
The feedstock provision and mechanical pretreatment stages (1 and 2) are based on the same data
and is modelled identically in bio-BDO and bio-ADA production, while the only difference among
the two models is the amount of feedstock required for production of 1 kg chemical (≈7 kg corn
stover for 1 kg BDO and ≈32 kg corn stover for 1 kg ADA). Bio-BDO production relies on mixed
sugars derived from the corn stover, while bio-ADA production relies on lignin derivatives. The
feedstock provision and mechanical pretreatment inventories applicable to both bio-BDO and bio-
ADA production are described in Section 6.1 and 6.2, respectively. The electricity, heat, steam,
and process water used in the production plants are also modelled equally for BDO and ADA. The
inventories for these utilities are described in Section 6.3. In Sections 6.4 and 6.5 the inventories
applicable to the remaining sub-stages (3, 4, and 5) are shown for BDO and ADA, respectively.
6.1
Feedstock provision
General
The feedstock used for bio-based BDO and ADA is the lignocellulosic 2G biomass, corn stover,
obtained as a by-product in the production of corn. With regards to the bio-based ADA production
corn stover an argumentation for the selection of corn stover is the lignin derived includes more p-
coumaric acid than lignin derived from wood, which consists of syringol as a pendant to p-coumaric
acid (Anderson et al., 2016a). The supply of corn stover is assumed to come from corn crops in
Iowa, US. Corn is according to the United States Department of Agriculture a very prevalent crop
in this US state (USDA, 2020). Since corn stover is a less valuable product than the corn itself, and
would not be produced, if the corn production was not present, it is argued that the corn cultivation
and related activities are considered "burden-free" and are not assigned to the studied system. This
means that the present study does not account for any part of the sowing, growing, and harvest
of corn and corn stover. Instead the present study accounts for the feedstock provision by using
system expansion taking into account what the corn stover is currently used for, and then including
alternative provision of these services in the system. In a study by Schmer et al. (2017) it was
examined, what the corn residues in the US are used for. The study showed that in the "central
region" including Iowa, Colorado, Kansas, Minnesota, Nebraska, South Dakota, and North Dakota,
the corn residue land utilisation as a percentage of total corn area was divided as in Figure 6.1 (a).
34
==PAGE:42==
Figure 6.1: (a) Utilisation of corn stover divided into different categories as deﬁned in Schmer et al.
(2017). (b) Utilisation of corn stover as adapted in the present study.
For the sake of simpliﬁcation, the categories "other", "not proﬁtable" and "left as organic matter",
are divided equally onto the categories "grazed", "harvested" and "no market or use" (Figure 6.1
(b)). For the corn stover needed to fulﬁl the functional unit in present study, 39% is considered to
be burden-free (i.e. not modelled). For the 26% harvested corn stover the system is expanded to
include alternative production of bedding in the form of the ecoinvent v3.6 process "straw produc-
tion, stand-alone production, RoW" on a 1:1 dry matter basis. For the 35% grazed corn stover the
system is expanded to include alternative production of fodder in the form of the ecoinvent v3.6
process "market for soybean meal, RoW". The amount of soybean meal needed to provide the same
fodder value as the corn stover is calculated based on the modiﬁed Scandinavian feed unit (SFU)
of corn stover and soybean meal (See Appendix A.8 for calculation procedure). Table A.20.1 and
A.22.1 in Appendix A.20 and A.22 discloses the LCI table of this process including all literature
references used for compiling the model.
Data quality and uncertainty: The main sources of uncertainty in the feedstock provision modelling
is not related to the data quality (DQI of 2.2 is obtained see Table 6.2 and 6.7), but to the procedure
of simplifying the categories stated in the survey from Schmer et al. (2017). Furthermore, it is
assumed that the harvested category mainly covers the use as bedding in stables, hence the mod-
elling includes the production of straw. However, the application of the harvested category could
potentially be another. The survey described in Schmer et al. (2017) was conducted in 2010, hence
corn stover might today also have an application as feedstock for bio-fuel production. Furthermore,
the choice of straw and soybean meal could be replaced with other production obtaining the same
function.
6.2
Mechanical pretreatment
The mechanical pretreatment is assumed to take place close to the corn cultivation area and the
transportation is assumed to be included in the feedstock provision. The mechanical pretreatment is
35
==PAGE:43==
adapted from INL (2013) consisting of electricity use of the three processes milling, densiﬁcation,
and drying. During the drying process the water content of the corn stover is reduced from 30%
in the corn stover entering the facility to 9% after drying. Table A.20.2 and A.22.2 in Appendix
A.20 and A.22 discloses the LCI table of this process including all literature references used for
compiling the model.
After the mechanical pretreatment the processed corn stover is transported to the chemical produc-
tion facility. Due to the lack of data a rough assumption of 100 km transportation distance is made.
The sensitivity and contribution of this process will be assessed in a perturbation and contribution
analysis. Table A.20.3 in Appendix A.20 and A.22.3 in Appendix A.22 disclose the inventories for
BDO and ADA, respectively, including all literature references used for compiling the model.
For bio-BDO a "feed handling" step is included at the reception at the chemical production facility
in accordance with Davis et al. (2015). This includes electricity required to handle the incoming
biomass, and the inventory can be seen in Table A.20.4 in Appendix A.20.
Data quality and uncertainty: The data quality is deemed to be good for this process obtaining a
DQI of 2.2 (see Table 6.2 and 6.7. The main source of uncertainty is the use of average data of
different technologies within milling, densiﬁcation and drying, since the exact technology used in
the particular cases of the present study is unknown. Furthermore, no data on the transportation
distance nor transportation type is available and is assumed to be 100 km and transportation by
road by 32 tonnes lorries.
6.3
Electricity, heat, process water and steam
General
The marginal energy source in the US is determined to be natural gas. Since both the BDO and
ADA cases are assumed to be located in the US the marginal utility processes are identical and will
be described in the following.
6.3.1
Marginal electricity
The marginal electricity process is based on ecoinvent v3.6 processes of two natural gas technolo-
gies used for electricity production in the US. The two processes are:
• electricity production, natural gas, conventional power plant, US-MRO
• electricity production, natural gas, combined cycle power plant, US-MRO
For each technology the total annual electricity production from natural gas is provided in the LCI
table in ecoinvent v3.6, which is used to determine the percent wise contribution of each technol-
ogy. The conventional power plant is the dominant technology accounting for 93% of the electricity
production leaving 7% to the combined cycle power plant technology. The above processes result
in the production of high voltage electricity and the electricity used in the BDO and ADA produc-
tion is assumed to be medium voltage electricity, hence transformation loss is included, which is
36
==PAGE:44==
adapted from the ecoinvent v3.6 process "electricity voltage transformation from high to medium
voltage, US-MRO" stating that 1 kWh medium voltage electricity requires 1.0056 kWh high voltage
electricity.
6.3.2
Marginal heat
The marginal heat process used in the BDO and ADA production in the present study is assembled
similarly to the marginal electricity production. Two processes producing heat from natural gas are
found in ecoinvent v3.6 and are used in the present study. The two processes are:
• heat and power co-generation, natural gas, conventional power plant, 100MW electrical, US-
MRO
• heat and power co-generation, natural gas, combined cycle power plant, 400MW electrical,
US-MRO
Also here an annual production volume is given in the respective ecoinvent v3.6 processes, which
are used to calculate the percent wise contribution of each technology, being 63% for the conven-
tional power plant technology and 37% for the combined cycle power plant technology.
6.3.3
Process water
The process water of the BDO and ADA production is based on the ecoinvent v3.6 process:’tap
water production, underground water with chemical treatment, RoW’. Since it is assumed that the
main water source in the US is ground water. The ecoinvent v3.6 process is adapted by replacing
the electricity processes with the marginal electricity process described above.
6.3.4
Marginal steam
For both BDO and ADA production steam is used in several processes. The marginal steam process
of the present study is adapted from the ecoinvent v3.6 process ’steam production, as energy carrier,
in chemical industry, RoW,’. The included electricity, heat and water used in the original ecoinvent
v3.6 process are replaced by the above described processes to obtain steam production representing
the US marginal energy.
In the case of BDO the marginal steam as described above is converted from MJ into kg considering
2.75 MJ/kg steam, since the available inventory data is given in kg steam. Furthermore, the steam
is divided into low (LP), medium (MP), and high pressure (HP) (1, 15 and 26.34 bar, respectively).
The speciﬁc enthalphy of steam as obtained from Engineering ToolBox (2003) has been used to
scale the ecoinvent v3.6 process from 2.75 MJ/kg to the respective speciﬁc enthalpies of LP, MP
and HP (2.68, 2.79 and 2.80 MJ/kg, respectively).
37
==PAGE:45==

6.4.1.1
Raw material stage
The feedstock for the Reppe process is acetylene and formaldehyde. Acetylene is obtained from
natural gas, while formaldehyde is produced from methanol obtained from natural gas.
Acetylene production: The acetylene production process is obtained from ecoinvent v3.6 "market
for acetylene production, RoW" and has not been changed for this study. The original ecoinvent
dataset represents Europe, but has been adjusted within ecoinvent to the "RoW" geography. It
is assumed that acetylene is acquired from the market and not produced at the same location as
the BDO. The amount of acetylene required for producing 1 kg BDO is 0.32 in accordance with
the ecoinvent v3.6 process "butane-1,4-diol production, RoW". This corresponds to 90.3% of the
38
==PAGE:46==
theoretical yield of BDO from acetylene. The process includes the consumption of natural gas and
oxygen for producing acetylene as well as the heat, electricity, cooling water and organic chemical
plant required. The direct emissions of ethyne to air are included. See Table A.21.1 in Appendix
A.21.
Formaldehyde production: The formaldehyde production process is obtained from ecoinvent v3.6
"market for formaldehyde, RoW" and has not been changed for this study. The original ecoinvent
dataset represents Europe, but has been adjusted within ecoinvent to the "RoW" geography. The
process is an average of two production methods, namely, the "silver process" and "formox pro-
cess". The amount of formaldehyde required for producing 1 kg BDO is 0.73 in accordance with
the ecoinvent v3.6 process "butane-1,4-diol production, RoW". The process includes the consump-
tion of methanol (from natural gas) and liquid nitrogen for producing formaldehyde as well as the
electricity and organic chemical plant required. An array of direct emissions to air are included.
Steam is produced as a by-product in the process, which substitutes the market for steam. Water
use and emissions to water are not included. See LCI Table A.21.1 in Appendix A.21.
Uncertainty and data quality: In the ecoinvent v3.6 "market for acetylene production, RoW" it is
mentioned that the acetylene production is based on theoretical data from process analysis, and that
it should not be used to evaluate systems where acetylene plays a major role. This can be said to
be the case for BDO production. The process is still applied in the present study due to lack of
data from real production processes, which introduces uncertainty to the results. Table 6.1 displays
a pedigree matrix assessing the data quality of the processes included in the conventional BDO
production. Here it is seen that acetylene and formaldehyde production both data quality indices
(DQI) of 3.0 and 2.8, respectively. This indicates relatively low data quality, which should be taken
into account when drawing conclusions from the LCA. The poor quality is largely driven by the
low completeness of the data, since the representativeness is unknown for acetylene production,
and based on data from few sites for formaldehyde.

6.4.1.2
Production stage
The production stage has been adapted from the ecoinvent v3.6 process "butane-1,4-diol produc-
tion, RoW". It consists of two steps, which are the reaction of acetylene and formaldehyde in the
Reppe process to 2-butyne-1,4-diol, and further hydrogenation to BDO. The data is based on Chau-
vel and Lefebvre (1989) and Cooper and Vigon (2001). These sources are representative for France
and the U.S. Midwest, respectively. The direct inputs of energy to the BDO production have been
changed from "RoW" geography to the marginal energy for the US deﬁned for the present study.
The input of heat, electricity, NaOH, cooling water and process water and some direct emissions
to air and water are deﬁned for the entire production process, while some inputs and outputs have
been assigned to the speciﬁc steps.
Reppe process: According to Haas et al. (2005); Copenhaver and Bigelow (1949); Izawa and
Yokoyama (2016) the Reppe process is catalysed by a copper-bismuth-acetylide catalyst. In the
ecoinvent v3.6 process "butane-1,4-diol production, RoW" only the copper in the catalyst is in-
cluded in accordance with Cooper and Vigon (2001). Emissions of copper to air and water are also
included. See LCI Table A.21.1 in Appendix A.21.
Hydrogenation: According to Copenhaver and Bigelow (1949); Haas et al. (2005); Gräfje et al.
(2019) the hydrogenation of 2-butyne-1,4-diol to BDO is in most cases catalysed by a nickel cat-
alyst. The nickel may be supported on oxides of zirconium or aluminium. In the ecoinvent v3.6
process "butane-1,4-diol production, RoW" only the nickel in the catalyst is included in accor-
dance with Cooper and Vigon (2001). Emissions of nickel to air and water are also included. The
hydrogen and nitrogen used in this step are also included. See LCI Table A.21.2 in Appendix A.21.
Uncertainty and data quality: The major uncertainty in the production process originates from the
completeness of the BDO production process, which is based on data from only two sites, which
cannot be considered representative for the general production of BDO through Reppe process
and hydrogenation. The reliability and geographical and technological correlation are relatively
good for this process, since it is based on data from actual production sites in France and the U.S.
Midwest (which Iowa is a part of) using the same technology as assessed in the present study. The
DQI for the process is 2.2 which indicates relatively good data quality (See Table 6.1).
6.4.2
Bio-based 1,4-butanediol
The inventory of bio-BDO production is based on a mass balance of water, materials and energy
for a number of deﬁned processes in the production line. The processes are depicted in Figure
6.3, and the mass balance is found in Appendix A.1 (attached). The level of detail in production
processes is higher for bio-based than for conventional BDO. Nonetheless, the completeness of the
conventional and bio-based BDO production is considered relatively equal allowing comparison of
the two processes. In Table 6.2 the unit processes deﬁned for bio-BDO production are outlined
along with their main data source, a short description, and an average DQI.

==PAGE:50==
As previously mentioned, the production of bio-based BDO is divided into ﬁve sub-stages, namely,
(1) feedstock provision, (2) mechanical pretreatment, (3) chemical pretreatment, (4) fermentation,
and (5) downstream processing (Table 6.2).
The following sections will describe the inventories of unit processes in the main production line
of bio-BDO, while processes outside of the main production line denoted "Other processes" are
described in Appendix A.9. Major assumptions and data sources are described in the text, and
a full list of assumptions is compiled and can be found in Appendix A.13. The inventories for
the feedstock provision and mechanical pretreatment have already been described in Section 6.1
and 6.2, since they are modelled equally for bio-BDO and bio-ADA production. The chemical
pretreatment, fermentation and downstream processing stages are each described in the following
section.
6.4.2.1
Chemical pretreatment
The mass ﬂows per unit process and amounts of water, materials and energy consumed throughout
the pretreatment of corn stover are obtained from the National Renewable Energy Laboratory study
by Davis et al. (2015). The LCI data for the production of water, materials, transport and energy is
obtained from ecoinvent v3.6 (See Appendix A.20 for speciﬁc ecoinvent processes).
The study by Davis et al. (2015) covers an integrated process for pretreatment of biomass and
catalytic conversion and upgrading to hydrocarbons. This means that some processes included in
Davis et al. (2015) have been excluded from the present study. This includes the enzyme produc-
tion plant, the combustion plant, the boiler, the generator and the catalytic conversion and upgrad-
ing to hydrocarbons. The processes included in the present study are deacetylation, dilute acid
pretreatment, enzymatic hydrolysis and a number of puriﬁcation steps. Furthermore, an internal
wastewater treatment plant has been included, and the burden of this plant has been divided be-
tween the pretreatment processes (included in present study) and the excluded combustion plant,
boiler, generator and catalytic conversion and upgrading plant.
Both Davis et al. (2015) and INL (2013) consider a mixed biomass obtained from single- and
multi-pass corn stover, switchgrass and additionally for INL (2013) a small amount of municipal
solid waste. For the current study it is assumed that the biomass only consists of generic corn
stover, however, the composition of the biomass from Davis et al. (2015) and INL (2013) is main-
tained. The biomass composition throughout the pretreatment has been presented on the basis of
water, lignin, cellulose, xylan, glucose and xylose content. An overview of the composition after
important pretreatment steps can be seen in Table 6.3.
43
==PAGE:51==
Table 6.3: Composition of biomass after important steps throughout the pretreatment process. Val-
ues adapted from Davis et al. (2015) and scaled to ﬁt biomass needed to produce 1 kg of bio-BDO.

Deacetylation: The biomass is fed to a deacetylation tank where NaOH is added for lignin solu-
bilisation. The assumed NaOH loading is 17 mg/g dry biomass. In a pilot scale deacetylation of
corn stover carried out by Chen et al. (2014) the NaOH loading was 50 mg/g dry biomass (calcu-
lated from information in the article), indicating that the loading from Davis et al. (2015) used in
the present study may be optimistic. The loading from Davis et al. (2015) is maintained to ensure
consistency with the mass balance. After deacetylation black liquor is removed and sent to wastew-
ater treatment. See Table A.20.5, Appendix A.20 for the full inventory of deacetylation, and Table
A.20.20, Appendix A.20 for the upstream wastewater treatment.
Dilute acid pretreatment: Subsequently, the biomass undergoes dilute acid pretreatment to further
depolymerise oligomers with sulphuric acid using high pressure steam. The sulphuric acid loading
is 9 mg/g dry biomass. A previous report by the NREL assumes a sulphuric acid loading of 18 mg/g
dry biomass (Humbird et al., 2011), indicating that the loading considered in Davis et al. (2015)
has been optimised, and may be considered optimistic. See Table A.20.6, Appendix A.20 for the
full inventory.
Ammonia conditioning: The obtained slurry is neutralised in an ammonia gas conditioning process.
The ammonia loading is 1.6 g/L slurry. The ammonia loading in Davis et al. (2015) is considered
a design target for 2017, since a loading of 4.8 g/L was used in Humbird et al. (2011), while the
state of technology was 1.6 g/L in 2012 according to Tao et al. (2014), indicating that this is a
fair assumption for 2020. Additional black liquor is removed at this step and sent to wastewater
treatment. See Table A.20.7, Appendix A.20 for the full inventory of ammonia conditioning, and
Table A.20.20, Appendix A.20 for the upstream wastewater treatment.
Enzymatic hydrolysis and lignin ﬁltration: The chemically pretreated stream is fed into an enzy-
matic hydrolysis process where mainly cellulase is added for depolymerisation to monosaccha-
rides. A subsequent lignin ﬁlter removes the lignin from the hydrolysate. The cellulase loading
44
==PAGE:52==
in Davis et al. (2015) was assumed 10 mg protein/g cellulose in the biomass, with a conversion of
90% of cellulose to glucose. However, only double this loading has been demonstrated by NREL in
2013, and therefore 20 mg protein/g cellulose has been used in the present study, while maintaining
the conversion assumption. The removed lignin stream consists of water (62%), lignin, and some
carbohydrates, and is assumed combusted. See Table A.20.8, Appendix A.20 for the full inven-
tory of enzymatic hydrolysis and lignin ﬁltration, and Table A.20.22, Appendix A.20 for the lignin
combustion.
Concentration, ﬁltration and ion exchange: Next the hydrolysate is concentrated in an evaporator,
and passed though a polishing microﬁlter and an ion exchange bed to remove impurities. The
amounts of NaOH and HCl used for regenerating the bed resins are 12.8 and 22.4 g/kg soluble
solids in the slurry, respectively, and are based on estimates by (Davis et al., 2015) and are therefore
highly uncertain. In the microﬁlter some sugars are unintentionally removed, and for this reason
part of the retentate is returned to the lignin ﬁlter, and the remainder is removed. Some water from
the puriﬁcation step can be recycled, and has been modelled as substituting process water (negative
water use). See Table A.20.9, Appendix A.20 for the full inventory.
Data quality and uncertainty: The average DQIs for each of the above described unit processes can
be seen in Table 6.2 (See Table A.10.1 in Appendix A.10 for full pedigree matrix). For the chemical
pretreatment stage the DQIs are between 2.7 and 2.8, indicating medium data quality. The temporal
and geographical correlation of the used data with the scope of the present study is relatively good,
since the corn stover pretreatment plant is simulated for the US and fairly recently (2015). The
fact that the data relies on simulated processes makes the reliability and completeness poor. Large
uncertainy prevails for the amount of materials and energy used per unit process, and this should
be taken into account when evaluating the results.
6.4.2.2
Fermentation
The production of bio-BDO through direct fermentation of sugars is based on Satam et al. (2019),
Burgard et al. (2016) and Forte et al. (2016). The overall feasibility of direct fermentation is highly
dependent on the yield (g/g) of the targeted chemical from the feedstock, the concentration of
chemical product in the obtained fermentation broth (titer, g/L) and the rate of production of chem-
ical (productivity g/L/hour) (Barton et al., 2014). For BDO obtained through direct fermentation in
literature the yield, titer and productivity values can be seen in Table 6.4.
According to the balanced stoichiometric reaction 1 mol of glucose is required for 1 mol of BDO:
C6H12O6 +0.5 O2 −→C4H10O2 +2 CO2 +H2O
(4)
This corresponds to a theoretical yield of 0.5 g BDO/g glucose, when disregarding carbon used for
cellular growth or by-products (Barton et al., 2014).
45
==PAGE:53==

present study.

b Not experimental results, but parameters used in technoeconomic analysis simulation of unit processes
Fermentation: Although not agreed upon in literature, the present study considers 0.5 g BDO/g
glucose the maximum theoretical yield and adapts a realistic yield of 0.40 g BDO/g glucose (80%
of the theoretical yield). In the present study the substrate for fermentation is assumed to be a mix
of glucose and xylose obtained from pretreatment of corn stover. The yield of BDO from xylose
was also considered ≈80% of the theoretical yield, corresponding to 0.468 g BDO/g xylose. This
was assumed based on Novy et al. (2015) obtaining this yield in ethanol fermentation from mixed
sugars.
The consumption of steam is obtained from Satam et al. (2019) which assumes dextrose syrup
(glucose) as the substrate for fermentation and a higher yield than the present study, the steam
consumption is adapted accordingly.
The overall efﬁciency of the downstream processing of fermentation broth is considered 92.5%,
hence 1.08 kg of BDO should be produced in the bioreactor to obtain 1 kg of BDO after downstream
processing.
The titers obtained in fermentation vary among studies, and the highest reported titer - supposedly
obtained at the Mater Biotech Novamont plant in northern Italy - is 140 g BDO/L fermentation broth
(Genomatica, 2017), which is used in the present study. The amount of water in the fermentation
reactor was calculated based on the titer of 140 g/L and the production of 1.08 kg BDO, resulting in
a total broth volume of 7.7 L. The volume of sugar water added to the reactor was subtracted from
this, which was then considered the amount of process water added to the reactor. The amount of
process water added to the reactor in Satam et al. (2019) was higher than in the present study, due
to the different substrates considered in the two studies.
The amount of nutrients needed for the fermentation media are based on a patent application by
Genomatica (2015b) mentioning M9 minimal media, and the composition of this type of media was
obtained from the chemical vendor Sigma Aldrich’s webpage (Sigma Aldrich, 2020). The webpage
mentions that vitamins and aminoacids are typically added to the media, but these constituents have
46
==PAGE:54==
been excluded due to lack of data. Furthermore, the seed train has been disregarded in the present
study. See Table A.20.11, Appendix A.20 for the full inventory of the fermentation process.
Sterilisation: Before fermentation the sugar is sterilised to avoid contamination of the reactor which
could potentially disturb the microorganisms and the process operation. After the fermentation the
broth is sterilised to kill the microorganisms. In Satam et al. (2019) the speciﬁc amount of steam
needed for each sterilisation step is not stated, so it is assumed that the "Other heat exchanger"
steam is split equally between these two processes. See Table A.20.10, Appendix A.20 for the full
inventory of the sugar sterilisation and Table A.20.12, Appendix A.20 for the broth sterilisation.
Data quality and uncertainty: The average DQIs for each of the above described unit processes can
be seen in Table 6.2 (See Table A.10.1 in Appendix A.10 for full pedigree matrix). For the fer-
mentation stage the DQIs are between 3.0 and 3.4, indicating low data quality. Satam et al. (2019)
performs Matlab simulations of the BDO production based on Genomatica patents and publications
to estimate the costs, energy and material consumption for a technoeconomic analysis. As tech-
noeconomic analyses are not focused on environmental ﬂows, the completeness and reliability of
the data is low. The mass balance for fermentation is obtained through rough estimations from few
operation parameters such as yield and titers in literature. This entails large uncertainties related
to the process. Furthermore, it is a large assumption that the fermentation can proceed with the
adapted efﬁciency for a mixed sugar substrate instead of only glucose. Although this assumption
is based on literature (Yim et al., 2011; Forte et al., 2016) it introduces large uncertainty in the
estimated inventory for the fermentation process. The media composition is not veriﬁed in any
literature sources, and is highly uncertain. The exclusion of vitamins and amino acids in the me-
dia and the seed train lowers the completeness of the process. This should be considered when
evaluating the LCA results.
6.4.2.3
Downstream processing
The downstream processing of fermentation broth to obtain polymer grade BDO was adapted for
the present study as seen in Figure 6.3 in accordance with Burgard et al. (2016) and Satam et al.
(2019). The description of unit processes and their material and energy requirements were adapted
from Satam et al. (2019). As the present study adapted a different yield, a mixed sugar substrate
instead of only glucose, and a lower recovery of BDO after fermentation, the downstream processes
have been adapted accordingly.
The processing can be divided into ﬁltration, ion exchange and concentration and puriﬁcation. The
ﬁltration processes include microﬁltration, ultraﬁltration and nanoﬁltration. The concentration and
puriﬁcation processes include evaporation, water distillation and BDO distillation. The composi-
tion of the fermentation broth after the fermentation and after the ion exchange bed are deﬁned
in Satam et al. (2019). Furthermore, it is stated that the composition of the ﬁnal BDO product
is 99.99% pure. The deﬁned mass balance can be seen in Appendix A.1 (attached), and the ap-
47
==PAGE:55==
proximated composition of the broth after selected unit processes can be seen in Table 6.5. The
broth composition downstream of fermentation is deﬁned on the basis of water, sugars, BDO, cells,
protein and "Other impurities". Other impurities is further detailed in Appendix A.1 (attached).
Table 6.5: Composition of fermentation broth after selected steps downtream of the bio-BDO fer-
mentation process.

Filtration: After sterilisation the fermentation broth is passed through a microﬁlter to remove spent
cells, an ultraﬁlter to remove proteins and other macromolecules and a nanoﬁlter to remove residual
sugars and multi-valent cations. It is assumed that all of the cells are removed in the microﬁlter,
95% of the proteins are removed in the ultraﬁlter, 85% of the sugars and 35% of the other impu-
rities removed in the nanoﬁlter. After each ﬁltration step diaﬁltration is performed for recovery
of BDO from the ﬁlter retentate to ensure high recovery efﬁciency. The amount of water used for
backwashing of each microﬁlter and electricity for this has been obtained from Satam et al. (2019).
The retentate and water used for washing the ﬁlters are sent to wastewater treatment. See Table
A.20.13, Appendix A.20 for the full inventory of the microﬁlter, Table A.20.14, Appendix A.20 for
the ultraﬁlter, Table A.20.15, Appendix A.20 for the nanoﬁlter, and Table A.20.26, Appendix A.20
for the downstream wastewater treatment.
Ion exchange: Subsequently, the ﬁltered broth is passed through a cation exchange bed to remove
positively charged ions and then through an anion exchange bed to remove negatively charged ions.
It is assumed that another 35% of the other impurities in the fermentation broth are removed in the
ion exchange bed. The amount of water needed for rinsing and regenerating the ion exchange beds
are adapted from Satam et al. (2019). The amount of acid and base used for regeneration is not
deﬁned in that study. Hence, it has been assumed that the acid/base requirements are equal to those
used in the ion exchange bed in the pretreatment of biomass deﬁned by Davis et al. (2015). In that
study the amount of acid and base required for regeneration is deﬁned by the amount of soluble
solids in the stream. It is assumed that 50% of the solids in the fermentation broth are soluble,
48
==PAGE:56==
which is a rough estimate. The retentate, rinsing and regeneration water is sent to wastewater
treatment. See Table A.20.16, Appendix A.20 for the full inventory of the ion exchange process,
and Table A.20.26, Appendix A.20 for the downstream wastewater treatment.
Evaporation: Next the ﬁltered broth is concentrated by applying steam in an evaporation column
based on Satam et al. (2019). The removed water vapor is scrubbed with cold process water to
recover potentially vaporised BDO. The removed water is sent to wastewater treatment. See Table
A.20.17, Appendix A.20 for the full inventory of evaporation, and Table A.20.26, Appendix A.20
for the downstream wastewater treatment.
Water and BDO distillation: The broth is now rich in BDO and passed through a water distillation
column which removes the residual water through the overhead stream, and lastly, a BDO distil-
litation column which removes BDO with a purity of 99.99% w/w BDO in the overhead stream.
The steam use has been adapted from Satam et al. (2019). The water removed is sent to wastewater
treatment. From the BDO distillation column the waste stream consists of impure GBL, which
is combusted. See Table A.20.18, Appendix A.20 for the full inventory of water distillation, Ta-
ble A.20.18, Appendix A.20 for the BDO distillation, and Table A.20.19, Appendix A.20 for the
downstream wastewater treatment.
Data quality and uncertainty:
The average DQIs for each of the above described unit processes can be seen in Table 6.2 (See
Table A.10.1 in Appendix A.10 for full pedigree matrix). For the downstream processing stage the
DQIs are all 3, since all the data is adapted from Satam et al. (2019) indicating low data quality. As
previously mentioned, the dataset is not compiled for use in environmental assessment, but rather
for technoeconomic analysis, hence, the completeness and reliability are low.
6.5
Life cycle inventory of adipic acid production
ADA
The inventories of conventional and bio-based ADA production are described in the following
sections. Each unit process described has a respective LCI table in Appendix A.23 for conventional
ADA and in Appendix A.22 for bio-ADA, which will be referenced in the text. The underlying
mass balance of the bio-ADA and conventional ADA production are found in Appendix A.14.1.
6.5.1
Conventional adipic acid production
The conventional ADA production consist of the two step oxidation process described in Section
3.4.3. The following section forms the inventory of the conventional ADA production describing
the modelling along with major assumptions and data sources. A list including all assumptions is
compiled and found in Appendix A.14. Figure 6.4 presents the processes and system boundaries of
the conventional ADA production. The process is adapted from the ecoinvent v3.6 process "adipic
acid production, RoW", which is disassembled, adapted and reassembled in EASETECH v3.3.7.

6.5.1.1
Raw material stage
The conventional ADA production uses cyclohexane as feedstock, which is derived from benzene
(e.g crude oil and natural gas). This section covers the production of benzene and cyclohexane.
Benzene production: The benzene production process is assumed to be acquired from the global
market, hence the ecoinvent v3.6 process is used, however, it is scaled to the amount of benzene
needed to produce the required amount of cyclohexane (0.7 kg for 1 kg ADA). The benzene process
starts at the raw material extraction and ends at benzene delivery at plant. The process does not
include recyclable amounts of N2 and O2 consumed, unspeciﬁed metal emissions to water and
air, unspeciﬁed CFC/HCFC emission to air, mercaptan emission to air, and dioxin to water. The
ecoinvent process is based on data obtained through company surveys and based on 11 European
sites, but has been adjusted to represent the geography of "RoW". All inputs and outputs of the
process can be seen in the appertaining LCI table in Table A.23.1 in Appendix A.23.
Cyclohexane production: The cyclohexane production is adapted from ecoinvent v3.6, which is
based on Sutter (2007) and Campbell (2011). The process is disassembled by collecting the un-
derlying processes and replace the electricity and heat processes with the marginal electricity and
heat processes assembled for the present study as described in Section 6.3. The cooling water is
modelled as a "once-through" system using "water, cooling, unspeciﬁed natural origin" from the
elementary exchanges in EASETECH. It is assumed that 99.6% of the water is returned to its nat-
ural origin, whereas the rest is lost in the cooling process. This assumption is adapted from the
ecoinvent v3.6 process "market for cooling energy, CH". In addition, the process was scaled to
50
==PAGE:58==
the amount of cyclohexane required for the ADA production. The process starts with the reception
of benzene at the facility and ends with the production of cyclohexane and includes raw material
consumption, energy use and direct emissions. The process is based on European data but is ad-
justed to represent the geography of "RoW". All inputs and outputs of the process can be seen in
the appertaining LCI table in Table A.23.2 Appendix A.23
Uncertainty and data quality: The largest uncertainty of the above processes is the representative-
ness of the data. Both the benzene and cyclohexane production is modelled for the production in
Europe, but assumed to be representative for the global production. Table 6.6 displays a pedigree
matrix assessing the data quality of the processes included in the conventional ADA production.
Here it is seen that benzene and cyclohexane production both obtain a data quality index (DQI) of
2.2 and 2.3, respectively, which indicates relatively good data quality, nevertheless the geograph-
ical correlation of the data is low since the processes are based on European data, however, the
energy was adapted to represent the US and Europe and the US may have similar or slightly similar
production conditions. Furthermore, the cyclohexane process is based on literature data and stoi-
chiometric calculations since no actual plant data is available, which is also represented in lower
reliability score compared to the benzene production. An issue of the pedigree matrix is the quali-
tative estimation of each parameter by the LCA practitioner. However, it provides an indication of
the overall data quality and accentuates in what way the data can be improved for further work.

production of the present study. The pedigree matrix is shaped in accordance with Weidema et al.

6.5.1.2
Production stage
Adipic acid production: The ADA production is adapted from ecoinvent v3.6, which is based on
Althaus et al. (2007) and Gendorf Chemiepark (2018). The process starts with the raw materials
entering the facility and ends with the production of 1 kg ADA after reﬁning to 99% purity. The
process is based on stoichiometric calculations. As for the cyclohexane process the ADA produc-
tion process is disassembled by collecting the underlying processes and replacing the energy and
sources with the marginal processes described in Section 6.3. The cooling water is modelled as de-
51
==PAGE:59==
scribed for cyclohexane. The ecoinvent process of ADA production does not include vanadium and
copper catalysts, these are added in the present study based on information derived from Oppen-
heim and Dickerson (2014). However, a vanadium production process does not exist in ecoinvent
v3.6 and copper is used as a proxy. The data used for compiling the ecoinvent process has no
geographical origin since it is based on stoichiometric calculations and estimations. All inputs and
outputs of the process can be seen in the appertaining LCI Table A.23.3 in Appendix A.23
Uncertainty and data quality: The uncertainty within this process primarily lies within the unavail-
ability of data, which represents a major challenge when assessing chemical production due to high
competition on the market. The process is based on literature data and stoichiometric calculations
since no actual plant data is available. Furthermore, the use of copper as proxy for vanadium might
not be representative. Some of the data is adapted from German chemical plants and are represen-
tative for European conditions. The above issue is also represented in the completeness score in the
pedigree matrix in Table 6.6. Since the data of the geographic representativeness has no speciﬁc
origin it was estimated to have low correlation even though the estimation might be representative
for the area of assessment. The DQI of the ADA production is 3.3 indicating areas of low data
quality, which must be taken into account, when drawing conclusions.
6.5.2
Bio-based adipic acid production
The processes and system boundaries of the bio-based ADA production is depicted in Figure 6.5.
The level of detail is high. However since the bio-ADA production is not commercial and is assem-
bled using literature data not combining the pretreatment and fermentation processes the bio-ADA
production is not complete. Nevertheless the completeness of the fossil-based and bio-based ADA
production is determined to be comparable. The following sections will provide descriptions of
the modelled unit processes in the main production line of bio-ADA, while processes outside of
the main production line denoted "Other processes" are described in Appendix A.11. Major as-
sumptions and data sources are described in the text, and a full list of assumptions is compiled and
can be found in Appendix A.14. As previously mentioned, the production of bio-based ADA is
divided into the ﬁve sub-stages (1) Feedstock provision, (2) mechanical pretreatment, (3) chemi-
cal pretreatment, (4) fermentation, and (4) downstream processing and hydrogenation. Feedstock
provision and mechanical pretreatment is modelled identically for both the production of bio-based
BDO and ADA and is described in Section 6.1 and 6.2.

6.5.2.1
Chemical pretreatment:
To the knowledge of the authors, a pretreatment process as the one described in the present study
does not exist, since the chemical pretreatment step - lignin ﬁrst, is a novel technology of techno-
logical readiness level (TRL) 3 according to EC (2014).
The lignin ﬁrst process is an organosolv fractionation process, where lignin is separated from the
holocellulose. This particular process focuses on an effective lignin fractionation resulting in high
purity lignin. The holocellulose can according to Anderson et al. (2016a); Karp et al. (2014) po-
tentially be used for 2G glucose production. Since the lignin ﬁrst process focuses on lignin the
composition of the obtained oil containing aromatic compounds is known. This is in contrast to
other pretreatment methods such as alkaline pretreatment as they do not assess the produced lignin
oil since it is of low quality and considered a residual (Karp et al., 2014; Linger et al., 2014).
In the lignin ﬁrst process is a hydrogenation process, in which corn stover is suspended in methanol
and a nickel catalyst on carbon support is added. Furthermore, phosphoric acid is added to enhance
the performance of the catalyst. Hydrogen is bubbled through the system and the vessel is heated
to 250°C and cooled down afterwards (Anderson et al., 2016a) as described in 3.4. Table 6.8 shows
the biomass composition after each step of the pretreatment stage of the bio-based ADA production.
The calculations of the mass balance forming the basis for the inventory can be found in Appendix
A.1 (attached). The composition of the lignin oil extracted in the lignin ﬁst process is displayed in
Figure 6.6.
Figure 6.6: Composition of the extracted lignin oil of the lignin ﬁrst process in percentage.
Since the lignin ﬁrst technology is at TRL 3 the process is not fully optimised. Assumptions are
made to theoretically optimise the process from Anderson et al. (2016a) resulting in the making of
rough assumptions. The major assumptions include: (1) the reduction of the ratio between solvent
(methanol) and feedstock from 30:1 to 1:1; (2) the lignin yield is assumed to reach same efﬁciency
55
==PAGE:63==
of the cellulose yield of cellulose extraction of biomass in the production of 2G sugar taken from
Davis et al. (2015); (3) an organic soluble oil is obtained through the lignin ﬁrst process consisting
mainly of methyl coumarate, methyl ferulate, propyl guaiacol, and propyl syringol (see Figure 6.6)
(Anderson et al., 2016b). The proportion of methyl p-coumaric acid in the lignin oil is assumed to
be the same as in Anderson et al. (2016b) despite the more efﬁcient lignin extraction yield; (4) the
heat required is calculated based on the heat capacity of methanol and the temperature difference
(from 25°C to 250°(Anderson et al., 2016a)), (5) the process does not include any recirculating
heating and cooling system; (6) solvent and catalyst is recovered after the lignin ﬁrst process; and
(7) the entire lignin oil is used as substrate for the fermentation even though only p-coumaric acid
is converted.
The cooling water is based on the same assumption as for the cooling water used for the conven-
tional ADA production using a "once-through" system. Table A.22.4 in Appendix A.22 discloses
the LCI table of this process including all literature references used for compiling the model.

Methanol and catalyst recovery: Both methanol and catalyst is assumed to be recovered after per-
forming the lignin ﬁrst process. In Corona et al. (2018b) 99.23% of the ethanol used as solvent
is recovered from the catalytic hydrogenation step. Based on this it is assumed that 99.23% of
the methanol from the lignin ﬁrst process can be recycled. This might, however, be an optimistic
assumption, since the mixture after the lignin ﬁrst process possibly includes more impurities com-
pared to the process described in Corona et al. (2018b). The methanol recovery is assumed to
consist of a distillation process. Roy and Dutta (2013) describes the energy consumption of the
distillation of ethanol, which has been adapted to methanol taking the different density of methanol
into account. Table A.22.5 in Appendix A.22 discloses the LCI table of this process including
literature references used for compiling the model.
The catalyst recovery is, due to the lack of data, modelled as the substitution of nickel and reac-
tivation of the carbon used for support. By using the reactivation step some energy consumption
is associated to the process. 90% of the catalyst is assumed to be recovered based on the recov-
56
==PAGE:64==
ery potential of activated carbon. In reality other catalyst recycling processes, for instant, an ion
exchange process might be more realistic for the recovery of metal catalyst. However, Sala et al.
(2017) describes the current challenges of recovering heterogeneous catalyst as the one used in
the lignin ﬁrst process. Table A.22.16 in Appendix A.22 discloses the LCI table of this process
including all literature references used for compiling the model.
Uncertainty and data quality: The main source to uncertainty within the chemical pretreatment step
is the limited data availability. The lignin ﬁrst process is based data obtained from a laboratory
scale proof-of-concept experiment. Many rough assumptions are made to theoretically optimise
the process. Table 6.7 displays the DQI of the two processes covered by the chemical pretreatment
step. The DQI of lignin ﬁrst is 2.8, which can be regarded as moderate data quality. Table A.12
in Appendix shows the individual scores forming the DQI. Here it is seen that the obtained data
is poorly regarding completeness and the technological correlation, underling the high uncertainty
related to this technology due to the low TRL. The DQI for the methanol and catalyst recovery is
even lower, since it is based on rough assumptions leading to rough estimates. Hence, for future
work the data quality of this production step should be improved and the high uncertainty of this
processes must be taken into account when evaluating the results. However, performing LCA
of early stage process development is still valuable since it gives indications on hot-spots of the
processes forming the base for process development and improvements (Tufvesson et al., 2013).
6.5.2.2
Fermentation
The bio-conversion step of this process is a fed-batch fermentation of methyl p-coumaric acid into
MA by an engineered strain of P. putida and is based on Vardon et al. (2015); Salvachúa et al.
(2018); Vardon et al. (2016); van Duuren et al. (2020); Corona et al. (2018b,a). The addition of
vitamins, amino acids, seed cultivation, and air supply are neglected in this process.
Two major assumptions are made regarding the fermentation process of the present study. (1)
methyl coumaric acid is the only compound of the lignin oil converted obtaining the same yields
as p-coumaric acid (0.94 mol MA/mol substrate (Salvachúa et al., 2018)), and (2) the titer of the
present study is assumed to be 100 g/L despite the fact that the highest titer reported in literature
of lab scale fermentation experiments is 34.5 g/L in Vardon et al. (2016) using benzoic acid as
substrate. The titer is one of the economic metrics when it comes to biochemical processes, and are
related to the operation costs of downstream processes, hence a commercial process must obtain a
certain titer to increase feasibility (Villadsen et al., 2011).
The by-product yield is assumed to be 0.06 mol/mol of feedstock assuming a theoretical yield of
1 mol MA/mol substrate. The by-products of the fermentation are catabolic intermediates such as
protocatechuate or catechol. In the present study they are modelled as being protocatechuate, since
no data on the yields of the different by-products are available.
57
==PAGE:65==
The amount of water needed for the fermentation is calculated based on the titer. The produc-
tion processes of the nutrients added to the fermentation process are taken from the ecoinvent v3.6
database. However, not all nutrients are covered by ecoinvent and proxies are used. Speciﬁcally,
potassium sulphate is used as proxy for potassium dihydrogen phosphate and sodium phosphate is
used as proxy for disodium phosphate. The amount of nutrients is calculated based on concentra-
tions derived from (Vardon et al., 2016) and the water added to the process. In the modelling of
the fermentation process it is further assumed that all nutrients are metabolised by P. putida. Table
A.22.6 in Appendix A.22 discloses the LCI table of this process including literature references used
for compiling the model.
Uncertainty and data quality: Several uncertainties are linked to the fermentation process. It is
assumed that methyl p-coumaric acid can be directly converted, if this is not the case an additional
process might be needed to remove the methyl group or further metabolic engineering must be
performed lowering the completeness of the assembled process. Vardon et al. (2015); Salvachúa
et al. (2018); Vardon et al. (2016); van Duuren et al. (2020) are mainly articles on the development
of successful and efﬁcient bio-based ADA production providing data regarding yield, separation
efﬁciency, through laboratory experiments, whereas Corona et al. (2018b) is a LCA study, based
on process simulations obtained using the software Aspen+, simulating large scale production of
ADA. Based on this some assumptions are made to simulate optimised processes. The DQI of
the fermentation process is 2.8, which is mainly due to the above described data retrieved from
laboratory experiments and process simulation. Furthermore, the completeness of the data is not
deemed high, which can be expected when assessing an early stage processes. The underlying
pedigree matrix of the DQI is found in Appendix A.12.
6.5.2.3
Downstream processing
To obtain polymer grade ADA, requiring purity of >99.8%, downstream processing is necessary.
Furthermore, the hydrogenation is more efﬁcient using high purity MA since impurities will reduce
the performance of the catalyst. The downstream processing consist of different separation and
puriﬁcation step followed by hydrogenation of MA into ADA. The downstream processing applied
in this production stage are primarily based on Vardon et al. (2016) and Corona et al. (2018b). It
consists of centrifugation, microﬁltration to remove proteins, activated carbon ﬁlter removing by-
products, crystallisation, drying, puriﬁcation using ethanol, upgrading to ADA by hydrogenation.
Furthermore, the present study includes recovery steps for activated carbon, ethanol, hydrogen
and catalyst. The composition of the fermentation broth after selected steps of the downstream
processing are presented in Table 6.9.

Centrifugation: This process includes the separation of cells from the fermentation broth. It is here
assumed that 20% of the water is removed along with the cells. The energy consumption is adapted
from Corona et al. (2018b), which provides electricity consumption for the entire separation process
(ﬁltration steps and crystallisation). It is assumed that the electricity covers this centrifugation
step and microﬁltration. The electricity consumption is divided equally between the two ﬁltration
steps. Table A.22.7 in Appendix A.22 discloses the LCI table of this process including literature
references used for compiling the model.
Microﬁltration: This process removes proteins from the fermentation broth. The MA recovery of
this process was adapted from Vardon et al. (2016), stating a MA recovery of 97.7%. The remaining
MA is entering the WWTP of the modelled system. The energy consumption is described in the
centrifugation paragraph above. Table A.22.8 in Appendix A.22 discloses the LCI table of this
process including literature references used for compiling the model.
Activated carbon ﬁlter: The activated carbon ﬁlter removes by-products and the unconverted aro-
matic compounds of the lignin oil, which are entering the WWTP of the system. The activated
carbon load is derived from Vardon et al. (2016). No loss of MA is considered in the activated
carbon ﬁlter. It is assumed, that this process does not consume any signiﬁcant electricity. Table
A.22.9 in Appendix A.22 discloses the LCI table of this process including literature references used
for compiling the model.
Crystallisation: MA is separated from the fermentation broth by crystallisation. The MA recovery
rate is derived from Vardon et al. (2016). The crystallisation is initiated by a pH shift using sul-
phuric acid. The sulphuric acid concentration is assumed to be 2M and the amount needed for the
pH shift is calculated using buffer chemistry. Information regarding cooling duties of the crystalli-
59
==PAGE:67==
sation step is derived from Corona et al. (2018b). Since the fermentation broth is cooled down to
5°C chilled water is used for this process. The amount of cooling water needed for this process
is estimated based on the heat capacity of water and the amount of cooling energy in MJ needed.
Based on Vardon et al. (2016) the recovery rate of the crystallisation is assumed to be 86%. It
is, furthermore, assumed that the water content of the MA after crystallisation is 5% of the water
added in the fermentation stage. The remaining fermentation broth is entering the WWTP of the
system. Furthermore, it is assumed that the ethanol used in this process is recovered and can be
used in the subsequent catalytic hydrogenation process. Table A.22.10 in Appendix A.22 discloses
the LCI table of this process including literature references used for compiling the model.
Ethanol puriﬁcation: According to Vardon et al. (2016) an additional puriﬁcation step using ethanol
as solvent is needed to obtain polymer grade ADA. However, little information regarding this pro-
cess is given in the article. The amount of ethanol used for this process is estimated using the
solubility of MA in ethanol (Scelfo et al., 2017), which increases with increasing temperature,
adding a 10% buffer. It is assumed that this process operates at 75°C. The heat required is adapted
from the catalytic hydrogenation process of Corona et al. (2018b), since both processes primarily
consists of the heating of ethanol to 75°C. However, the amount of ethanol used for this process is
not the same for both processes and the heat consumption might be overestimated. The MA recov-
ery of this process is derived from Vardon et al. (2016) and 96.6% is recovered. The extracted salts
of this process are sent to the inert landﬁll of the system described in Appendix A.11 and the ele-
mentary composition of the salts is derived from Vardon et al. (2016). Table A.22.11 in Appendix
A.22 discloses the LCI table of this process including literature references used for compiling the
model.
Catalytic hydrogenation: The catalytic hydrogenation step is adapted from Corona et al. (2018b). In
this process puriﬁed MA dissolved in ethanol and a Rh/C catalyst is added. The process is supplied
with hydrogen and heated to 75°C. The amount of ethanol, catalyst, hydrogen, heat and electricity
required for the process is derived from Corona et al. (2018a). The amount of Rh on carbon is
assumed to the equal to the lignin ﬁrst process being 5% w/w. A ’once-through cooling system is
assumed for the afterwards cooling and 99.6% of the cooling water is lead back to its origin. The
conversion rate of this process is 0.97 mol ADA/mol MA (Vardon et al., 2016). Based on Corona
et al. (2018b) ethanol and hydrogen are recovered. It is furthermore, assumed that the catalyst
is recovered. Table A.22.12 in Appendix A.22 discloses the LCI table of this process including
literature references used for compiling the model.
Ethanol, hydrogen and catalyst recovery: Both ethanol, hydrogen and catalyst is assumed to be re-
covered after performing ethanol puriﬁcation and catalytic hydrogenation. The ethanol and hydro-
gen recovery rate are based on Corona et al. (2018b) being 99.23% and 48% respectively. The
ethanol recovery is assumed to consist of a distillation process similar to the methanol distillation
described in the chemical pretreatment step, estimating the energy consumption based on Roy and
Dutta (2013).
60
==PAGE:68==
The hydrogen is assumed to be recirculated and is modelled by substituting 48% of the hydrogen
entering the catalytic hydrogenation process.
The catalyst recovery is modelled as described in Section 6.5.2.1 replacing the substitution of nickel
with rhodium. Table A.22.14 A.22.15 A.22.16 in Appendix A.22 discloses the LCI tables of this
processes including literature references used for compiling the model.
Uncertainty and data quality: As for the previous process stages. The main source of uncertainty
is the underlying data quality. Using laboratory scale data is not optimal and lowers the data
quality in regards to completeness and technological correlation. The DQI for each sub-process
within the downstream and hydrogenation stage are revealed in Table 6.7 and Table A.12.1 in
Appendix A.12 shows the underlying pedigree matrix. showing that the process excluding the
recovery processes obtain a score around 3. The difference in the scores is due to some processes
being based on veriﬁed experimental data and other to a higher degree include qualiﬁed estimations.
As for the recovery processes of the pretreatment the data quality of recovery process of this stage
is low (DQI of 4.6-4.8) due to the many rough estimations made. In general most of the data
quality of assembling the bio-based ADA production should be improved and better data should be
collected to improve the LCI results, this is nevertheless, a very time consuming step. However,
it is deemed that the results of the present study will still compute valuable results. The pedigree
matrix compiling the DQI is found in Appendix A.12.
6.6
Basis for sensitivity analysis
General
The sensitivity of the results is tested through perturbation analysis for continuous parameters and
through scenario analysis for discrete parameters. Some scenarios are applicable to both bio-based
BDO and ADA and are described in Appendix A.15. Scenarios only applicable to bio-BDO are de-
scribed in Appendix A.16, while scenarios only applicable to bio-ADA are described in Appendix
A.17. An overview of all scenarios can be seen in Table 6.10.
The perturbation analysis is conducted using the LCA software EASETECH. Each parameter is
changed +10% and sensitivity ratios are calculated with the following formula:
SR =
∆output
output
,∆input
input

(5)
The sensitivity ratios are then normalised to the maximum absolute sensitivity ratio within each
impact category. This reveals the most sensitive parameter in each impact category with the nor-
malised value of 1. The parameters with normalised value below 0.25 are considered not sensitive
within the category, the parameters with normalised value between 0.25 and 0.75 are considered
moderately sensitive within the category, and parameters with normalised value above 0.75 are
considered highly sensitive within the category.

==PAGE:70==
7
Life Cycle Impact Assessment
General
The characterised and normalised results obtained from LCIA using ReCiPe 2016 and World 2010
normalisation references are presented in the following sections. The unit for externally normalised
results is person equivalents (PE). The impact categories in focus in the current study are: cli-
mate change (CC), freshwater eutrophication (FEU), marine eutrophication (MEU), fossil resource
scarcity (FRS), terrestrial eco-toxicity (TET), freshwater eco-toxicity (FET), marine eco-toxicity
(MET), human toxicity: cancer (HTC), human toxicity: non-cancer (HTNC), land use (LU), and
water use (WU).
7.1
Uncertain and variable impact categories
General
There is a general consensus in LCA that the complexity of quantifying toxic effects entails high
uncertainties in the characterisation factors and consequently the LCIA results for toxic categories
(Rosenbaum et al., 2018; PRé Sustainability, 2014). This is often used as an argument to exclude
toxic impact categories from LCA studies, however, these categories are included in the present
study, and conclusions based on impacts in toxic categories are made with extra caution. Accord-
ing to Aymard and Botta-Genoulaz (2017) using different LCIA methods and normalisation factors
can drastically change the results of human toxicity impacts. Furthermore, the normalisation ref-
erences currently available for toxic impacts are incompletely covering toxic substance emissions,
leading to underestimated normalisation references, and hence, overestimated normalised toxic im-
pact results (Pizzol et al., 2017). These issues will be examined further in sensitivity analysis.
Some LCIA impact categories represent global environmental issues such as global warming repre-
sented by CC impact. However, other impact categories represent environmental issues that differ
largely regionally or even locally (Sedlbauer et al., 2007). Among these are the LU and WU im-
pact categories. These categories are subject to much discussion in relation to inclusion of spatial
differentiation. Nonetheless, spatial differentiation is rarely included in LCA studies including the
present study. Instead, the LU and WU impacts obtained in the present study are used mainly to
compare the m2 ·yr and m3 consumed, respectively, between conventional and bio-derived chemi-
cals, and not to assess the speciﬁc impact on the environment.
7.2
1,4-butanediol
BDO
7.2.1
Characterised net impacts
The characterised net impacts for conventional BDO and bio-BDO are displayed in Table 7.1.
The table shows that conventional BDO production has numerically lower impacts in eight out of
11 impact categories, including FEU, MEU, TET, FET, MET, HTC, HTNC, LU and WU. This
observation does not take any model or parameter uncertainty into account. The impact categories
where the largest differences between conventional and bio-BDO are observed are LU (872 times
63
==PAGE:71==
larger for bio-BDO), MEU (12 times larger for bio-BDO) and FRS (18 times larger for conventional
BDO). For conventional BDO production the categories FET and MET have negative impacts,
indicating environmental "savings" related to the production.

1.4
Bio-BDO has less than half of the CC impact compared to conventional BDO, which is in line with
claims by Novamont (2020) for their Mater Biotech bio-BDO production plant in Italy. CC impact
is commonly used in argumentation favouring the transition away from fossil-based chemicals, and
therefore, a lower CC impact could be expected. As previously mentioned the FRS impact is 18
times higher for conventional BDO, which was also expected due to the use of fossil resources
as feedstock in the conventional BDO production (acetylene and formaldehyde). The difference
is much higher between the two BDO production pathways in FRS than CC, which is due to the
larger extraction of fossil resources in conventional BDO production, while the chemical products
are not being used or disposed off within the scope of this study (cradle-to-gate study). If the BDO
is to be used in polymer production and the polymer is incinerated at its end of life, the incineration
of conventional BDO would entail even higher CC impacts compared to bio-BDO, since the feed-
stock is fossil, whereas the feedstock is biological for bio-BDO (carbon from biological sources is
considered biogenic and not counted as contributing to the global warming effect).
The CC impact for bio-BDO is generally higher in the current study compared to other studies in
literature examining the life cycle of bio-BDO or products containing bio-BDO (range between 1.6-
3.0 kg CO2-eq/kg bio-BDO, See Table 3.2). However, it should be noted that among these studies
and the present study there is a lot of variation in the scope and modelling procedures including se-
lected feedstock crop, whether it’s 1G or 2G biomass, and the pretreatment and production methods
employed. This is to be discussed further.
64
==PAGE:72==
The LU category has the highest discrepancy in the impacts between conventional and bio-BDO
(872 times larger for bio-BDO). The large discrepancy factor is partly due to conventional BDO
production having an impact within this category very close to zero. LU impact is expected to
be higher for bio-BDO than conventional BDO due to the use of 2G biomass (corn stover) as
feedstock for production. However, in the present model the impacts related to LU come from the
production of soybean meal and straw, which are the products used to replace the services provided
by corn stover before shifting to bio-BDO production. Hence, this should be taken into account
when looking at the higher LU impacts for bio-BDO compared to conventional BDO. This will be
examined further in sensitivity analysis.
The lowest differences in impacts between conventional and bio-BDO are found within WU and
HTNC. As previously mentioned the toxic impact categories have high uncertainties related to
them and therefore it is only possible to rank compared products if the differences are relatively
large. This is true for the other toxic impact categories as well (TET, FET, MET and HTC with dis-
crepancy factors ranging from 2.3-3.5 between conventional and bio-BDO). For WU the difference
is also too small to deem conventional or bio-BDO favourable when considering the data quality,
assumptions and related uncertainties.
7.2.2
Externally normalised impacts
The externally normalised impact results for conventional and bio-BDO are displayed in Figure
7.1. The impacts of FEU, FRS, FET, MET and HTC are shown in a separate diagram due to their
large PEs in either the conventional or bio-BDO production process. It is not surprising to ﬁnd
higher PEs in FET, MET and HTC, since the normalisation references for the toxicity categories
are known to have high uncertainties and generally are underestimated, often resulting in high
PEs in these categories. However, this can also be said for TET and HTNC that have PEs in the
relatively low end within this study.
In order to assess if the normalised impacts are realistic a very rough estimation is made on the
amount of BDO produced per global population annually. According to Ingram and Le (2013) the
production of BDO was estimated to be around 1.5 billion kg/yr in 2012. The global population
amounted to 7.2 billion in the same year (UN, 2013). The global BDO production could then be
said to be 0.2 kg/pers/yr in 2012. Which means that one kg BDO (functional unit) is produced
yearly per ﬁve persons globally. Considering this none of the obtained PEs seem implausibly high.
For bio-BDO in the CC impact category the above means ≈0.0005 PEs are used for the yearly
production related to ﬁve persons. This low number seems immediately plausible given the vast
amount of activities that contribute to a persons overall climate change impact, hence the production
of BDO should not take up a large part of a persons CC impact budget.

ADA
7.3.1
Characterised net impacts
The characterised net impacts of the conventional and bio-ADA production are displayed in Table
7.2. Bio-ADA production has numerically lower impacts in seven out of 11 impact categories. The
seven impact categories include: CC, FRS, TET, FET, MET, HTC, and HTNC. This observation
does not take any uncertainty into account. The impact categories, where the numerical difference
within the conventional and bio-ADA production is largest are MEU, FEU, HTNC and LU. For
MEU and LU Bio-ADA is approximately 60 times higher compared to conventional ADA, whereas
FEU is 19 times higher than conventional ADA production and HTNC is 16 times lower for bio-
compared to conventional ADA obtaining a negative impact meaning that the bio-ADA production
is an environmental saving in this category. The high discrepancy of LU is due to biomass used
for 2G. However, the accounted LU is represented by the substitution of straw for bedding and soy
bean meal for fodder and not the actual corn production providing the feedstock for production. The
actual LU of the bio-ADA production might thus, be higher, however, this depends on modelling
choices of allocating the emissions of corn production to the corn grain and corn stover, which is
not included in the present study, but discussed in the sensitivity analysis. The small difference in
the FRS category is due to the use of fossil derived solvents used in the bio-ADA production.

4.3
Table 7.2 also shows a small difference between conventional ADA and bio-ADA production in the
CC impact category. This ﬁnding is not in line with ﬁndings from literature, some of which can be
seen in Table 3.3. The table shows, that the different studies ﬁnd the CC impact of the conventional
ADA production to be between 22.6 - 25.6 kg CO2-eq. These numbers are similar to the 27.0
kg CO2-eq. found in the present study. However, Corona et al. (2018a) includes a conventional
ADA production for the US obtaining a CC impact of 12.9, which is more than half the impact of
the present study, which also aimed at simulating the ADA production in the US. The difference
might lay within the assumption of better N2O abatement technologies the US production process
in Corona et al. (2018b). Since N2O is a very potent greenhouse gas this can have a substantial
impact on the CC category.
Looking at the different literature results regarding bio-ADA production the results range from 4.0
kg CO2-eq. in Aryapratama and Janssen (2017) and 18 kg CO2-eq. in van Duuren et al. (2020). The
two processes are very different using different feedstock and pretreatment method. Furthermore,
Aryapratama and Janssen (2017) is a direct fermentation process and van Duuren et al. (2020) a
semi chemical processes as the one of the present study. van Duuren et al. (2020) is the process
closest to the results obtained in the present study (24.0 kg CO2-eq.) using similar feedstock, fer-
mentation and downstream processing but different pretreatment methods. In addition, a different
environmental assessment was made by Colodel et al. (2009), which in relative terms conclude no
signiﬁcant difference in CC impact with bio-ADA having a slightly lower CC impact. The same
can be said about the CC impact of the present study.
67
==PAGE:75==
7.3.2
Externally normalised impacts
The externally normalised results are displayed in Figure 7.2. The impacts of FEU, FRS, FET,
MET and HTC are shown in a separate diagram due to their large PEs in either the conventional or
the bio-ADA production process. The normalisation references of the toxicity impact categories are
known to be subject to high uncertainties, thereby underestimating these categories, which results
in high PEs. It is therefore not surprising to ﬁnd high PEs within FET, MET and HTC.
To determine, whether the found PEs are within a realistic range a "back-of-the-envelope" calcula-
tion is made ﬁnding one persons yearly ADA consumption. According to BCC Research (2019) the
annual global production volume of ADA in 2017 was 3.8 million tonnes and the global population
in 2017 was 7.6 billion people (UN, 2017). One person therefore consumed 0.5 kg ADA in 2017,
hence the results of this study having a functional unit of 1 kg ADA supplies 2 persons per year.
One person uses approximately 1‰ of its climate budget on the consumption of ADA. In the light
of this the magnitude of the results of the present study do not seem unrealistically high compared
to the innumerable activities contributing to a persons climate change impacts. The other impact
categories lie within the same range and non of them are deemed unrealistically high.

results are displayed in two diagrams to enhance visibility of lower impact categories. CC = climate
change. FEU = Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET
= terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human
toxicity: cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
