8
Interpretation
General
8.1
Contribution analysis
In the following sections the production of bio-BDO and bio-ADA will be examined further to re-
veal the main contributing life cycle stages, unit processes, consumption categories and substances.
These will be held up against the data quality, major assumptions and model choices taken during
the LCI.
8.1.1
1,4-butanediol
BDO
The bio-BDO production life cycle is divided into ﬁve stages, namely, feedstock provision, me-
chanical pretreatment, chemical pretreatment, fermentation, and downstream processing (See an
overview of which unit processes are included in each stage in Table 6.2). Additional processes that
lie outside the main bio-BDO production line are categorised under "other processes", i.e. wastewa-
ter treatment and handling of by-products. The contributions of each stage are visualised in Figure
8.1. Looking across impact categories, the main contributing stages in the bio-BDO production
are feedstock provision, fermentation and "other processes". Chemical pretreatment contributes
visibly to all impact categories, although mainly to MEU and WU. Mechanical pretreatment and
downstream processing contribute less considerably when looking across impact categories. Me-
chanical pretreatment mainly contributes to CC and FRS since it accounts for the electricity and
fuels needed to mill, dry and densify corn stover, transport it to the bio-BDO plant and handle it at
reception at the bio-BDO plant. Downstream processing does not contribute largely to any impact
categories.
8.1.1.1
Feedstock provision has signiﬁcant impact on bio-BDO performance
From Figure 8.2 it is evident that feedstock provision is a highly contributing process. In Figure
8.2 the stages are unfolded to reveal the contribution of each unit process to the environmental
impacts of bio-BDO production. In the present study feedstock provision is modelled as the system
expansion necessary to substitute the services that are currently provided by the corn stover (before
it is shifted to the production of BDO). Accordingly, in Figure 8.2 the feedstock provision has
been divided into "Biomass substitution (Soybean meal)" and "Biomass substitution (Straw)". It
becomes evident that soybean meal and straw production contribute more or less equally to the
LU category. Aside from this straw production contributes negatively to HTNC, which is due to
metal (mainly zinc) uptake by the plant during cultivation. Soybean meal production contributes
largely to FEU and MEU due to treatment of vegetable oil reﬁnery wastewater and nitrate leaching
to groundwater during cultivation, respectively. It is also the second largest single contributing
process to the CC impact, which relates to soil carbon release due to land use changes (plant carbon
uptake deducted).
69
==PAGE:77==
Figure 8.1: Environmental impact contributions of each life cycle stage in the bio-BDO production
normalised to the sum of absolute values within each category. CC = climate change. FEU =
Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial
ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity:
cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
8.1.1.2
Side stream processes drive climate change and other impacts
In Figure 8.1 the "Other processes" include the unit processes outside the main production line
of bio-BDO, namely, (1) upstream WWT, (2) upstream biogas combustion, (3) upstream sludge
combustion, (4) upstream utilities electricity, (5) lignin combustion, (6) downstream WWT, and
(7) GBL combustion. "Other processes" contribute largely to FEU, TET, FET, MET with positive
contributions, and to CC and FRS with negative contributions. In Figure 8.2 the "Other processes"
are unfolded to disclose the unit processes. Substitution processes are shown separately. This
reveals that heat substitution related to combustion of the lignin side stream ("Heat subst. lignin
comb.") account for the majority of "Other processes" contribution to CC, FRS, FET and MET
(>90%). All of these impacts relate to the heat mix deﬁned for the model, which relies on natural
gas. The observed positive impacts from heat substitution, hence, indicate categories where the heat
mix has negative impacts and vice versa. The reason why the heat mix has negative contributions
to FET and MET relate to removal of copper ions from groundwater (contribute >90% to FET and
MET for heat, see substance contribution for heat production in Appendix A.19, Table A.19.1).
70
==PAGE:78==
Figure 8.2: Environmental impact contributions of unit processes in the bio-BDO production nor-
malised to the sum of absolute values within each category. Substitution processes are shown
separately. CC = climate change. FEU = Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil
resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine
ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity: cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use.
WU = water use.
This relates to the heat being co-generated with electricity, thereby substituting electricity produc-
tion. The combustion of lignin as a side stream has not been the main focus of this study, hence,
the data quality should be improved. The fuel value of the lignin stream is derived from its content
71
==PAGE:79==
of lignin and carbohydrates and literature values for these components in other types of biomass
than corn stover. Furthermore, it is assumed that the lignin stream is dried from 62% to 40% wa-
ter content before combustion, and the electricity demand for this is adapted from the mechanical
pretreatment drying process, and is therefore uncertain.
Another process outside the main bio-BDO production line with visible contributions is the down-
stream WWT, which has considerable contribution to FEU. This is due to the release of phosphate
to surface water (contribute 89% of FEU for downstream WWT, see substance contribution for
downstream WWT in Appendix A.19, Table A.19.8). The upstream WWT does not contribute
visibly to FEU, which may be due to the different modelling approaches for the two WWT plants.
The upstream WWT model is based on literature data from Davis et al. (2015), while downstream
WWT is based on Ecoinvent v3.6 process "Treatment of wastewater from vegetable oil reﬁnery,
GLO". This will be further investigated in sensitivity analysis.
8.1.1.3
Fermentation nutrients are the largest contributors to human toxicity: cancer
In Figure 8.1 the fermentation stage includes - aside from the fermentation process itself - both
sugar sterilisation (before fermentation) and broth sterilisation (after fermentation). These pro-
cesses are shown individually in Figure 8.2. The sterilisation steps contribute with <15% of the
impacts of the fermentation stage in all impact categories. The fermentation process is the highest
single contributor to HTC, and has considerable contributions to FEU, TET, FET, MET, HTNC
and WU. In Figure 8.3 the fermentation process is examined to see which sub-processes contribute
the most. It is clear that production of sodium phosphate for the fermentation media is the main
contributor within all categories except for MEU, where LP steam is the largest contributor. In
HTC sodium phosphate production contributes with 96% of the impacts, which is related to long-
term Chromium VI emissions to ground- and surface water (contribute 76% and 21% of HTC for
fermentation, see substance contribution for fermentation in Appendix A.19, Table A.19.7). Potas-
sium sulfate production, which is used as a proxy for potassium phosphate in the fermentation
media, has some inﬂuence in most categories and is very uncertain, since it is not the actual media
constituent modelled. Furthermore, it is undisclosed which exact substances and concentrations
are used for the fermentation media in existing bio-BDO production. The substances used in the
current study are based on the M9 minimal media being mentioned in Genomatica (2015b) and the
concentrations of this type of media stated on Sigma Aldrich’s webpage (Sigma Aldrich, 2020). It
is very likely that the fermentation media at a large bio-BDO production facility will be optimised
from that of a standard formulation, and hence, the modelled amount of nutrients can be considered
fairly conservative. On the other hand, Sigma Aldrich (2020) states that the addition of amino acids
and vitamins is common, and these are not included in the present study due to lack of data.
72
==PAGE:80==
Figure 8.3: Environmental impact contributions of each sub-process in the fermentation unit pro-
cess for bio-BDO normalised to the sum of absolute values within each category. FEU = Freshwa-
ter. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity.
FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity: cancer. HTNC
= human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
8.1.1.4
Chemicals are more contributing in most categories than electricity consumption
In Figure 8.4 the sub-processes have been sorted into a number of groups not related to the unit
processes in which they are used. This reveals the contribution of overall consumption of categories
such as chemicals, electricity, process water, and steam. Aside from feedstock provision and heat
substitution, that have already been deemed highly contributing to overall impacts, it is evident
that the category "chemicals" is a considerable contributor to several impacts. This overlaps partly
with the nutrients used in the fermentation media, but additionally includes the chemicals used in
pretreatment of the corn stover. Furthermore, it is seen that energy consumption (electricity and
steam) are not highly contributing across impact categories. Nonetheless, it is likely that energy
consumption is a point of interest for optimisation for economic reasons. Enzymes have a rela-
tively large contribution to MEU making up almost 100% of the "enzymatic hydrolysis and lignin
ﬁltration" impact seen in Figure 8.1.

==PAGE:81==
Figure 8.4: Environmental impact contributions of selected categories in the production of bio-
BDO normalised to the sum of absolute values within each category. FEU = Freshwater. MEU
= marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET =
freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity: cancer. HTNC =
human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
8.1.2
Adipic acid
ADA
The bio-ADA production process is divided into six process stages in accordance to the division
in 6.5: feedstock provision, mechanical pretreatment, chemical pretreatment, fermentation, down-
stream processing and hydrogenation, and other processes. An overview of which processes are
included in which production stage is displayed in Table 6.7. A process contribution analysis is
performed using the above division to show the contribution of the different process groups across
the impact categories. Figure 8.5 shows the outcome of the process contribution analysis.
8.1.2.1
Other processes has signiﬁcant impact across impact categories
It becomes evident that "Other processes" (biomass combustion, WWTP, and inert landﬁll) con-
tributes markedly within eight out of 11 impact categories; being CC, FEU, FRS, TET, FET, MET
74
==PAGE:82==
GTC, and HTNC. This observation states that side streams and how residuals are treated and dis-
posed of has high inﬂuence on the environmental impact of bio-ADA production. In the present
study the unit processes within this production stage are processes, for which data was unknown.
Furthermore, these processes were not the main focus of the report resulting in high uncertainty and
low data quality, hence the processes consists of existing database processes from ecoinvent v3.6
or integrated processes in EASETECH, adding uncertainty to the representativeness and complete-
ness of the chosen processes and thereby bio-ADA production. However, this ﬁnding reveals the
importance of side stream handling of the bio-ADA production, which can be improved in future
assessments.
Figure 8.5: Process contribution divided into process groups feedstock provision, mechanical and
chemical pretreatment, fermentation, puriﬁcation and hydrogenation, and other processes, describ-
ing the impact contribution of the different process steps within bio-ADA production. The contri-
butions are normalised to the sum of absolute values shown in percent. CC = climate change. FEU
= Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial
ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity:
cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
75
==PAGE:83==
8.1.2.2
Feedstock provision is the dominant contributor within MEU and LU
"Feedstock provision" is the dominant process in regards to MEU and LU (approximately 80%
and 95%, respectively) and to a smaller extent in HTNC, where it has a negative contribution
representing a saving. The high MEU impact almost solely (99.8%) originates from the soybean
meal production including the soybean cultivation. A substance contribution analysis, of which the
results can be found in Appendix A.19.4, reveals nitrogen to be the most contributing substance
for "feedstock provision", 96.1% is associated to the release of of nitrogen to groundwater. The
large contribution of "feedstock provision" in LU is not surprising since it covers the cultivation of
soybean meal and wheat straw substituting corn stover used for fodder and bedding purposes. The
negative contribution within HTNC is largely associated to the wheat straw production, accounting
for 88% of the impact. The substance contribution analysis discloses that the HTNC saving is due
to the uptake of zinc by the plant (85.9% of is zinc emitted to agricultural soil is avoided).
As for "other processes", "feedstock provision" can be considered a side stream process and dif-
ferent ways of modelling the feedstock provision can inﬂuence the model results greatly, which is
elaborated in the sensitivity analysis in Section 8.3.1. The size of the contribution within this pro-
cess group discloses the importance of good data quality and validated assumptions within these
processes. Nevertheless, this type of system expansion is driven by the model choices made by
LCA practitioners and no consensus is obtained in literature on this important topic.
8.1.2.3
Energy intensive processes contribute to CC, FRS and ecotoxicity
Within the main production stream "Chemical pretreatment" and "Downstream processing an hy-
drogenation" are the most contributing process groups in CC, FRS, FET, MET and HTC. "Chemical
pretreatment" further contribute to WU. These two process groups both use considerable amounts
of solvent and include the some of the most energy intensive processes. The speciﬁc processes con-
tributing within these stages are further discussed in sections below assessing all unit processes.
8.1.2.4
Fermentation reveals high WU contribution
Figure 8.8, furthermore, reveals the noteworthy contribution of the fermentation process within
WU. The fermentation process only represents a small part of the entire bio-ADA production pro-
cess, however, it solely accounts for 36% of the net WU impact.
8.1.2.5
Biomass combustion is the most contributing process within "Other processes"
Dividing the above mentioned process stages into unit processes reveals the process speciﬁc con-
tributions and Figure 8.6 displays the results. The division of "other processes" discloses that the
biomass combustion process is the most contributing process across the impact categories. The
contribution is mainly due to heat substitution (accounts for more than 90% of the impacts of the
biomass combustion process). The most affected impact categories are CC, FRS, FET and MET.
76
==PAGE:84==
Figure 8.6: Process contribution of the bio-ADA production. The contributions are normalised to
the sum of absolute values and presented in percent. CC = climate change. FEU = Freshwater.
MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET
= freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity: cancer. HTNC =
human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
A substance contribution analysis is made for the marginal heat process to represent the substances
related to heat substitution of the biomass combustion process. A Table of the results are presented
in Appendix A.19.2. The substance contribution analysis found the most contributing substance
within CC to be fossil carbon dioxide to non-urban air or from high stacks accounting for 79.8%
and fossil methane to air to account for 11.7%, which is expected for a combustion process mainly
resulting in the formation of carbon dioxide and water under optimal combustion conditions. As
the marginal heat used in the present study consist of natural gas the most contributing substance
within FRS is the natural gas as a natural resource in ground accounting for 97.4%, which is con-
sidered a saving (negative impact) since the heat substitution avoids the combustion of natural gas.
For the freshwater and marine ecotoxicity the most contributing substance is long term copper ion
77
==PAGE:85==
to ground water, which accounts for 92.0% and 90.9%, respectively. In FEU and HTC the WWTP
shows a substantial contribution, which is included in the "other processes". This ﬁnding is not sur-
prisingly since it is known that the release of nutrients such as nitrogen and phosphate can result in
algae bloom as well as it is known that treating wastewater prevents the release of toxic compounds
such as chromium VI into the environment thereby resulting in a saving in HTC. The substance
contribution analysis disclosed phosphate released to surface water to be the most contributing ac-
counting for 89.4% but also phosphate released to ground water in the long term is accounting for
10.1% within FEU. For HTC avoided long term release of chromium VI to groundwater and sur-
face water account for 72.6% and 24.5 of the savings within this category. The remaining process
"Landﬁll" show very little contribution across all impact categories, however, this process only ac-
counts for process speciﬁc emissions, such as energy consumption, due to lack of data for leachate
generation and other matters.
The "Feedstock provision" is here split into its two constituents straw (feedstock provision) and
soybean meal (feedstock provision). It is seen that the soybean meal production is accountable for
most of the impact of the feedstock provision. And a signiﬁcantly contribution of the soybean meal
is observed in the MEU category, which is due to the wastewater treatment of vegetable oil reﬁnery
and leaching of nitrate to groundwater during the cultivation of soybeans. Furthermore, the straw
production only shows considerable impacts in the HTC and LU categories, where it accounts for
87% and 57%, respectively. The high contribution of the straw production is explained in HTC by
metal taken up by the plant during cultivation.
8.1.2.6
Contribution of lignin ﬁrst is dominated by methanol, nickel and phosphoric acid
The zooming in to unit process contribution reveals lignin ﬁrst and methanol recovery to be the most
contributing processes within "Chemical pretreatment". Methanol recovery discloses environmen-
tal savings within the ecotoxicity categories by preventing the production of methanol. Lignin ﬁrst
contributes considerably to FRS, caused by the use of methanol, which can be determined in Figure
8.7. The impact is, however, almost cancelled out by the recovery of most of the methanol. The
most contributing process is natural gas as natural resource from ground accounting for 95.1%.
From Figure 8.7 it is, moreover, seen that the use of methanol is the cause of most of the impacts
related to the lignin ﬁrst process. The lignin ﬁrst process is the largest contributor to TET, which is
almost solely caused by the use of nickel as catalyst. This contribution can be reduced by further
optimisation of the process. Within TET the most contributing substance is revealed to be long
term copper emissions to air in areas with low population density, it accounts for 73.8% whereas
nickel to air accounts for 14.1%. The last major contributor within the lignin ﬁrst process is phos-
phoric acid, which is used to enhance the performance of the nickel catalyst. It is accountable for
the contribution of lignin ﬁrst to WU. The lignin ﬁrst process is evidently a hot-spot of the bio-
ADA production and regarding CC, FRS, TET, HTC and WU. For further developing the bio-ADA
production attention should be given the optimisation of this process collecting better data and val-
78
==PAGE:86==
idating the many assumptions made. However, the impact of lignin ﬁrst will also be reduced by
reducing the amount of feedstock (corn stover) required since it drives the amount of methanol and
catalyst.
Figure 8.7: Contribution of the lignin ﬁrst process included in the chemical pretreatment of the bio-
ADA production process. The contributions are normalised to the sum of absolute values. CC =
climate change. FEU = Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity.
TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC =
human toxicity: cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
8.1.2.7
Steam contributes largely to climate change and freshwater and marine ecotoxicity
A contribution analysis is made where sub-processes are sorted into groups not related to the unit
processes in which they are used, to visualise the impacts of otherwise hidden sub-processes. Figure
8.8 reveals the results of the contribution analysis. The major contributing groups are found to
be heat substitution (covering biomass combustion), feedstock provision, WWTP as in previous
contribution analysis and will not be discussed further. Besides the above mentioned contributors
are steam, chemicals, and solvents. Steam has a noteworthy contribution to CC, FRS, FET, MET,
HTC, and HTNC, which are the same categories where heat substitution has a negative impact. It
is seen that the contribution of heat substitution almost cancels out the contribution of both steam
and heat used in the bio-ADA production, which is not surprisingly since the production of steam
79
==PAGE:87==
primarily uses heat from natural gas as energy source. It is therefore also not surprising that the most
contributing substance within CC and FRS are fossil carbon dioxide to non-urban air or from high
stacks and natural gas from ground as for the marginal heat substance analysis accounting for 79.8%
and 97.4%, respectively. Results of the substance contribution analysis can be found in Appendix
A.19.3. The negative contribution of steam within FET and MET are due to the prevention of long
term emission of copper ion to groundwater, which accounts for 91.9% and 90.9%, respectively.
The environmental savings observed in FET and MET should not be interpreted as an indicator for
not reducing the steam consumption of the bio-ADA production, but is important to have in mind
with regards the changes to the production process representing different trade-off across impact
categories. Reducing the consumption of steam in the bio-ADA production would decrease the CC
and FRS impact, hence energy optimisation is recommended for the further process development
work.
8.1.2.8
Grouping reveals impact contribution of chemicals, solvents and catalyst
The bio-ADA production process includes the use of the solvents methanol and ethanol. The sol-
vents contribute to CC, FRS, and WU. However, the impacts are almost cancelled out by the re-
covering of solvents. Not recovering the solvents would result in higher impacts within the above
mentioned impact categories.
The grouping reveals the impact of the chemicals used in the bio-ADA production. Substantial
contribution is seen within, HTC, HTNC, and WU. The HTC contribution is highly deﬁned by the
use of phosphoric acid (72%). For HTNC the main contributers are phosphoric acid and sodium hy-
droxide (37% and 34%, respectively), which are used in lignin ﬁrst and fermentation, respectively.
The same picture is drawn when looking at WU, where phoshoric acid and sodium hydroxide ac-
count for 52% and 31%, respectively. To minimise those contribution the lignin ﬁrst process must
be optimised and the organism used in the fermentation process should be able to tolerate lower pH
to minimise the use of sodium hydroxide as neutralising agent.
In addition to the solvent the grouping also reveals the contribution of the chemicals used within
the bio-ADA production and it can be seen, that it has a signiﬁcant contribution to TET, which
is also the case for the catalysts. The catalyst nevertheless are recovered resulting in an partial
out-cancelling of the catalyst contribution. This is however, not the case for the chemicals used.
80
==PAGE:88==

absolute values and shown in percent. CC = climate change. FEU = Freshwater. MEU = marine
eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater
ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity: cancer. HTNC = human toxicity:
non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
8.2
Perturbation analysis: parameter uncertainty
General
The procedure for perturbation analysis is described in Section 6.6. In the following sections the
parameter sensitivity for bio-based BDO and ADA is described. Only parameters considered mod-
erately or highly sensitive within an impact category are described (value normalised to maximum
absolute sensitivity ratio within each category >0.25). Sensitivity ratios for all parameters without
normalisation can be seen in Appendix A.18.
81
==PAGE:89==
8.2.1
1,4-butanediol
BDO
For the bio-BDO production model 11 out of 65 tested parameters are deemed moderately or highly
sensitive within one or more impact categories. These parameters are shown in Figure 8.9.
Figure 8.9: Sensitivity ratios for moderately or highly sensitive parameters within an impact cat-
egory normalised to the maximum absolute sensitivity ratio within the category. CC = climate
change. FEU = Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET
= terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human
toxicity: cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
In all impact categories (except for CC, FRS, HTC and HTNC) the results are most sensitive to-
wards changes in the yield in fermentation. In all these categories an increase in the yield entails a
decrease in potential impacts. This is because a higher yield means less corn stover is required to
provide the sugar necessary to produce 1 kg of bio-BDO. The yields used in the present study are
80% of the theoretical yields of BDO from glucose and xylose, respectively. This means there is
some room for optimisation if the by-products are minimised. Since xylose conversion to bio-BDO
is not currently practised industrially, 80% of theoretical yield may be an optimistic value, however,
it is obtained in bioethanol fermentation with engineered Saccharomyces cerevisiae (Novy et al.,
2015) and Yim et al. (2011) reports "approximately equal efﬁciency of BDO production" from xy-
82
==PAGE:90==
lose substrate in the laboratory. Furthermore, Forte et al. (2016) assumes 80% of the theoretical
yield of BDO from both C5 and C6 sugars in their LCA of BDO production from wheat straw
which obtained data from a Novamont project.
The heat substitution related to lignin combustion is the most sensitive parameter in the CC and FRS
categories, and second most sensitive in the FET and MET categories. In CC and FRS an increase
in heat substitution leads to a decrease in potential impacts, while the opposite is true for FET and
MET. This is in line with the contribution of heat substitution to these categories. As previously
mentioned, the data for handling of the lignin side stream is based on many assumptions such as
how it is dried and what the fuel value is. The sensitivity of the parameter and the high uncertainty
related to assumptions indicates that it is a very important parameter to investigate further in future
research efforts.
In HTC the sodium phosphate used for fermentation media is the most sensitive parameter (release
of chromium VI to ground and surface water during production, see substance contribution for
fermentation in Appendix A.19, Table A.19.7), while for HTNC it is the straw production used to
substitute for corn stover (increase in the amount of straw entails a decrease in HTNC potential
impacts). As previously mentioned, the amount of sodium phosphate used in the fermentation
media is fairly uncertain, since there have been no records of actual concentrations of nutrients
used in the media for direct fermentation to BDO. Hence, the media used in the present model is
the standard M9 media formulation. As this proves to be the most sensitive parameter for HTC for
the whole bio-BDO production, more accurate data is required.
The number of parameters equalling >25% of the most sensitive parameter varies within each
impact category. For FEU there are six parameters equalling >25% of the most sensitive parameter.
This indicates only, that it is not one single parameter in the model that controls the potential impact
in this category. The opposite is true for HTC where there are no parameters equalling >25% of
the most sensitive parameter (sodium phosphate in fermentation media). The sensitivity ratio of
the most sensitive parameter within each impact category can be seen above the bars in Figure 8.9.
It can be seen that the category with the highest maximum sensitivity ratio (SR) is FRS, where an
increase of 10% in the heat substitution leads to a 62.4% decrease in FRS impact. The category
with the lowest maximum SR is FEU, where an increase of 10% in yield leads to a 4% decrease in
FEU impact.
8.2.2
Adipic acid
ADA
15 parameters out of 49 are determined to be moderately or highly sensitive in at least one impact
category. The results obtained in the perturbation analysis are disclosed in Figure 8.10. The highest
sensitivity is seen within MEU, and HTNC, hence, high data quality is very important for the
highly sensitive parameters of these impact categories. The impact category with the least sensitive
parameters is FEU. However, the normalisation method used in the present study does not take the
83
==PAGE:91==
size of the maximum SR into account. The highest max SR is seen in HTC, where a 10% increase
in the yield results in a 67.9% increase in the HTC impact. In contrary the lowest maximum SR is
seen in FEU, where a 10% increase in the COD of the wastewater leads to a 6.9% reduction in the
FEU impact, which coincidentally is also the least sensitive impact category, where only COD and
the yield obtain moderate to high sensitivity.
Figure 8.10: Results of the perturbation analysis of ADA model parameters. The sensitivity ra-
tios are normalised the maximum absolute value within the given impact category. CC = climate
change. FEU = Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET
= terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human
toxicity: cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
The yield shows moderately or highly sensitivity within nine of 11 impact categories, being the
most sensitive parameter within HTC, LU and WU. Changing the yield will result in a reduction in
feedstock, hence, requiring less land and water for irrigation, which are positive changes leading to
environmental savings. However, the increase in the yield will result in higher HTC impacts most
84
==PAGE:92==
likely due to an increased release of metals resulted by less land cultivation. Since the yield has
shown to be very sensitive in many impact categories it is important to gather good quality data to
obtain representative data.
The efﬁciency of the biomass combustion plant revealed high sensitivity within CC, FRS, FET,
MET and HTNC and moderate within TET and HTC, hence data quality also is of high importance.
However, in the present study an integrated process from EASETECH was used and an assumption
of 90% heat efﬁciency was made. To give a more reliable LCIA result this process has to be
improved by gathering data about the heat generation of the biomass assumed to be combusted
in the bio-ADA production. This could be done through laboratory experiments. Furthermore, the
assumption regarding the heat efﬁciency of the plant must be validated. Due to the high contribution
of this process the data must be of high quality to ensure the best results of the LCA.
An interesting observation is made within MEU, showing four different parameters having a sensi-
tivity score close to 1 (SFU of corn stover, SFU of soymeal, straw, and yield fermentation ). Hence
conclusions made within this impact category must take the sensitivity of these parameters into
account that small changes within the data can have high effects on the change in the LCIA results.
Three of the four parameter are included in the feedstock provision process and the discovered
sensitivity discovers this process to be highly sensitive to changes. Feedstock provision showed
high contribution within MEU and LU, and being highly sensitive means that the data used in this
process must be of high quality to obtain the representative LCIA results.
In addition, attention should also be given to the impact categories, where the maximum sensitivity
ratios are high, which are HTC, FRS, FET, MET, and HTNC. Here the increase in the parameters
resulted in large changes in the LCIA results. As an example, the perturbation analysis discloses
that eight of 15 parameters are sensitive within HTNC increasing the uncertainty of the representa-
tiveness of the results within this impact category and data quality within the found parameters are
highly important since relatively small changes in these parameters result in big difference in the
LCIA results.
8.3
Sensitivity scenario analysis
General
An overview of the sensitivity scenarios is shown in Table 6.10, Section 6.6. Some scenarios
are tested for both bio-based BDO and ADA, and the procedure for these scenarios is found in
Appendix A.15. The procedure for scenarios only tested for bio-BDO is found in Appendix A.16,
and for bio-ADA in Appendix A.17. Similarly, the results from sensitivity scenarios tested for
both bio-based BDO and ADA are presented together in Section 8.3.1 (for 2G biomass scenarios),
Section 8.3.2, and Section 8.3.3. Finally, scenarios only applicable to bio-BDO or bio-ADA are
presented in Section 8.18 and 8.3.5, respectively.
85
==PAGE:93==
8.3.1
2G biomass scenarios
General
The sensitivity was tested for a number of scenarios related to the modelling procedure for the 2G
biomass used as feedstock to the bio-BDO and bio-ADA production process and the results are
depicted in Figure 8.11 and 8.12, respectively. It is observed that the ranking of conventional BDO
versus bio-BDO does not change with any of the tested scenarios except for the "mass allocation"
scenario in the HTNC category. Modelling the 2G biomass as half of the burden from corn produc-
tion (50% burden to corn grain and 50% burden to corn stover) increases bio-BDO HTNC impact
enough so that it becomes larger than the HTNC impact for conventional BDO. This is mainly due
to zinc and arsenic leaching to the groundwater in the corn grain production process.
For the ADA production a change in the ranking between conventional ADA and bio-ADA is
observed in CC, when applying the "full burden" modelling approach. When applying the "mass
allocation" scenario the ranking changes drastically in the HTC category. The difference lie within
the release of chromium VI, which is negative using the baseline biomass modelling approach and
positive when applying mass allocation.
It is seen that modelling the 2G biomass as "burden free" will make both the bio-BDO and bio-ADA
less impactful in all impact categories except for HTC and HTNC. The reason why the potential
impact increases in HTC and HTNC is because the soybean meal production has negative impacts
(environmental beneﬁts) in the HTC category, while straw production has negative impacts (envi-
ronmental beneﬁts) in the HTNC category, due to the up take of metals from the soil (zinc, and
chromium VI) during cultivation, and these two processes are disregarded in the "burden free"
biomass scenario. For both bio-BDO and bio-ADA production biomass contributes with most of
the potential LU impacts in the baseline models, the LU impact practically diminishes when mod-
elling the biomass as "burden free". However, the LU is still higher for bio-BDO with the "burden
free" biomass scenario than for conventional BDO. The remaining LU impacts are in the case of
bio-BDO related to enzyme production for enzymatic hydrolysis of the corn stover and sodium
phosphate production for the fermentation media, the latter being the only source of LU impacts of
bio-ADA. For both bio-BDO and bio-ADA production other large reductions are seen in categories
such as CC, MEU. MEU is more than halved for both products. The HTNC impact is on the other
hand more than doubled, which all are considerable changes.
An interesting distinguishing between bio-BDO and bio-ADA is revealed in the HTC impact. Both
impacts are increased by modelling the burden free scenario. However, for bio-ADA the impact of
the "burden free" scenario increases 97% relative to the baseline, which is only 13% for BDO. This
is due to the bio-ADA having more processes resulting in negative HTC impacts and removing
the feedstock substitution as one of them results in the positive and negative impacts to almost
cancel each other down, whereas BDO has only few processes leading to negative HTC impacts
and removing the feedstock substitution does not have a the same drastic effect.
86
==PAGE:94==
Figure 8.11: Results of sensitivity analysis regarding the 2G biomass modelling approach of the
bio-BDO production. The results are internally normalised to the maximum absolute value. CC =
climate change. FEU = Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity.
TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC =
human toxicity: cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
Figure 8.12: Results of sensitivity analysis regarding the 2G biomass modelling approach of the
bio-ADA production. The results are internally normalised to the maximum absolute value. CC =
climate change. FEU = Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity.
TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC =
human toxicity: cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
87
==PAGE:95==
Modelling the 2G biomass with "full burden" increases the potential impacts in all impact categories
except for HTC and HTNC for both bio-BDO and bio-ADA. This naturally shows the opposite
effect of modelling the 2G biomass as "burden free", while the relative changes in the "full burden"
scenario are generally smaller.
In the "mass allocation" scenario increases are observed in all impact categories except for CC, LU
and FEU (no visible change observed in the latter) for both bio-BDO and bio-ADA. In the cate-
gories MEU, TET, FET, HTC, HTNC and WU the allocation by mass scenario has the highest po-
tential impacts of all bio-BDO biomass scenarios investigated, for bio-ADA it is the highest of the
bio-based production scenarios, but not higher than the conventional ADA production. However,
the "mass allocation" scenario and can therefore be considered the most conservative modelling
option. It is generally not common to use this approach when dealing with co-products that have
dissimilar economic and industrial value, like corn grain and corn stover, since it is the corn grain
market that drives the production of corn crops. In this case allocation by economic value can be
used instead, giving less burden to the low value by-product and more burden to the high value
product. However, it can be an issue that the prices of the products ﬂuctuate over time.
8.3.2
Renewable energy mix scenario
General
The sensitivity of the results is tested when changing from a grid mix relying on natural gas to a
renewable grid mix. The renewable electricity relies on equal parts wind, solar and hydro power,
while the renewable heat relies on wood combustion. The impacts of 1 kWh electricity and 1 MJ
heat from each source can be seen in Figure 8.13.
Figure 8.13: Environmental impacts from renewable and natural gas energy. Electricity is nor-
malised to the maximum absolute value within electricity per category, and heat is normalised to
the maximum absolute value within heat per category. CC = climate change. FEU = Freshwater.
MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET
= freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity: cancer. HTNC =
human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
88
==PAGE:96==
The scenarios are shown in Figure 8.14 for production of BDO and in Figure 8.15 for ADA. No
major changes are observed for either BDO or ADA in the FEU, MEU or WU categories.
Both bio-based BDO and ADA production entail signiﬁcant heat substitution due to combustion
of by-product biomass (lignin for BDO and carbohydrates for ADA). Nonetheless, the production
processes differ signiﬁcantly in the fact that bio-BDO production has net production of heat and
steam, while bio-ADA production has a net consumption of heat and steam. This can be observed
in the CC and FRS categories, where the change to renewable energy results in increases for bio-
BDO, while it results in decreases for bio-ADA. For both conventional BDO and ADA the impacts
are signiﬁcantly decreased with the renewable energy mix. This changes the ranking between
conventional and bio-chemical production, meaning that conventional BDO and ADA perform
better than their bio-based counterparts in CC, and additionally in FRS for ADA.
For the conventional production processes all of the toxicity impacts (TET, FET, MET, HTC and
HTNC) increase when changing to a renewable grid. This can be explained by the natural gas heat
having substantial negative contributions (environmental savings) in all of these categories, which
is not the case for the renewable heat (Figure 8.13). As previously mentioned the savings in toxic
impacts for the natural gas heat are related to the removal of toxic metals from groundwater and
air (see substance contribution for heat production in Appendix A.19, Table A.19.1). This does
not mean that the production of heat from natural gas removes these metals from the environment,
but that electricity is co-produced with the heat, which substitutes the electricity market. For the
bio-based BDO and ADA the opposite pattern is observed; the toxicity impacts decrease when
changing to a renewable energy mix (except for HTC for bio-ADA). This is explained by the large
heat substitution from biomass combustion carrying less impact with the renewable grid mix.
The LU impact of renewable electricity and heat is considerably higher than for natural gas, which
has close to zero LU impact (Figure 8.13). The LU impact of renewable electricity comes mainly
from solar energy production, while wind and hydro power also consumes land. For renewable
heat it is the production of wood that causes the LU impact. This is observed for conventional
BDO and ADA, where the LU impacts increase signiﬁcantly with a change to renewable energy.
Oppositely, for bio-based BDO and ADA, the change to renewable energy entails a small reduction
in LU impacts, which is due to the heat substitution from biomass combustion. The reason why
the change is small is because the LU impacts in bio-based BDO and ADA production is already
relatively high due to cultivation of biomass used as feedstock.
Finally, it can be concluded that the change from a natural gas to renewable energy mix results
in large climate change reductions for the conventional chemicals, while the reduction is minor
for bio-ADA, and an increase is observed for BDO. This is due to substantial heat substitution
from biomass combustion. The heat substitution is the cause of most observed patterns for bio-
based chemicals when switching to renewable energy, and it should be taken into account that the
uncertainty of the biomass combustion processes are high.
89
==PAGE:97==
Figure 8.14: Results of sensitivity analysis using a renewable energy mix in the BDO production.
The results are internally normalised to the maximum absolute value. CC = climate change. FEU
= Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial
ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity:
cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
Figure 8.15: Results of sensitivity analysis using a renewable energy mix in the ADA production.
The results are internally normalised to the maximum absolute value. CC = climate change. FEU
= Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial
ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity:
cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
90
==PAGE:98==
8.3.3
LCIA method scenario
General
To assess the robustness of the results a sensitivity scenario is made using two different LCIA
methods (ReCiPe2016 and ILCD2017). The obtained results for both BDO and ADA are displayed
in Figure 8.16 and 8.17, respectively.
It is seen that for the CC category the impacts change very little when using ILCD2017 instead
of ReCiPe2016 as LCIA method. This is in line with the CC category being known as one of
the most robust impact categories across LCIA methods. The difference becomes smaller, when
comparing the characterised results leading to the conclusion, that the differences of the normalised
results to some extent are explained by the use of different normalisation factors. ReCiPe2016
uses the appertaining normalisation factors updated in 2019 (RIVM, 2020) and ILCD2017 uses
normalisation factors recommended by the European Commission’s joint research centre published
in 2017 (Sala et al., 2017).
Figure 8.16: Results of the model robustness assessment of the BDO production. The LCIA meth-
ods assessed are ReCiPe 2016, which is the one chosen for the present study and ILCD2017. ILCD
is comprised of several different models and uses IPCC2013 for climate change, CML2001 for FRS
and USEtox for FET, HTC, and HTNC. CC = climate change. FEU = Freshwater. MEU = marine
eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater
ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity: cancer. HTNC = human toxicity:
non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
91
==PAGE:99==
Figure 8.17: Results of the model robustness assessment of the ADA production. The LCIA meth-
ods assessed are ReCiPe 2016, which is the one chosen for the current study and ILCD2017. ILCD
is comprised of several different models and uses IPCC2013 for climate change, CML2001 for FRS
and USEtox for FET, HTC, and HTNC. CC = climate change. FEU = Freshwater. MEU = marine
eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater
ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity: cancer. HTNC = human toxicity:
non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
For the bio-BDO production there is a 26% increase in the FRS impact, while for the conventional
BDO there is a 25% decrease in this category. The reason why the change is not proportional
for the bio-BDO and conventional BDO production, can partly be explained by the natural gas
heat substitution in the bio-BDO production. Because ILCD 2017 assigns lower FRS impact to
the extraction of natural gas than ReCiPe 2016 the negative impact from heat substitution to this
category becomes less dominating leading to an increase. Natural gas heat is not substituted in
the conventional BDO. Nonetheless, the impacts are within the same order of magnitude for both
conventional and bio-BDO when changing the LCIA method.
For the bio-ADA production a 21% and 30% decrease is observed in the FRS category. The reason
for the decrease rather than increase in this category compared to the observations of bio-BDO can
be explained by the fact, that the bio-BDO production produces more heat than it consumes, which
is not the case of the bio-ADA production. As for bio-BDO the results of the bio-ADA are in the
same magnitude when changing the LCIA method.
For the FET category the impact decreases for conventional and bio-BDO and bio-ADA production
(24-25% for BDO and 21-30% for ADA) when changing the LCIA method.
For HTC the results of the two cases (BDO and ADA) are very different. Here the ILCD method
92
==PAGE:100==
leads to a decrease in impact, whereas the impact increases using the ILCD method on the ADA
production. The increase in the ADA production might partly be explained by the different re-
sults obtained from the wastewater treatment, which changes from -0.1 to -0.04 PE, furthermore
the electricity used is evaluated differently within the two methods where ReCiPe2016 results in
negative HTC impacts and ILCD in positive impacts. For BDO the decrease is caused by the use
of sodium phosphate used in the fermentation, which releases chromium VI to groundwater, which
is estimated differently within the two ILCD method.
For HTNC the impacts change drastically when using ILCD 2017 instead of ReCiPe 2016. For
conventional BDO the HTNC impact increases drastically, while for bio-BDO the HTNC impact
decreases drastically. In both cases the change of LCIA method changes the order of magnitude of
the results. The decrease in HTNC impact for bio-BDO production can be partially explained by
the ILCD 2017 method assigning more negative HTNC impact for zinc uptake from agricultural
soil than ReCiPe 2016 (in the straw production process).
In conclusion, the human toxicity impacts (cancer and non-cancer) are sensitive to the change
in LCIA method. For the CC impact the results seem rather robust since they change less than
10% when changing the LCIA method. For FRS and FET visible changes in the impacts are
observed in both cases. With regards to FET and the human toxicity categories high variation
is seen when changing the LCIA method, which therefore are often excluded from LCA studies
(Rosenbaum et al., 2018). The ILCD method uses the USEtox model, which is considered to be
the best available model for human toxicity, hence any conclusions regarding human toxicity in the
current study should be well considered taken the above into account (Rosenbaum et al., 2018).
Aymard and Botta-Genoulaz (2017) found that using different LCIA and normalisation factors can
lead to substantial different results within human toxicity, which is also observed in the present
study. Making conclusion based on these impact categories should therefore be made with caution
and if those impact categories are the focus of a given study thorough consideration should be given
to the use of LCIA method used for these categories. In none of the impact categories the ranking
between conventional and bio-BDO production changes when changing LCIA model.
8.3.4
Other sensitivity scenarios for 1,4-butanediol
BDO
Additional sensitivity scenarios are tested for the bio-BDO production aside from the 2G biomass,
renewable energy and LCIA method scenarios. These scenarios are shown in Figure 8.18. None
of the tested scenarios change the ranking between conventional and bio-BDO production in any
impact categories.
8.3.4.1
Using 1G corn starch as feedstock increases FRS drastically, yet decreases LU
The sensitivity of the results when changing the biomass feedstock from 2G (corn stover) to 1G
(corn starch) is tested, and it is observed that the changes in overall bio-BDO performance are
93
==PAGE:101==
considerable in all impact categories except for WU (absolute changes ranging from 18% in HTC
to 643% in FRS). The drastic increase in FRS is partly explained by the low FRS impact in the
baseline scenario, and is due to the use of natural gas heat in corn starch production. The decrease
in LU impact is due to reduced requirements of 1G biomass compared to 2G biomass.
Figure 8.18: Other sensitivity scenarios deﬁned for bio-based 1,4-butanediol. CC = climate change.
FEU = Freshwater. MEU = marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial
ecotoxicity. FET = freshwater ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity:
cancer. HTNC = human toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
When using 1G biomass as feedstock for bio-BDO production the CC impact increases with 54%
compared to the baseline. In a study by Patel et al. (2018) the CC impact from production of
polybutylene succinate from succinic acid and BDO is examined. The CC impact of bio-BDO from
1G biomass in Patel et al. (2018) is obtained through personal communication with Novamont (that
owns the only current bio-BDO production plant) (see Table 8.1). In Patel et al. (2018) the CC
impact of bio-BDO from 2G biomass is calculated by subtracting the CC impacts of 1G sugar from
the total CC impact of 1G bio-BDO obtained from Novamont, and then adding the CC impact of
2G sugar they obtained. The 2G sugar in Patel et al. (2018) is obtained from steam explosion of
corn stover. The obtained C5 sugars are used for biogas production and the lignin is combusted.
As is seen in Table 8.1 the impacts of BDO production from 1G and 2G biomass are considerably
lower in Patel et al. (2018) than in the present study.

It should be noted that Novamont’s procedures to calculate the CC impact for their production of
1G bio-BDO are undisclosed and their production process is proprietary. Nonetheless, it is evident
that when subtracting the CC impact of 1G sugar from the CC impact of their bio-BDO production,
there are no CC impacts related to the fermentation and downstream processing. This indicates
that the CC impact for bio-BDO production by Novamont is underestimated, which can explain the
difference between this study and that of Patel et al. (2018).
8.3.4.2
Only converting glucose increases all impacts except for FRS and HTNC
The sensitivity of the results when only converting glucose in the biomass hydrolysate and not xy-
lose to BDO is tested, since the conversion of xylose to BDO is surrounded by large uncertainty.
The scenario entails an increase of 64% in the amount of 2G biomass required, which results in vis-
ible increases in CC, FEU, MEU and LU impacts in the range 28-62%, while the FRS and HTNC
decreased with 99.8% and 12%, respectively. HTNC decreased due the 2G biomass carrying nega-
tive impacts in this category, while the decrease in FRS impact is explained by the increased amount
of side stream lignin combustion suppressing natural gas heat when processing more biomass. It is
noted that this scenario does not take into account an alternative use of the produced xylose, and the
probability that another pretreatment method may have been employed to produce only glucose.
8.3.4.3
Using database process for upstream WWT increases the LCI completeness
The sensitivity of the results when using the same data source (ecoinvent v3.6 database process) for
upstream and downstream wastewater treatment in the bio-BDO production is tested. The use of
data from Davis et al. (2015) for upstream wastewater treatment in the baseline is disregarded. The
two datasets show very different impacts - the upstream WWT mainly has visible positive impacts
in the CC category due to energy use and direct emissions to air as well as negative impacts in CC
and FRS related to electricity substitution from biogas incineration, while the downstream WWT
mainly has positive impacts in freshwater eutrophication, and negative impacts in HTC. The non-
existent impacts of the upstream WWT in the FEU category is an indication of a lower completeness
95
==PAGE:103==
of the upstream WWT data from Davis et al. (2015), which does not include concentration of
pollutants in the treated wastewater released to the environment.
These patterns were also observed in the scenario, where the CC and FRS impacts increased by
7% and 127%, respectively due to less electricity use and no electricity substitution from biogas
incineration (only present in the Davis et al. (2015)). The FEU impacts also increased by 47% while
HTNC impacts decreased with 30%. The scenario highlights the high inﬂuence of wastewater
treatment on the FEU impact category. The composition of treated wastewater released to the
environment could preferably be added to the upstream WWT dataset from (Davis et al., 2015) to
increase the completeness. Additionally, the downstream WWT dataset from ecoinvent v.3.6 for
treatment of wastewater from vegetable oil reﬁnery is not speciﬁc for bio-BDO production, and it
would have been preferred to have a speciﬁc dataset for treatment of BDO wastewater rather than
scaling the database process based on the COD content in the two wastewaters.
8.3.4.4
No drying of biomass shows large CC and FRS reductions, but may not be viable option
The sensitivity of the results when not drying the corn stover after harvest and before transport to
the bio-BDO production facility is tested because the the drying electricity contributes with 36%
of the total electricity demand of the bio-BDO production. This increases the transported mass
by 30%. As the transport carries less CC and FRS impacts than the electricity from natural gas
it is observed that with no drying the CC impact decreases with 28% while the FRS decreases
with 167% becoming negative. There are no major effects in other impact categories. As transport
typically carries a large part of expenses in industrial processes, there may be a trade-off between
the obtained environmental saving in CC and FRS and the potential increase in costs. Nonetheless,
electricity costs are also reduced, hence, the increased costs of transport should be weighted against
the decreased costs of electricity.
8.3.5
Other sensitivity scenarios for adipic acid
ADA
Except the 2G biomass modelling, renewable energy mix, and LCIA method sensitivity scenario’s,
which are made for both BDO and ADA, separate scenarios are made simulating a further op-
timised bio-ADA production. The results of the different scenario’s relative to the conventional
ADA and baseline bio-ADA models are presented in Figure 8.19. The overall picture is that the
"Increased titer" scenario does not change the ranking between the conventional ADA and bio-
ADA production. For the "Full lignin oil conversion" scenario the ranking is changed in CC and
FRS and for "Glucose substitution" scenario the ranking is changed in CC and MEU. The reasons
will be discussed in the following.
96
==PAGE:104==
Figure 8.19: Sensitivity results of process optimisation processes (Titer 150 g/L and Further
metabolic engineering) and a more realistic modelling of the residual biomass generated in the
chemical pretreatment process (Glucose substitution). The results are internally normalised to the
maximum absolute value within each category.CC = climate change. FEU = Freshwater. MEU =
marine eutrophication. FRS = fossil resource scarcity. TET = terrestrial ecotoxicity. FET = fresh-
water ecotoxicity. MET = marine ecotoxicity. HTC = human toxicity: cancer. HTNC = human
toxicity: non-cancer. LU = land use. WU = water use.
8.3.5.1
Increasing the titer shows signiﬁcant reduction in FEU and HTC
Regarding the "increased titer" scenario Figure 8.19 shows noteworthy changes within FEU and
HTC compared to the baseline bio-ADA production. Within the remaining impact categories the
"increased titer" follows the bio-ADA baseline but having slightly lower impacts. The contribution
analysis in Section 8.1.2.8 revealed the most contributing substance ﬂows and processes showing
that the WWTP largely is contributing to FEU and HTC. Increasing the ﬁnal titer will lead to a
reduction in water used in the fermentation process which will affect the amount of wastewater to
be treated, hence lowering the contribution of this process and thus the impact within FEU. For the
HTC category the reduction in the WWTP contribution reduces the environmental savings from
this process, furthermore, increasing the titer leads to a reduction in the use of nutrients included
in the chemicals group in Section 8.1.2.8. The nutrients account for 27% of the chemical used
in the entire production process. Another reduction is made in the energy consumption of the
fermentation process since less water has to be heated reducing the environmental saving within
HTC from energy consumption, which is seen in Section 8.3.2. Increasing the titer results in a
slightly better performance, however, the set up of the model was not optimal for assessing the titer
as mentioned in Appendix A.17.
97
==PAGE:105==
8.3.5.2
Full lignin oil conversion reveals increase in CC and FRS
In contrast to the titer scenario, which inﬂuences the downstream puriﬁcation process the "full
lignin oil conversion" scenario inﬂuences the feedstock required and thereby the pretreatment pro-
cesses. Figure 8.19 discloses reductions within nine of 11 impact categories compared to the base-
line bio-ADA scenario. The main consequence of this scenario is the reduction of feedstock re-
quired, which is reduced by approximately 60%, thus reducing the impact of feedstock provision,
which is the dominating contributor to MEU and LU and a considerable contributor to HTNC. The
reduction in feedstock requirements reduces MEU and LU by 50% and 60% respectively.
Moreover the "full lignin oil conversion" reduced the amount of methanol used in the chemical
pretreatment and thereby the energy consumption of this process. The lignin ﬁrst process has high
contributions to CC, FRS and TET, HTC, and WU. TET, HTC and WU are, thus reduced drastically,
which is revealed in Figure 8.19. This reduction does, however, not show within CC and FRS,
which has increased compared to the baseline scenario. This might seem surprising, nevertheless,
the reduction in feedstock results in less heat substitution from the biomass combustion, since less
biomass is available for combustion and the contribution analysis revealed heat substitution has a
large negative impact within CC and FRS disclosing the impacts of the energy and solvent intense
processes such as the catalytic hydrogenation process.
A drastic reduction of approximately 60% relative to the baseline is observed in FEU which can
be explained by the reduction of COD in the wastewater reducing the impact of the WWTP. The
observed decrease in FET and MET is a result of less heat substitution and less energy required
for the methanol recovery, which have the opposite effect since one generates heat and the other
consumes heat.
8.3.5.3
Replacing biomass combustion with glucose substitution changes the results drastically
Replacing the biomass combustion with glucose substitution to simulate more realistic use of the
residual biomass of the chemical pretreatment leads to signiﬁcantly different results. This scenario
discloses the highest impacts within CC and FRS, which is not surprisingly in the light of the
ﬁndings of heat substitution resulting in large environmental savings in CC and FRS. By replac-
ing the combustion plant with glucose substitution these beneﬁts are removed and replaced by a
beneﬁt being six times lower. However, within the remaining nine impact categories the impacts
of the glucose substitution scenario are reduced in comparison to the baseline scenario exposing
environmental savings within MEU, the ecotoxicity and human toxicity categories. The reductions
within FET and MET can be explained by the absence of heat substitution, representing the most
contributing part in these categories in the baseline scenario, whereas the glucose substitution re-
veals environmental savings in the FET and MET categories. MEU is dominated by the impact of
feedstock provision, which includes the cultivation of land for soybean meal and straw production.
The glucose production also requires the cultivation of land and since this process is a substitution
98
==PAGE:106==
process the impacts of this category are seen as an environmental saving. The change within HTC
can partly be explained by the contribution of infrastructure of glucose production from corn starch.
Infrastructure has been included for database processes, but the bio-ADA plant is not included in
the present study. This is an inconsistency of the modelling of the present study, but was justiﬁed
by Frischknecht et al. (2007). However, in this particular case the avoided infrastructure is domi-
nating this category of the "glucose substitution" scenario. This also applies for HTNC. The WU
is more than halved replacing the biomass combustion with glucose substitution. This is related to
the avoided corn cultivation of the glucose production.
The "glucose substitution" scenario is a good example visualising trade-offs. It only a carbon
footprint is made the results indicate, that a shift away from the conventional ADA production
is not recommended using the bio-ADA production including glucose substitution. However, the
environmental saving in MEU, ecotoxicity, human toxicity, and LU categories are taken out of the
equation, which in principle might compensate the increase in CC and FRS.
8.4
Qualitative uncertainty analysis
General
LCI data quality, assumptions and related uncertainties have been presented in Section 6 for each
life cycle stage for bio-BDO and bio-ADA. Based on the contribution analysis and sensitivity of
the results the major sources of uncertainty will be discussed in the following sections. The ma-
jor sources of uncertainty for bio-BDO and bio-ADA are visualised in Figure 8.20, which shows
that uncertainties related to feedstock provision are identical for bio-BDO and bio-ADA, while
remaining sources of uncertainty differ between the two.

Figure 8.20: Major sources of uncertainty for bio-BDO and bio-ADA based on data sources, con-
