99
==PAGE:107==
8.4.1
Feedstock provision
General
The feedstock provision is based on the same data and has been modelled identically in bio-BDO
and bio-ADA production (See Section 6.1). The only difference among the two models is the
amount of feedstock required for production of 1 kg chemical (≈7 kg corn stover for 1 kg BDO
and ≈32 kg corn stover for 1 kg ADA). In the contribution analyses for bio-BDO and bio-ADA
the feedstock provision was found considerably contributing to HTNC (negatively) and LU for the
straw production process, and to CC, FEU, MEU and LU for the soybean meal production process.
Furthermore, in the perturbation analyses it was found that a number of parameters related to the
amount of straw and soybean meal used to substitute corn stover were sensitive in both bio-BDO
production and bio-ADA production (SFU of corn stover, SFU of soymeal, percentage of corn
stover substituted by straw and percentage of corn stover substituted by soymeal). In the sensitivity
scenario where different approaches to modelling 2G biomass was investigated, it was found that
the choice of model procedure could drastically change the results, and even the ranking between
conventional and bio-production. The procedure used for modelling the feedstock is based on the
thesis that - even though corn stover is a by-product of corn grain production - corn stover cannot
be considered burden free. This is because the corn stover has already found areas of utilisation
in agricultural practice. It was therefore decided to replace these areas of utilisation with other
products in the model. This type of system expansion is a common practice in consequential LCA
modelling, however not always consistently used in literature LCAs. The uncertainty related to the
data and assumptions combined with the high contribution of feedstock provision to the results,
makes this process an important point of interest to improve in further research. Decreasing the
uncertainty in the feedstock provision will reduce the overall uncertainty of the results markedly.
The data obtained from Schmer et al. (2017) was simpliﬁed in the present study due to limited
resources, but the uncertainty originating from these simpliﬁcations could be avoided by using the
detail in the data by Schmer et al. (2017).
The variability in utilisation of corn residues is a challenge for the consistent life cycle assessment
of corn stover as bio-energy or chemical feedstock. The variability is hardly reduced, but can be
accounted for by including different feedstock scenarios.
8.4.2
1,4-butanediol
BDO
The entire bio-BDO production process from corn stover harvest to bio-BDO product at facility
gate is adapted from literature sources. The pretreatment of biomass to obtain sugar water for
BDO production was adapted from a study utilising the sugars for hydrocarbon production (Davis
et al., 2015). The fermentation process data was collected from multiple literature sources and
put together based on rough mass balance calculations. For both pretreatment and downstream
processing of fermentation broth the data sources focused on technoeconomic analyses rather than
environmental assessment. In conclusion, the data used to evaluate the environmental impacts of
bio-BDO production in the present study is surrounded by large uncertainty.
100
==PAGE:108==
Four major sources of uncertainty in the results (aside from the feedstock provision) are identiﬁed
to be (1) side stream lignin handling, (2) wastewater treatment, (3) fermentation, and (4) enzymatic
hydrolysis. These will be discussed in the following.
8.4.2.1
Side stream lignin handling
The side stream lignin handling consisting of combustion to produce heat which substitutes the
natural gas heat grid proved to be the most contributing process in the CC, FRS, FET and MET
categories (See Section 8.1.1). Furthermore, the heat substitution proved to be the most sensitive
parameter for CC and FRS categories, and second most sensitive for FET and MET categories in
perturbation analysis (See Section 8.2.1). The sensitivity scenario using combustion of wood chips
for heat instead of natural gas also showed that changing the heat source had severe inﬂuence on
the results (See Section 8.3.2). The high substitution of natural gas heat in the bio-BDO baseline
made the change to a renewable heat source look like a disadvantage in some categories (i.e. 178%
increase in FRS). The modelling of side stream lignin handling contains several rough assumptions
including the drying process, the fuel value of lignin, and the efﬁciency of the heat combustion.
The high contribution of heat substitution to some impact categories, and the sensitivity of the
results to changes in the modelling procedures combined with the high uncertainty makes this an
area of further reﬁnement in data collection and model assumptions. The uncertainty in the results
could be reduced by examining the true fuel value of lignin from chemical pretreatment of corn
stover. Additionally, it should be investigated whether or not it is preferable to dry the lignin before
combustion, and if it is, what the energy requirements are. Furthermore, any auxiliary fuels used
in the start-up of a combustion process are disregarded in the present study. Finally, the efﬁciency
of an industrial scale lignin combustion plant should be considered to reduce the uncertainty in the
results.
8.4.2.2
WWT upstream and downstream
The WWT upstream proved to have relatively little contribution to the overall impacts of bio-BDO
production, while the WWT downstream proved to be the most contributing process in FEU (See
Section 8.1.1. None of the parameters related to WWT upstream or downstream were sensitive in
perturbation analysis (See Section 8.2.1). Nonetheless, the choice of using two different WWT sys-
tems for upstream and downstream production was challenged in the sensitivity scenario, where all
the wastewater from the bio-BDO production was handled using the same procedure as downstream
WWT in the bio-BDO baseline (ecoinvent v3.6 process "treatment of wastewater from vegetable
oil reﬁnery, GLO" scaled to estimated COD of wastewater). This increased the FEU, since the
upstream WWT adapted from Davis et al. (2015) was lacking information about pollutants in re-
leased treated wastewater, which was included in the ecoinvent process. Furthermore, the CC and
FRS impacts also increased due to exclusion of the electricity substitution from biogas engine in
the upstream WWT adapted from Davis et al. (2015). Even though the WWT data obtained from
101
==PAGE:109==
Davis et al. (2015) is based on Aspen simulations of corn stover pretreatment and therefore more
speciﬁc to the present study than the ecoinvent process it lacks completeness about pollutants in
released treated wastewater. To obtain larger conﬁdence in the results, the wastewater treatment
data quality should be increased.
8.4.2.3
Fermentation
The fermentation nutrients proved to account for the largest contribution to HTC impact in the con-
tribution analysis, largely driven by sodium phosphate production. Additionally, in the sensitivity
analysis it was found that sodium phosphate production was the only highly sensitive parameter in
the HTC category.
The media composition for the present study is surrounded by large uncertainty, as no sources in
literature disclose a media assembled speciﬁcally for Genomatica’s BDO producing E. coli strain
(to the best knowledge of the author). The combination of uncertainty and high contribution of this
parameter to the HTC impact, makes it important to obtain better data for media composition to
increase the conﬁdence in especially the HTC results.
Furthermore, the yield used in fermentation was found to be the overall most sensitive parameter
for bio-BDO production, since an increase in yield would entail decreases in all upstream processes
of the fermentation process. Some disagreement in literature about the current state of technology
is observed, and it is deemed highly important whether the BDO production uses only glucose
or both glucose and xylose as substrates to the fermentation process. To get to any conclusions
whether bio-BDO or conventional BDO is preferred from an environmental perspective it is crucial
to decrease the uncertainty originating from the fermentation process parameters.
8.4.2.4
Enzymatic hydrolysis
The enzymatic hydrolysis of corn stover was found to be contributing considerably to the MEU,
TET, HTNC and WU impacts for bio-BDO production. In line with this, the cellulase used in
enzymatic hydrolysis of corn stover was a moderately or highly sensitive parameter in the same
categories. The data source for corn stover pretreatment in the present study is Davis et al. (2015),
which considered a cellulase loading of 10 mg/g cellulose and a conversion efﬁciency of 90% of
cellulose to glucose. For the present study this was deemed too optimistic, as the state of technology
at the time of the study was considered double the amount. Therefore, the amount of cellulase used
in enzymatic hydrolysis is surrounded by some uncertainty, which has a high inﬂuence on the
uncertainty of the results. Further research into the enzymatic hydrolysis of corn stover should be
included in future evaluation of bio-BDO production from corn stover to obtain higher conﬁdence
in the results. Furthermore, the chemical loadings in other pretreatment steps adapted from Davis
et al. (2015) were not changed to more conservative estimates, and there is a risk that if more
conservative estimates were adapted the other pretreatment steps would also be highlighted in the
contribution analysis.
102
==PAGE:110==
8.4.3
Adipic acid
ADA
A general challenge of the assembly of the bio-ADA process was the lack of sufﬁcient data. The
process does not exist in commercial scale, hence full scale data does not exist. Nonetheless,
a lot of research is made within this ﬁeld regarding the fermentation process and some kind of
consensus have been found within the fermentation process and the appertaining puriﬁcation and
hydrogenation steps in literature (van Duuren et al., 2011b, 2020; Corona et al., 2018b; Vardon
et al., 2015, 2016; Salvachúa et al., 2018). Many processes have thereby been estimated based
on either physico-chemical properties, expert judgements, by adapting information from similar
processes, and by guesstimating. One of the articles have estimated input values using Aspen
simulating full scale processes. The same challenge was seen within the pretreatment methods
generation high quality lignin oil. The overall uncertainty of the model and thereby the LCIA
results are deemed to be high.
The above performed contribution analysis revealed four highly contributing processes (1) feed-
stock provision, (2) biomass combustion, (3) WWTP, and (4) lignin ﬁrst, and (5) methanol and
catalyst recovery. The uncertainty of the above processes and their inﬂuence on the uncertainty of
the LCIA results will be discussed in the following.
8.4.3.1
Side stream carbohydrate handling
Besides being one of the most contributing process within several impact categories (see Section
8.1.2). The perturbation revealed high sensitivity towards the heat efﬁciency of the biomass com-
bustion plant being highly sensitive in ﬁve impact categories (see Section 8.2.2). Thus the uncer-
tainty of this process will highly determine the uncertainty of the model. Due to lack of data the
uncertainty of this process is deemed high. It is based on an integrated process in EASETECH,
As mentioned in Section 6.5 combusting the residual solids of the chemical pretreatment is not the
most realistic scenario, since it has potential to be used for 2G glucose production. Before putting
effort in improving the biomass combustion process the most realistic treatment or processing of
the residuals should be examined. However, if the process is assumed to include the biomass
combustion the following should be taking into account.
The assumption regarding the heat efﬁciency of the combustion plant turned out to have high inﬂu-
ence on the results, thus improved data collection and validation of the assumption should be made
to increase the reliability of the model as well as decreasing the appertaining uncertainty. Another
assumption made within this process is related to the heating value of the residual biomass, which
is combusted. The heating value has inﬂuence on the energy content of the biomass, hence an over-
estimated heating value will result in overestimated heat production and heat substitution resulting
in the underestimation of CC, FRS and overestimation of FET and MET. The underestimation of
the heating value will thus result in the reverse observations. In addition carbon dioxide emissions
from biomass are considered to have zero climate change impact, arguing that the carbon released
103
==PAGE:111==
will be taken up in new biomass relatively fast, however, this argumentation can be challenged,
since some biomass such as trees, still has relatively long regeneration time, furthermore some
of the biomass results in indirect land use change, which often is neglected due to difﬁculties in
accounting those impacts.
8.4.3.2
WWT whole plant
The WWTP in the bio-ADA production was found to be the main contributor within FEU and have
considerable contribution within HTC (see Section 8.1.2). The perturbation analysis found the
COD and the water addition within this process to be sensitive, where the COD is highly sensitive
to the above mentioned impact categories (see Section 8.2.2). The uncertainty of theses processes
are hence deemed to dictate the uncertainty of the results within the above impact categories.
The water added in the WWT of the bio-ADA production is added based on the COD of the waste
stream entering the WWTP, which is found to be extremely high. This can be explained by the
use of too little water within the bio-ADA process, by neglecting process water used for cleaning
the plant. Thus, to reduce the COD water is added to the level of the COD of the wastewater
stream entering the ecoinvent v3.6 process "treatment of wastewater from vegetable oil reﬁnery,
GLO". This processes is used since no data was found on the use of WWTP since the bio-ADA
production process does not exist in commercial scale, but at the moment is still on laboratory and
small pilot plant scale, which do not report data on wastewater treatment since the primary focus
is in developing the biochemical production route. However, the WWTP used in the present study
was chosen, since it was the WWTP handling wastewater with high COD. Having better regarding
the water use would might affect the choice of WWTP. The optimal situation would be to have data
regarding the WWTP associated to the bio-ADA production as well as accurate data for the total
water use of the bio-ADA production, which would increase the reliability of the model and the
results within FEU and HTC. In principle the water added in this step could have been spread out on
the different steps included in the bio-ADA production. To omit the step of adding big amount of
water in one step better data should be collected, where the water use including water for cleaning.
This would also affect the COD of the wastewater stream. The COD was estimated by calculating
the theoretical oxygen demand (TOD) of each organic compound entering the WWTP. The actual
COD might therefor be even higher. This estimation should be reevaluated and one could determine
the COD of the wastewater stream through laboratory experiments.
8.4.3.3
Lignin ﬁrst
The contribution analysis in Section 8.1.2 found the lignin ﬁrst process to contribute noteworthy
to CC, FRS, TET, HTC and WU. Furthermore, the use of phosphoric acid in the lignin ﬁrst pro-
cess showed to be moderately sensitive within HTC, HTNC and WU (see Section 8.2.2). The
uncertainties of this process, thus inﬂuences the uncertainty of the LCIA results.
The lignin ﬁrst process is one of the most uncertain processes within the bio-ADA production since
104
==PAGE:112==
it is based on a small scale "proof of concept" experiment by Anderson et al. (2016a). As described
in Section A.22 many rough assumptions are made regarding the amount of substances used and
the energy consumption.
Small scale experimental data do not assess the energy consumption nor the further treatment of
residual products. The energy demand is hence estimated based on physico-chemical properties
only taken the actual heating into account and not the energy required for keeping the desired
temperature for a given time. Furthermore, the use of hydrogen was adapted from Corona et al.
(2018b), since both are hydrogenation processes. All these relative rough assumptions lead to a
deemed high uncertainty of the process. Hence the amount of phosphoric acid used to enhance
the performance of the catalyst, which was found to be the most contributing part regarding HTC
and WU impacts of the lignin ﬁrst process is uncertain. Furthermore, the amount of phosphoric
acid turned out to be a sensitive parameter within the same impact categories, hence, collecting
better data or making better estimates as well as validating the assumptions is of great importance
to enhance reliability of both the lignin ﬁrst process as well as the LCIA results within HTC, and
WU. Lignin ﬁrst has its highest contribution within TET, which is mainly due to the nickel catalyst,
however, the catalyst is as mentioned based on rough assumptions. In general it is recommended
to try to ﬁnd or to generate better data, which can then improve the model and thereby enhance the
reliability of the results.
8.4.3.4
Methanol and catalyst recovery
Methanol and catalyst recovery is the ﬁfth process found to have considerable contribution within
several impact categories, especially, the ecotoxicity categories (see Section 8.1.2). The TET im-
pact is highly determined by the recovery of catalyst, whereas the FET and MET impact is related
to the energy consumption of recovering methanol. The perturbation analysis further found the
energy consumption of the methanol recovery to be moderately sensitive within several impact
categories as well as the recovery rate for the methanol recovery (see Section 8.2.2).
As described in Section 6.5 the methanol and catalyst recycling is primarily based on rough esti-
mations. To eliminate or reduce the uncertainty of a literature search should be made to increase
the reliability. Perhaps the methanol could be recycled by ﬁltration instead of distillation, which
would most likely change the energy consumption considerably. However, more insight on the
composition of the liquid after the lignin ﬁrst process is required as well as how the lignin oil is
separated from the methanol if it is done by a liquid-liquid of solid liquid extraction. To determine
this a chemical engineer is needed or the performing of experiments could be made.
Regarding the catalyst the degree of degradation is not known, which controls the amount of cat-
alyst can be recovered. Research is made on how heterogeneous metal catalyst can be recovered
(Arai and Zhao, 2015). The assumption of 90% recovery might be very high regarding the current
available technology.
105
==PAGE:113==
9
Conclusions and recommendations
General
A major challenge of the compiled study is the data collection. The chemical industry is exposed to
strong competition, hence companies are not willing to reveal information on their production. Fur-
thermore, the production of bio-chemicals is an emerging ﬁeld limiting the knowledge and amount
of available large scale data. This has resulted in low data quality in the constructed models of the
present study, which again has led to the generation of uncertain results complicating the drawing
of clear conclusions on the performance of bio-BDO and bio-ADA relative to their conventional
counterparts. However, the obtained results are able to give indications of trade-offs and hot-spots
of the bio-based production processes.
The motivation for the transition towards bio-based chemical production is mainly focusing on the
climate change impact, which is expected to be decreased when shifting from fossil feedstock to
renewable feedstock. The environmental impacts of a bio-based production of BDO and ADA
was explored. The results of the BDO assessment conﬁrms the expectations of the production of
bio-chemicals showing indications of bio-BDO performing better relative to conventional BDO
production in the CC category and a much stronger indication is seen on the lower impacts in the
FRS category. This is not the case for ADA, where the differences between the conventional ADA
and the bio-ADA production within the CC and FRS categories are too small given the uncertainty
of the model. However, the bio-ADA production requires substantial amounts of solvents resulting
in the small reductions in CC and FRS. High reductions are observed in FEU and MEU of the
bio-ADA production. Common for both BDO and ADA is the high increase in LU as a direct result
of using biomass as feedstock.
Hot-spots have been identiﬁed for both bio-BDO and bio-ADA production. For BDO those entail
the drying of corn stover accounting for 36% of the electricity consumption, contributing to CC
and FRS; cellulase in enzymatic hydrolysis (MEU, TET and WU), fermentation (FEU, MET, HTC,
HTNC, and WU), and up-stream and downstream WWT (FEU). For bio-ADA the identiﬁed hot-
spots are lignin ﬁrst (CC, FRS, TET, HTC, and WU), WWT of the whole plant (MEU and HTC),
methanol and catalyst recovery (TET, MET FET).
The side stream processes (e.g. WWTP, lignin and biomass combustion) are found to be drivers
for both bio-BDO and bio-ADA. Especially, the substitution of heat from the combustion of lignin
(BDO) and holocellulose (ADA) shows high contribution across several impact categories. Due to
the large production of heat from the combustion processes changing to renewable energy indicates
an environmental burden for the production since the substitution of renewable energy does not
have the same environmental beneﬁts as the substitution of fossil energy (natural gas in this case).
This is an effect of the consequential LCA modelling framework. The transition towards renewable
energy is still preferred. Furthermore, high uncertainties are associated to the amount of heat
produced in these processes.
106
==PAGE:114==
Finally, the 2G biomass modelling is found to be important for the results taking the current appli-
cation of corn stover into account. Sensitivity analysis showed big differences in the results when
modelled in different ways. There exists no consensus among LCA practitioners and reﬁning of
the applied model is necessary since it is surrounded by many assumptions.
Further research:
The LCA models constructed for the BDO and ADA production can still be improved. If continuing
the work of the present study, suggested areas of improvement are:
• Generally, better data should be collected, preferably and ideally large scale data from man-
ufacturers. For the bio-ADA production this is currently not possible. More effort should be
put into constructing the conventional BDO and ADA production models, which are currently
based on ecoinvent v3.6 processes.
• In relation to the gathering of better data, validation of assumptions should be done to de-
crease the level of uncertainty.
• Multiple pretreatment methods as well types of biomass for feedstock should be assessed to
obtain more general insights about the bio-based chemicals’ life cycles.
• The choices of biomass substitution are based on rough assumptions, hence the results of
assessing different fodder, and bedding substitutes could be of interest.
• Investigating if corn stover is competing with the production of bio-fuels and whether bio-
fuels or bio-chemical production obtains the highest valorisation of the feedstock.
• Assessing the potential synergy between the bio-BDO and bio-ADA production since they
use each other’s residuals as feedstock for the fermentation.
• Comparing the semi chemical production of bio-ADA with a direct fermentation process.
• Performing a prospective LCA of the bio-ADA production based on self obtained laboratory
results.

A.3
Other petrochemical and biochemical BDO production pathways
BDO
A.3.1
Petrochemical pathway: Acetoxylation of butadiene
The acetoxylation pathway for producing BDO from butadiene was developed by Mitsubishi Chem-
icals in 1982 and since used by the Mitsubishi-Kasei Corporation (Müller, 2011; Izawa and Yokoyama,
2016). This development ended the Reppe process’ BDO production monopoly dating back to the
1930’s (Izawa and Yokoyama, 2016). The raw materials in the butadiene process had advantages
compared to the Reppe process, because they were of petroleum rather than coal origin, but due to
high availability and low cost of natural gas and coal in China the Reppe process has still remained
utilised over the years (Izawa and Yokoyama, 2016).
The process requires two main steps from butadiene to BDO (Müller, 2011; Izawa and Yokoyama,
2016).
(1) In the ﬁrst step butadiene is oxidised to 1,4-diacetoxy-2-butene over a palladium-
tellurium catalyst at 3 MPa and 80°C along with a nitrogen-oxygen mixture and acetic acid. (2)
In the second step 1,4-diacetoxy-2-butene is hydrogenated to 1,4-diacetoxybutane, which is then
hydrolysed to BDO or THF.
A.3.2
Petrochemical pathway: Hydrogenation of maleic anhydride
Maleic anhydride is due to its structure a good precursor to simultaneously produce BDO and THF
(Müller, 2011). The process of using maleic anhydride as feedstock for BDO and THF production
is called the Davy process.
The process requires three main steps from maleic anhydride to BDO. (1) In the ﬁrst step dimethyl
maleate is obtained from esteriﬁcation of maleic anhydride with methanol (JM Davy Technologies,
2015). (2) In the second step dimethyl maleate is completely vaporised with H2 before fed into a
ﬁxed bed reactor (150°C and 15 bar). The reactor has two ﬁxed catalysts (palladium on alumina
and copper-zinc oxide. In the presence of the palladium on alumina catalyst dimethyl maleate is
hydrogenated and fully converted into dimethyl succinate. In the presence of the copper-zinc oxide
catalyst dimethyl succinate is converted at 97% into gammabutyrolactone with trace amount of
BDO and THF (Teh et al., 2019). (3) In the third step the gammabutyrolactone rich stream is fed
into a second hydrogenation reactor with a copper-zinc oxide catalyst for reaction to BDO with
trace amounts of methanol and THF (210°C and 75 bar). The gammabutyrolactone is converted
into BDO at 95% (Teh et al., 2019).
A.3.3
Petrochemical pathway: Isomerisation of propylene oxide
LyondellBasell, formerly ARCO, has commercialised a process to produce BDO based on the iso-
merisation of propylene oxide (Haas et al., 2005). The process requires two main steps from propy-
lene oxide to BDO (Haas et al., 2005; Izawa and Yokoyama, 2016). (1) In the ﬁrst step propylene
oxide is isomerised to allyl alcohol over a lithium phosphate catalyst at 200-300°C and 1 MPa
121
==PAGE:129==
(Müller, 2011). (2) In the second step allyl alcohol is hydroformylated to BDO. According to Haas
et al. (2005) the reaction happens in the presence of a rhodium catalyst, same molar concentration as
rhodium of diphenylphosphinobutane and with triphenylphosphine in excess. According to Izawa
and Yokoyama (2016) the reactions happens via the intermediate 4-hydroxybutyraldehyde and in
the presence of a Nickel Raney catalyst (Izawa and Yokoyama, 2016). 2-methyl-propanediol is also
formed in this process (Izawa and Yokoyama, 2016). According to patent of Dairen Chemical al-
lyl alcohol conversion was 67% to BDO and 33% to 2-methyl-propanediol (Izawa and Yokoyama,
2016).
A.3.4
Biochemical pathway: Catalytic hydrogenation of biosuccinic acid (hybrid)
In 2012 BioAmber Inc., which at the time were producing bio-succinic acid from sugar, announced
that they had completed a production campaign of BDO from hydrogenation of bio-succinic acid
(Ji and Huang, 2014). The BDO from biosuccinic acid is produced in two main steps: (1) anaerobic
fermentation of sugars to succinic acid by either Actinobacillus succinogenes, Anaerobiospirillum
succiniciproducens, Mannheimia succiniciproducens or E. coli, and (2) hydrogenation of succinic
acid to produce BDO in the presence of a bimetallic catalyst (Ji and Huang, 2014; Pham Minh et al.,
2010). Hence, the process is considered a hybrid between a biological and a chemical production
route. The hydrogenation of succinic acid carried out by BioAmber Inc. was performed using a
catalyst patented by DuPont (Ji and Huang, 2014). The plant based ingredient producer, Roquette,
is currently producing biosuccinic acid under the trademark Biosuccinum®(Roquette, 2020) and to
the best knowledge of the author is the only commercial producer of biosuccinic acid.
A.3.5
Biochemical pathway: Using furfural or furan as feedstock
According to Silva et al. (2020) a promising technology for biochemical production of BDO is to
use furfural or furan as feedstock. Furan can easily be obtained from furfural by decarbonylation
(Gräfje et al., 2019; Fischer and Pinkos, 1999).
In the patent by Fischer and Pinkos (1999) on behalf of BASF a process for obtaining BDO from
furan is described. The patent states that furan can be converted in reaction mixture with water
and in the presence of hydrogen over a hydrogenation catalyst and elevated pressure. The catalyst
can be based on a number of elements, for instance rhenium or rhodium, as suggested by Gräfje
et al. (2019) and Silva et al. (2020), respectively. According to Gräfje et al. (2019) and recent
patent application by Wadman and Lange (2018) on behalf of Shell Oil Company, Shell is reﬁning
the process ﬁrst patented by BASF. Both of the latter processes have tetrahydrofuran as a byprod-
uct. In Li et al. (2014) a process is presented for obtaining BDO from furfural in two steps. The
selective oxidation of furfural to the isomers 1(5H)-furanone and 2(3H)-furanone and subsequent
hydrogenation of the furanones to 1,4-BDO is described. Both steps occur in the same reactor in
the presence of a platinum based catalyst (Li et al., 2014). A yield of 85.2% of BDO from furfural
is achieved when using platinum nanoparticles supported over TiO2 −ZrO2 (Li et al., 2014).
122
==PAGE:130==
A.4
Other petrochemical and biochemical ADA production pathways
ADA
A.4.1
Petrochemical pathway: Hydrogenation of phenol followed by cyclohexanol oxidation
The ﬁrst commercially available production route was based on phenol, which today is econom-
ically out competed by benzene, resulting in only few phenol based plants (Bart and Cavallaro,
2015a; Musser, 2005; Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014). This process is similar to the in Section
3.4.3 described two stage oxidation of cyclohexane. The difference lies within the production of
KA oil, which in this case is based on phenol instead of cyclohexane.
In this process phenol is produced via crude oil derived cumene oxidation with ammonia hydroxide
(Zakoshansky, 2007; Bart and Cavallaro, 2015a) producing acetone as co-product. The process
includes either vapour phase or liquid phase hydrogenation of phenol to KA oil using a Pd/Al2O3
catalyst. The vapour phase hydrogenation achieves a KA-oil yield of 99% whereas the liquid
phase hydrogenation obtains a KA yield of 97-99%. The hydrogenation is followed by a three
step distillation separating KA oil from by-products e.g cyclohexenyl (Oppenheim and Dickerson,
2014).
The KA oil obtained in this process undergoes nitric acid oxidation into ADA as described in
Section A.4.
A.4.2
Petrochemical pathway: Nitric acid oxidation of cyclohexanol from cyclohexene
As the name of this process indicated this process also entails nitric acid oxidation as described in
the DuPont process in Section A.4. In this process benzene is partially hydrogenated into cyclo-
hexene using an aqueous ruthenium catalyst system. Cyclohexene is then hydrolysed into cyclo-
hexanol using ZSM-5 zeolite suspended in water. Cyclohexanol selectivity in this process is 99%
and the conversion is between 10-15%. The advantage of this process is the low waste formation
(Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014).
A.4.3
Petrochemical pathway: Butadiene and carbon monoxide based production
The butadiene process was never commercialised, however, it gained signiﬁcant attention (Oppen-
heim and Dickerson, 2014). This process uses butadiene and carbon monoxide. BASF developed
a process, which is sufﬁciently advanced to become commercial. There exist two variations of this
process. One is a carboalkoxylation, where an alcohol is used to produce pentenoates the other is a
hydrocaboxylation, where water is used to produces pentenoic aicds. Dimethyl adipate is procued
which is then further hydrolysed into ADA (Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014; Musser, 2005).
A.4.4
Biochemical pathway: Chemo-catalytic production via two chemical catalytic steps
The characteristics of a chemo-catalytic process in regards to the production of bio-ADA is the fact
that it does not include any biological conversion step. It is a purely chemical process using renew-
123
==PAGE:131==
able feedstock (e.g. glucose) (Gunukula and Anex, 2017). Rennovia developed a chemo-catalytic
process, which ﬁled for a patent in 2010 (Boussie et al., 2013). In their process D-glucose is oxi-
dised over a chemical catalyst using air forming glucaric acid, which is further hydrodeoxygenated
into ADA using yet another chemical catalyst (Boussie et al., 2013; Gunukula and Anex, 2017;
Bernhard et al., 2014; Oppenheim and Dickerson, 2014). The process claims a molar yield of 66%
for the ﬁrst step and a 89% molar yield for the second step (Bernhard et al., 2014).
Advantages related to this chemo-catalytic process is the high production rates of the metal cata-
lysts, however, it reduces the selectivity of the process. Furthermore, it requires the use of expen-
sive sugars, since it has to be free of impurities (Gunukula and Anex, 2017). The Rennovia process
nearly reached commercialisation as a pilot plant was built, however,they had to exit the market in
2018 due to economical infeasibility (De Guzman, 2018).
A.4.5
Biochemical pathway: Direct fermentation of glucose
Bio-ADA can be produced through direct fermentation of sugars. The major traditional renewable
feedstock within biotechnology is glucose (Bernhard et al., 2014). In a direct biochemical process
the feedstock is directly converted into the desired product in a fermentation step.
ADA can under anaerobic conditions be synthesised from glucose by the reverse β-oxidation path-
way in a metabolic engineered E. coli (Gunukula and Anex, 2017). Sugar and E. coli seed is fed
into the fermentor containing fermentation media at pH 5-7. E. coli converts the sugar into ADA.
The pH is maintained using a strong base (Gunukula and Anex, 2017). After fermentation the broth
must be separated and puriﬁed by a combination of centrifugation, mircoﬁltration, and distillation
by distillation removing water and impurities. After distillation it is cooled and ﬁltered before
crystalisation (Gunukula and Anex, 2017).
An advantage of this process route is the high selectivity of the conversion and it allows the use
unpuriﬁed sugars lowering the feedstock costs (Gunukula and Anex, 2017). Weaknesses of this
process is the low production rates of biocatalysts compared to metal catalysts.
A challenge of a fermentation process resulting in ADA production is ﬁnding and engineering a
suitable microorganism, since ADA is a non-natural compound (Wu, 2016; Oppenheim and Dick-
erson, 2014). Another challenge is the fact that the structure of the sugar is different from the end-
product, which makes the biotechnological conversing complex, involving cascades of enzymatic
steps (Bernhard et al., 2014). A successful fermentation process consists of three key performance
indicators, which determines the cost price of the product, e.g. bio-ADA. The ﬁrst key performance
indicator is the yield, which should be close to the maximal theoretical yield. The second is the
volumetric productivity followed by the product concentration (Wu, 2016). To optimise these in-
dicators extensive cycles of metabolic engineering and process optimisation is needed, which is
expensive and time consuming(Wu, 2016).
124
==PAGE:132==
A.4.6
Biochemical pathway: Direct fermentation of fats and alkanes (Verdezyne)
A direct fermentation process was developed by the company Verdezyne (Picataggio and Beardslee,
2012; Beardslee and Picataggio, 2012). The yeast Candida tropicalis was successfully engineered
to convert glucose into ADA through fatty acid synthesis and the ω-oxidation pathway (Picataggio
and Beardslee, 2012). Using the fatty acid intermediates results in the possibility to broaden the
substrate spectrum towards fats and vegetable oils, and alkanes as non-food raw materials (Bern-
hard et al., 2014; Kruyer and Peralta-Yahya, 2017). C. tropicalis is applied in several biological
processes and is able to grow at low pH, which is and advantage when producing an acid in a con-
tinuous process and can reduce the amount of neutralising agent. This process achieved a molar
yield of about 40% from glucose (Picataggio and Beardslee, 2012).
This process nearly reached commercialisation after Verdezyne ran a pilot plant. However Verdezyne
had to exit the market in 2018 (De Guzman, 2018).

==PAGE:133==
A.5
Goal deﬁnition according to ISO14044:2006
General
Table A.5.1: Comprised goal deﬁnition in accordance with DS/EN ISO 14044 (2006) replenishing
the Goal and scope in Section 5.
Goal deﬁnition
Intended
application
To provide guidance the further work of developing biochemical production
routes for BDO and ADA and their appertaining environmental impacts.
Furthermore, the present study will identify major challenges, knowledge
gaps, and areas of improvement related to the biochemical production. The
study will compare the biochemical production route to the conventional
counterpart and may act as basis for early stage support for decision makers.
Limitations
(methodological)
Two separate (e.g. BDO and ADA, respectively) full life cycle assessments
will be performed leading to the opportunity to conclude on overall envi-
ronmental impacts.
• However, one speciﬁc biochemical production route is chosen for each
chemical (e.g. BDO and ADA) and many different combination exist or are
under development. The conclusions of the present study are hence only va-
lid for the chosen production route, which must be taken into account when
making recommendation.
• The present study consists of cradle to factory gate assessments not taking
the use and end-of-life stage into account.
• The data is exclusively literature data, hence no primary data is used for
the foreground system.
• Speciﬁcally for ADA: no commercial production route, hence the biochem-
ical production route is based on laboratory scale data.
Decision-context
The decision context of the present study is found to be B: ’macro-level de-
cision support’, since implementing or replacing the conventional produc-
tion of BDO or ADA with the biochemical production will have to structu-
ral consequences.
Target audience
The target audience of the present study is the chemical industry, especially,
those working with enhancement of green chemistry and the development of
biochemical alternatives to fossil-based chemicals. Another audience are po-
litical decision makers working with the green transitioning of the industrial
sector.
Comparative
studies
The present study will include a comparative element, hence the same func-
tional unit is applied for both systems. However, the study will not be dis-
closed to the public and an external critical review is not necessary.
Commissioner
of the study
There is no commissioner or other inﬂuential actors to the present study, sin-
ce it forms a MSc. thesis conducted at the Technical University of Denmark.

==PAGE:134==
A.6
Scope deﬁnition according to ISO14044:2006
General
Table A.6.1: Comprised scope deﬁnition in accordance with DS/EN ISO 14044 (2006) replenishing
the Goal and scope in Section 5.
Scope deﬁnition
Deliverable
The present study will include a full cradle-to-gate life cycle assessment,
which will include:
• Life cycle inventory
• Life cycle impact assessment including:
• Hot-spot analysis
• Perturbation analysis,
• Sensitivity
• Qualitative uncertainty analysis.
Object of
assessment
The present study entails two objects of assessment. (1) the biochemical
production of BDO derived from 2G biomass, and (2) the biochemical
production of ADA derived from 2G biomass. Detailed descriptions of
the biochemical production of BDO and ADA can be found in Section
3.3 and 3.4 in the main report.
Functional unit
The functional unit of the present study is:’the production of 1 kg poly-
mer grade BDO/ADA in the state of Iowa, US, in 2020’.
LCI modelling
framework
The LCI modelling framework is based on the decision context, which
in the goal deﬁnition is found to be decision context B, hence the con-
sequential LCI modelling framework is applied whenever possible. This
means marginal processes are used for the structurally changed back-
ground system and average processes are used for the remaining proces-
ses. Furthermore, the consequential modelling will result in system ex-
pansion for multifunctional processes. If system expansion cannot be
applied allocation is applied.
System boundary
and completeness
The system boundaries for both the conventional and biochemical pro-
duction of BDO and ADA can be found in Section 5 in the main report.
They include everything from resource extraction to the production of 1
kg BDO or ADA for the conventional production and the feedstock pro-
vision to the production of 1 kg BDO or ADA for the biochemical pro-
duction. The completeness of the conventional and biochemical produc-
tion is deemed to be equal for both BDO and ADA.

==PAGE:135==
Table A.6.1 continued from previous page
Scope deﬁnition
Representativeness
of LCI data
• Geographical: The geographical scope of the current study is Iowa, US,
since it is a state in the corn belt producing high quantities of corn, and
hence corn stover. All processes are aimed to ﬁt the geographical loca-
tion
of Iowa or the US. In situations where no US processes or data is availa-
ble global processes are used.
• Temporal: The conventional process is and has been representative for
a long time since it is well established and has dominated the market for
many years. The biochemical production processes represent a snapshot
of the current situation. The BDO process is assumed to be representa-
tive for longer time than the ADA process, since the BDO process has
reached commercialisation whereas the ADA process is still on labora-
tory scale. The development of the ADA process is assumed to rapidly
being optimised and the representativeness of the ADA process in the
present study is deemed representative for a short time period (5 years)
• Technological:
Due to the well established nature of the conventional BDO and ADA
production processes the technological scope is assumed to be represen-
tative since the processes are highly optimised and no big adjustments
are expected until more environmentally friendly processes exist to re-
place them. Due to the lack of time to mature and optimise the biochem-
ical production technologies presented in the present study are consider-
ed representative now and some years ahead (5 years)

==PAGE:136==
Table A.6.1 continued from previous page
Scope deﬁnition
Preparation of the
basis for the impact
assessment
The life cycle impact assessment method chosen to evaluate the life cyc-
le inventory of the present study is ReCiPe2016 with a hierarchical per-
spective assessing at midpoint level. The impact categories in the present
study are: climate change, freshwater, and marine eutrophication, fossil
resource scarcity, terrestrial, freshwater and marine ecotoxicity, human
toxicity cancer and non-cancer, land, and water use. The impact catego-
ries have been chosen due to their relevance to the scope of the present
study as early stage assessments. climate change and fossil resource scar-
city are included since they present the commonly motivation of the tran-
sition toward green chemistry. LU is included due ti the apparent strains
on the environment using biomass. WU is included since fermentation
processes require large amounts of water, which can be a scarce resource
depending on the geographical location. High uncertainties are related to
the toxicity categories and must be taken into consideration when making
any conclusions. The normalisation of the LCIA results is performed us-
ing the afﬁliated normalisation factors to ReCiPe2016, normalising to the
global population in 2010.
Special requirements
for comparison
Due to the comparative nature of the present study the conventional and
biochemical routes should be modelled on the same basis. Hence, the fun-
ctional unit must be the same for all systems and the system boundaries
must be similar. The conventional routes are based on the equivalent eco-
invent processes. The biochemical routes are contrary, modelled based on
a combination of literature data and assumptions and guesstimates made
by the LCA practitioners. The present study will not be disclosed to the
public.
Need for critical
review
Since the present study is not being disclosed to the public there is no
need for an external critical review.
Planning reporting
of results
The results obtained of the present study will be reported in accordance
to DS/EN ISO 14040 (2008) and DS/EN ISO 14044 (2006) with some
adaptation to increase readability due to the present study comprises a
MSc. thesis including a literature study as well as methodological ele-
ments.

A.8

General
The modiﬁed Scandinavian Feed Unit (SFU) of corn stover and soybean meal is calculated to relate
the mass of soybean meal required to replace mass of corn stover used for chemical production. The
calculations are carried out in accordance with the procedures of Madsen et al. (2007). Information
regarding various constituents in the feed is required for the calculations and are obtained from
Feedipedia.org2020 (2020) (See Table A.8.1).

A.9
Inventory of ’Other processes’ for bio-based 1,4-butanediol production
BDO
Processes outside the main production line of bio-BDO are denoted as "Other processes" in the
present study and covers (1) lignin combustion, (2) upstream wastewater treatment, (3) utilities
electricity upstream, (4) upstream sludge combustion, (5) upstream biogas engine, (6) downstream
wastewater treatment and (7) GBL combustion.
Lignin combustion: The lignin stream removed in the lignin ﬁlter after enzymatic hydrolysis con-
sists of water (62%), lignin, and some carbohydrates, and is assumed combusted with a fuel value of
20.7 MJ/kg dry matter (adapted from Demirbas (2017) and Kim et al. (2017)). Drying of the lignin
stream from 62 to 40% water content is assumed, and the electricity use for this is adapted from
INL (2013) energy used to dry the corn stover during mechanical pretreatment (scaled based on
the kg of water removed). The heat of vaporisation of water at 20°C is 2.453 MJ/kg water, which
is subtracted from the fuel value (Kirkham, 2014). The combustion produces heat with a 90%
efﬁciency. The combustion process is based on the EASETECH v3.3.7 "Combustion plant, man-
ufacturing industry (boiler between 50 and 300 MW), wood and similar (updated in 2016), DK".
The residues after combustion are deposited as bottom ashes on a sanitary landﬁll (EASETECH
process). See Table A.20.22, Appendix A.20 for full inventory.
Upstream wastewater treatment: Black liquor removed from deacetylation and ammonia condition-
ing and microﬁlter retentate is sent to an internal wastewater treatment plant (Davis et al., 2015).
As previously mentioned, the wastewater treatment plant described in Davis et al. (2015), is serv-
ing an integrated process for pretreatment and catalytic conversion and upgrading to hydrocarbons,
and hence, is multifunctional. The scope of the present study should only include the wastewater
treatment that is related to the black liquor and microﬁlter retentate, and not to wastewater from
other unit processes. 77% of the wastewater treatment has been included in the scope of the present
study, while 23% has been excluded. These allocation keys are based on an average of the ﬂow
and impurities in the wastewater coming from included (58% of the ﬂow, 96% of the impurities)
and excluded unit processes (42% of the ﬂow and 4% of the impurities). The wastewater treatment
plant includes anaerobic digestion of sludge to produce biogas, which is combusted in a station-
ary engine. The remaining sludge after anaerobic digestion is assumed combusted without energy
recovery. See Table A.20.20, Appendix A.20 for full inventory of upstream wastewater treatment.
Utilities electricity upstream: Electricity used to deliver utilities to the pretreatment plant is adapted
from Davis et al. (2015). The utilities in Davis et al. (2015) serve an integrated process for pretreat-
ment of biomass and catalytic conversion and upgrading to hydrocarbons. The present study only
includes the utilities that are related to the air and process water delivery to unit processes included
in the present study, and not from excluded unit processes. 48% of the utilities are allocated to the
present study (biomass pretreatment), while 52% are excluded. The allocation key is based on an
average of air and process water delivered to included unit processes (35% of the air, 60% of the
process water) and excluded unit processes (65% of the air and 40% of the process water). See
132
==PAGE:140==
Table A.20.24, Appendix A.20 for full inventory.
Upstream sludge combustion: The remaining sludge after anaerobic digestion is assumed com-
busted without energy recovery. The combustion emissions are based on EASETECH v3.3.7 "Com-
bustion plant, manufacturing industry (boiler between 50 and 300 MW), wood and similar (updated
in 2016), DK". The residues after combustion are deposited as bottom ashes on a sanitary landﬁll
according to the EASETECH v3.3.7 process "Bottom Ash Landﬁll". See Table A.20.21, Appendix
A.20 for full inventory.
Upstream biogas engine: The wastewater treatment plant includes anaerobic digestion of sludge
to produce biogas ( 50% CH4, which is combusted in a stationary engine at a fuel value of 38
MJ/Nm3 to produce heat at a 51% efﬁciency and electricity at a 39% efﬁciency (80% overall
efﬁciency). See inventory in LCI Table A.20.21, Appendix A.20. The direct emissions are based
on the EASETECH v3.3.7 process "Stationary engines, biogas per m3 CH4". See Table A.20.23,
Appendix A.20 for full inventory.
Downstream wastewater treatment: The downstream wastewater treatment was based on the ecoin-
vent v3.6 process "treatment of wastewater from vegetable oil reﬁnery, GLO", which deﬁnes the
inventory based on m3 wastewater with a COD of 42 kg/m3. This process was scaled to the esti-
mated COD of downstream wastewater in the present study, which was 13.7 kg/m3. The COD was
estimated from theoretical oxygen demand calculations for elemental composition of impurities in
the wastewater. See mass balance in Appendix A.1 (attached) for calculations. See Table A.20.26,
Appendix A.20 for full inventory.
GBL combustion: The remaining waste after BDO distillation is assumed combusted without en-
ergy recovery. The combustion emissions are based on EASETECH v3.3.7 "Combustion plant,
manufacturing industry (boiler between 50 and 300 MW), wood and similar (updated in 2016),
DK". The residues after combustion are deposited as bottom ashes on a sanitary landﬁll according
to the EASETECH v3.3.7 process "Bottom Ash Landﬁll". See Table A.20.25, Appendix A.20 for
full inventory.
Data quality and uncertainty: The data quality indicators for each of the above described unit pro-
cesses can be seen in Table A.10.1. For the "other processes" the DQIs are between 2.8 and 4,
ranging from medium to very low data quality. The best data quality is obtained in the upstream
wastewater treatment utilities electricity upstream, since these are adapted from Davis et al. (2015)
process simulations. The data quality is lower for the remaining processes as the they are largely
based on rough assumptions founded in data from completely different systems than the one evalu-
ated here. This introduces uncertainty to the results of the LCA, which should be taken into account
during evaluation.

A.11
Inventory of ’Other processes’ for bio-based adipic acid production
ADA
Waste streams (side streams) are attached to the main production line of bio-based ADA. No de-
scription of waste treatment is described in the literature describing the main production line, since
most of them are articles reporting laboratory experiments. Therefore processes from ecoinvent
v3.6 is used and assumptions made. Other processes covers (1) wastewater treatment, (2) biomass
combustion, and (3) inert landﬁll.
Wastewater treatment: Wastewater is generated from centrifugation, activated carbon ﬁlter, and
crystallisation. Waste water generated by the cleaning of the different steps of the main production
line has not been included due to the lack of data, which underestimates the amount of wastewater
treated. The chemical oxygen demand of the wastewater was estimated calculating the theoretical
oxygen demand of the different compounds in the wastewater, which turned out to be very high and
it is assumed that more water must come through the system. Hence, water is added to reduce the
theoretical oxygen demand to 42 kg/m3, which is the COD in wastewater treated in the ecoinvent
v3.6 process ’treatment of wastewater from vegetable oil reﬁnery, GLO’, which was chosen for the
modelling of a wastewater treatment plant (WWTP), since it is the process found to treat wastewater
with the highest COD. It is further assumed that the WWTP substitutes 82% water, which is based
on Davis et al. (2015). Table A.22.18 in Appendix A.22 discloses the LCI table of this process
including all literature references used for compiling the model
Biomass combustion: The solids obtained from the lignin ﬁrst process include holocellulose, which
in the bio-based ADA production is assumed to be combusted. The integrated process ’Combus-
tion plant, manufacturing industry (boiler between 50 and 300 MW), wood and similar, DK’ in
EASETECH was used for this purpose due to the lack of data. It is assumed that it mainly consists
of hydrocarbons (holocellulose) and to a small extent of lignin. The heating value was estimated
using literature value of cellulose, hemicellulose, and non-wood lignin and weighted by the glucan,
xylan, and lignin composition of the biomass. The heating value has further been used for the entire
mass entering the biomass combustion plant. Furthermore, it is assumed that the combustion plant
generates heat with an efﬁciency of 90%, which is based on a combination of expert judgement
and making a qualitative guess. The bottom ash of the combustion process is sent to a bottom ash
landﬁll, which is an integrated EASETECH process. Table A.22.17 in Appendix A.22 discloses
the LCI table of this process including all literature references used for compiling the model
Inert landﬁll: Salts are removed from the crystallised MA to increase its purity. The salts are land-
ﬁlled and the process hereof was taken from ecoinvent v3.6 ’treatment of inert waste, inert material
landﬁll, RoW’. It only accounts for process speciﬁc emissions. Vardon et al. (2016) made an ele-
mentary analysis of MA before and after the salt removing process and the elementary composition
was derived from here. Table A.22.19 in Appendix A.22 discloses the LCI table of this process
including all literature references used for compiling the model
135
==PAGE:143==
Uncertainty and data quality: The uncertainty of the stage ’Other processes’ is mostly based on the
representativeness of the used data. Table 6.7 in Section 6.5 show the DQI of the different pro-
cesses being between 2.6 (inert landﬁll) and 3.2 (biomass combustion). For all three low scores are
obtained within geographical and technological correlation (see Table A.12.1 in Appendix A.12),
simply due to the lack of knowledge, on which processes would be used in reality. To exactly
know data of an operating plant is needed or better insight in the waste stream composition. The
sensitivity and contribution of the above process will be assessed in Section 8.
