A.13
List of assumptions for 1,4-butanediol production
Table A.13.1: List of assumptions for 1,4-butanediol models (conventional and bio-based). The assumptions are divided into "general",
"conventional BDO" and each life cycle stage for bio-BDO.
List of assumptions
General
•
Steam is used in multiple processes within the bio-BDO production plant and included both high pressure (HP), medium pressure (MP)and low
pressure (LP). It is mentioned in (Davis et al., 2015) that HP is 26.34 bar, and it is assumed that MP is 15 bar and LP 1 bar. The speciﬁc enthalpy
of steam for HP, MP and LP is 2.80, 2.79 and 2.68 MJ/kg steam. The ecoinvent v3.6 process "market for steam, in chemical industry, RER"
deﬁned per kg has been applied. This process indicates speciﬁc enthalpy of steam is 2.75. This process has been scaled to the HP, MP and LP
deﬁned in the present study by means of the speciﬁc enthalpy.
•
It is assumed that cooling water for BDO production is obtained from an undeﬁned natural origin and is not cooled down inside the plant before it
is applied. An elementary exchange has been used to model this, namely, "water, cooling, unspeciﬁed natural origin" . It is furthermore assumes
that a once-through system is used and only 0.4% evaporates inside the plant, meaning that 99.6% of the water is discharged back to its natural
origin. This is based on the ecoinvent v3.6 process "cooling energy, from natural gas, at cogen unit with absorption chiller 100kW, CH"
Conventional BDO
•
The production of BDO has been adapted from the ecoinvent v3.6 process "butane-1,4-diol production, RoW", which used the Reppe process and
subsequent hydrogenation to produce BDO. This has been considered representative for the conventional production of BDO.
•
The theoretical yield of BDO from acetylene is 3.461g/g, and the yield used in ecoinvent v3.6 process "butane-1,4-diol production, RoW" is
90.3% of this according to the input of acetylene. It has been assumed that each step in the BDO production process has equal efﬁciency,
equalling 95% of the theoretical yield of 2-butyne-1,4-diol from acetylene and 95% of the theoretical yield of BDO from 2-butyne-1,4-diol.
•
The ecoinvent v3.6 process "butane-1,4-diol production, RoW" has been divided in two processes in the EASETECH model, namely, "Reppe
process" and "hydrogenation". The copper ﬂows are assigned to the Reppe process as this process uses a copper catalyst, while the nickel ﬂows
are assigned to the hydrogenation process, as this process uses a nickel catalyst. Furthermore, the liquid hydrogen and nitrogen is assigned to the
hydrogenation process. All remaining ﬂows are split equally between the two processes.
•
In the ecoinvent v3.6 process "butane-1,4-diol production, RoW" chemical factory construction is included, which has been removed for the
present study, since the construction of production plant for bio-based BDO is not included.
•
The cooling water used in the ecoinvent v3.6 process "butane-1,4-diol production, RoW" has been adapted to the aforementioned "once through"
system where 99.6% of the water is returned to its natural origin.
Bio-BDO: Feedstock
==PAGE:145==
Table A.13.1 continued from previous page
List of assumptions
•
The 2G biomass used in Sc2-bioBDO in the present study is assumed to be consisting of only corns tover, although Davis et al. (2015) used
extensively for the LCI model of biomass pretreatment assumes the biomass is a mix of single-pass corn stover, multi-pass corn stover and
switchgrass. The focus of the present study is to compare environmental performance of petrochemical and 2G biochemical production of BDO,
and not to evaluate the supply system of biomass for bioenergy, hence it is considered a reasonable estimate.
•
The current use of corn stover in Iowa is assumed to be: 39% "no market or use" and hence, burden-free, 26% harvested, and, hence replaced by
straw for bedding material, and 34% grazed, and hence, replaced by soybean meal for fodder. The straw has been replaced 1:1 on a dry matter
basis (ecoinvent v3.6 "straw production, stand-alone production, CH" adapted to the US by changing the embedded processes "irrigation" from
CH to US and the "fodder loading" and "mowing" from CH to GLO). The soybean meal has been scaled based on the SFUs calculated for
soybean meal and corn stover (ecoinvent v3.6 "market for soybean meal, RoW"). Adapted from Schmer et al. (2017).
•
The SFUs of soybean meal and corn stover are estimated fromthe composition of these feed types obtained from feedipedia.org, while the water
content is in line with the mass balance of the present study, 30% water in corn stover at harvest and 89% water in soybean meal according to
ecoinvent v3.6 "market for soybean meal, RoW".
Bio-BDO: Mechanical pretreatment
•
The transport from crop to feedstock facility (mechanical pretreatment) is assumed to be included in the feedstock provision
•
The electricity consumption for milling, drying and densifying corn stover after harvest is adapted from Idaho National Laboratory (2013), and is
an average value of different technologies.
•
The transport of feedstock from the mechanical pretreatment facility to the BDO production plant is assumed to be 100 km by truck on average,
which is an estimate by the authors. The means of transport is assumed to be the largest truck available in ecoinvent v3.6 ("market for transport,
freight, lorry >32 metric ton, EURO6, RoW")
•
In Idaho National Laboratory (2013) it is stated that the water content is reduced to 9% to reduce the mass of the biomass for transportation to the
end-user. In Davis et al. (2015) it is stated that the received biomass contains 20% water. The discrepancy in water content between the two
processes is disregarded. The transported mass from mechanical pretreatment contains 9% water and the received mass at the BDO faciclity
contains 20%.
Bio-BDO: Chemical pretreatment
•
Electricity for chemical pretreatment in Davis et al. (2015) is not divided into deacteylation, dilute acid pretreatment and ammonia conditioning,
and therefore it has been assumed that each of these processes carry 1/3 of the chemical pretreatment electricity use.
•
Black liquor removed during chemical pretreatment of biomass is given by Davis et al. (2015) and it is assumed that half of the black liquor is
removed from the deacetylation step, while the other half is removed from the ammonia conditioning step.
•
Process for chemical pretreatment in Davis et al. (2015) is not divided into deacteylation, dilute acid pretreatment and ammonia conditioning, and
therefore it has been assumed that 1/2 of the process water goes to deacetylation, and 1/2 goes to ammonia conditioning.
==PAGE:146==
Table A.13.1 continued from previous page
List of assumptions
•
According to the Davis et al. (2015) design basis the cellulase loading is 10 mg enzyme protein/g cellulose, and the conversion efﬁciency of
cellulose to glucose is 90% . However, only the use of almost double this loading has been demonstrated by NREL in 2013, and therefore 20 mg
protein/g cellulose has been used in the present study, while maintaining the conversion assumption.
•
"Hydrolysis and lignin ﬁltration", and "Concentration, ﬁltration and ion exchange" use 10% of the 46 MW power generated for the entire plant
(incl. hydrocarbon production) in Davis et al. (2015). 1/4 of this is assumed to go to hydrolysis and lignin ﬁltration, while 3/4 are assumed to go to
concentration, ﬁltration and ion exchange.
•
There is not mass conservation regarding water in the enzymatic hydrolysis and lignin ﬁltration process deﬁned by Davis et al. (2015), hence, the
missing water is assumed evaporated.
•
The lignin ﬁlter uses air according to Davis et al. (2015), which is atmospheric. Hence, it is assumed that the electricity for delivering the air is
included in the energy consumption of the plant, and hence, the atmospheric air in and out of the ﬁlter is not modelled.
•
Davis et al. (2015) includes electricity for utilities aside from the electricity assigned to the process steps. This electricity has been included as a
separate process in the model.
•
Recycled process water from concentration, ﬁltration and ion exchange in Davis et al. (2015) is assumed to substitute the use of new process water.
Bio-BDO: Fermentation
•
The sum of steam required to sterilise sugar water and fermentation broth is given in Satam et al. (2019) as "other heat exchangers", so it is
assumed 50% of this steam is used for sugar water sterilisation and 50% is used for broth sterilisation
•
The total amount of fermentation broth was calculated based on the titer of 140 g BDO/L and the production of 1.0811 kg BDO (considering
downstream loss of 7.5% BDO), resulting in a total broth volume of 7.721 L. The amount of process water added to the fermentation reactor was
calculated as the total amount of fermentation broth minus the amount of sugar water added (from pretreatment).
•
The fermentation process described in Satam et al. (2019) has a scruber to remove volatile organic compounds in the off-gas and it is assumed that
no remaining gases are emitted from the reactor. This may be a rough assumption.
•
The seed train (growing of E. coli before fermentation) has been disregarded in the present study.
•
Although not agreed upon in literature, the present study considers 0.5 g BDO/g glucose the maximum theoretical yield of BDO from glucose
using Genomatica engineered E. coli and adapts a realistic yield of 0.40 g BDO/g glucose (80%of the theoretical yield).
•
Different titers are obtained in literature for the direct fermentation of sugar to BDO. In the present study a titer of 140g/L is assumed.
•
Satam et al. (2019) considers a dextrose substrate for the fermentation using Genomatica engineered E. coli. In this study the substrate is assumed
to be a mix of glucose and xylose obtained from pretreatment of corn stover. The yield of BDO from xylose was also considered ≈80% of the
theoretical yield, corresponding to 0.468 g BDO/g xylose. Thiswas assumed based on Novy et al. (2015) obtaining this yield in ethanol
fermentation from mixed sugars.
•
The amount of Na2HPO4 needed for the fermentation media is based on M9 minimal media (Genomatica and Novamont, 2015; Sigma Aldrich
2020).
Bio-BDO: Downstream processing
==PAGE:147==
Table A.13.1 continued from previous page
List of assumptions
•
A downstream recovery of BDO of 92.5% is assumed. It is assumed that 1/7 of the 7.5% BDO lost downstream is lost per one of the seven
downstream processes.
•
It is assumed that all cells are removed in the microﬁltration step.
•
It is assumed that 95% of the proteins are removed in the ultraﬁltration step.
•
It is assumed that 85% of the residual sugars, and 35% of the other impurities (mainly salts) are removed in the nanoﬁltration step.
•
It is assumed that in the ion exchange step an additional 35% of the other impurities in the fermentation broth out of the bioreactor are removed
(mainly salts).
•
It is assumed that in the evaporation step 30% of the other impurities in the fermentation broth out of the bioreactor are removed (ethanol).
•
The amount of acid and base used for regeneration of the ion exchange resins downstream of fermentation is not deﬁned in Satam et al. (2019).
Hence, it has been assumed that the acid/base requirements are equal to those used in the ion exchange bed in the pretreatment of biomass deﬁned
by Davis et al. (2015). In that study the amount of acid and base required for regeneration is deﬁned by the amount of soluble solids in the stream.
It is assumed that 50% of the solids in the fermentation broth are soluble, which is a rough estimate.
•
The water distillation unit described in Satam et al. (2019) uses chilled water which is replaced by cooling water in the present study. This is a
rough assumption, as cooling water does not have equal cooling capacity as chilled water.
•
Satam et al. (2019) mentions a purity of BDO after downstream processing of 99.99%, which is assumed obtained in the present study. Satam
et al. (2019) mentions a maximum content of 2-Pyrrolidone of 5 ppm and 2-(4’-Hydroxybutoxy)-THF of 500 ppm. In the present study these
numbers are assumed 5 ppm and 95 ppm, respectively.
Bio-BDO: Other processes
•
The fuel value of the lignin waste stream is assumed to be 20.7 MJ/kg dry matter which was adapted from Demirbas (2017); Kim et al. (2017).
The lignin waste is assumed combusted. From this value the heat of vaporisation (2.453 MJ/kg for water at 20°C) is subtracted for each kg of
water in the lignin waste stream (62% water content). The remaining energy is transformed to heat energy with an efﬁciency of 90%. The
generated heat is substituted 1:1 with the marginal energy deﬁned for the US in the present study. The direct emissions from combustion are
adapted from EASETECH v3.3.7 "Combustion plant, manufacturing industry (boiler between 50 and 300 MW), wood and similar (updated in
2016), DK". The bottom ashes are considered sent to bottom ash landﬁll, and the environmental burdens of this landﬁll have been adapted from
EASETECH v3.3.7 "Bottom Ash Landﬁll".
•
The wastewater treatment plant described in Davis et al. (2015) serves an integrated process for pretreatment of biomass and catalytic conversion
and upgrading to hydrocarbons. The present study only includes the wastewater treatment that is related to the black liquor and ﬁlter retentate, and
not to wastewater from other unit processes. 77% of the wastewater treatment has been allocated to the present study (biomass pretreatment),
while 23% has been excluded. The allocation key is based on an average of the ﬂow and impurities in the wastewater coming from included (58%
of the ﬂow, 96% of the impurities) and excluded unit processes (42% of the ﬂow and 4% of the impurities).
==PAGE:148==
Table A.13.1 continued from previous page
List of assumptions
•
The upstream wastewater treatment plant uses air according to Davis et al. (2015), which is atmospheric. Hence, it is assumed that the electricity
for delivering the air is included in the energy consumption of the plant, and hence, the atmospheric air in and out of the wastewater is not
modelled.
•
It is assumed that all of the chemicals used in the upstream wastewater treatment plant are ammonia, since it is mentioned in Davis et al. (2015)
that ammonia constitutes most of the chemicals.
•
The utilities in Davis et al. (2015) serve an integrated process for pretreatment of biomass and catalytic conversion and upgrading to
hydrocarbons. The present study only includes the utilities that are related to the air and process water delivery to unit processes included in the
present study, and not from excluded unit processes. 48% of the utilities have been allocated to the present study (biomass pretreatment), while
52% has been excluded. The allocation key is based on an average of air and process water delivered to included unit processes (35% of the air,
60% of the process water) and excluded unit processes (65% of the air and 40% of the process water).
•
The sludge from upstream wastewater treatment has 40% dry matter, and a fuel value of 16.2 MJ/kg dry matter (Kim et al., 2005; Aragón-Briceño
et al., 2017). However, the average fuel value (including water) is only 6.3 MJ/kg, which is low for combustion. This means that an auxiliary fuel
could be added to aid the process, however, it is assumed in this study that no auxiliaries are added and that the sludge is only burned for the sake
of managing the mass, and not for energy recovery. The direct emissions from combustion are adapted from EASETECH v3.3.7 "Combustion
plant, manufacturing industry (boiler between 50 and 300 MW), wood and similar (updated in 2016), DK". The bottom ashes are considered sent
to bottom ash landﬁll, and the environmental burdens of this landﬁll have been adapted from EASETECH v3.3.7 "Bottom Ash Landﬁll".
•
The amount of energy in the biogas from the upstream wastewater treatment plant is assumed 38 MJ/Nm3 CH4 and the methane content is 51%
vol. in the biogas. The energy is transformed to heat energy with an efﬁciency of 51% and to electricity with an efﬁciency of 38%. The generated
heat and electricity is substituted 1:1 with the marginal energy deﬁned for the US in the present study. The direct emissions from combustion are
adapted from EASETECH v3.3.7 "Stationary engines, biogas per m3 CH4".
•
The COD content in the downstream wastewater has been estimated based on the theoretical oxygen demand calculation which relies on the C, H,
O and N elemental composition of the impurities in the wastewater. The actual COD is typically higher than the theoretical. The fraction of
wastes denoted as "other impurites" has been considered 50% acetate and 50% 4-Hydroxybutanoate.
•
The downstream wastewater treatment plant has been based on the ecoinvent v3.6 process "wastewater from vegetable oil reﬁnery" which has a
COD content of 42 kg/m3, while the COD content in the wastewater generated downstream in the present study was estimated to be 13.7 kg/m3.
The ecoinvent process was adapted accordingly.
•
The GBL waste stream has 100% dry matter, and a fuel value of 6.4 MJ/kg (Satam et al., 2019), which is low for combustion, even though it is
considered combusted in Satam et al. (2019). This means that an auxiliary fuel could be added to aid the process, however, it is assumed in this
study that no auxiliaries are added and that the GBL waste stream is only burned for the sake of managing the mass, and not for energy recovery.
The direct emissions from combustion are adapted from EASETECH v3.3.7 "Combustion plant, manufacturing industry (boiler between 50 and
300 MW), wood and similar (updated in 2016), DK". The bottom ashes are considered sent to bottom ash landﬁll, and the environmental burdens
of this landﬁll have been adapted from EASETECH v3.3.7 "Bottom Ash Landﬁll".
==PAGE:149==
A.14
List of assumptions for adipic acid production
Table A.14.1: List of assumptions for adipic acid models (conventional and bio-based). The assumptions are divided into "general", "conven-
tional ADA" and each life cycle stage for bio-ADA.
List of assumptions
General
•
Annual operations hours is 8000h
•
Titer of 100 g/L to make the process more realistic as a commercial process
•
Corn stover composition (lignin, cellulose, hemicellulose, ash, water) derived from several literature sources and using the average.
•
A once-trough system is assumed. According to the ecoinvent v3.6 "market for cooling energy, CH" 0.4% of the water is evaporated during the
cooling process resulting in 99.6% of the water being lead back to the original source.
•
Amount of cooling water is calculated based on heat capacity and temperature difference.
•
The carbon support of the Ni/C and Rh/C catalyst used in the bio-ADA production is assumed to be activated carbon.
Conventional ADA
•
Copper is used as proxy for vanadium, since no vanadium production process exist in ecoinvent v3.6.
•
Ecoinvent v3.6 process is assumed to be representative for the conventional benzene, cyclohexane and adipic acid prodution.
•
Benzenen is bought from the global market, hence it is not adapted to use the marginal energy processes as used in the other processes.
Bio-ADA: Feedstock provision
•
The current applications of corn stover were derived from Schmer et al. (2017). The shares of the categories "left on land","Not proﬁtable", and
"Other" in Schmer et al. (2017) were equally divided and added to "Harvested", and "Grazed".
•
The "Harvested" category from Schmer et al. (2017) is assumed to cover the use of corn stover for bedding.
•
Corn stover is assumed not to be used for bio-fuel production and was not included in the feedstock modelling.
•
Straw substitutes the corn stover used for bedding 1:1. The fraction of corn stover for bedding is 26% of the total corn stover used adapted from on
Schmer et al. (2017).
•
Soybean meal substitutes corn stover used as feed based on SFU of corn stover and soybean meal. The fraction of corn stover used for feed for
cattle is 34% of the total corn stover used; adapted from Schmer et al. (2017)
•
The remaining 39% are not used for any other purposes and can be considered free; adapted from Schmer et al. (2017).
Bio-ADA: Mechanical pretreatment
•
30% water content in the harvested corn stover.
•
Using average electricity data of different technologies for milling, densiﬁcation, and drying.
•
9% water content after drying.
•
Transportation distance of 100 km from the feedstock facilityto the BDO production plant.
Bio-ADA: Chemical pretreatment
==PAGE:150==
Table A.14.1 continued from previous page
List of assumptions
•
Assumed same amount of H2 as in the adipic acid hydrogenation. However here I used the output of methyl coumarate instead of adipic acid.
•
Heating and cooling in the lignin ﬁrst process is calculated based on the heat capacity of methanol and the temperature difference from 25°C to
250°C.
•
Cooling water is used to cool down the process.
•
The lignin ﬁrst process is assumed to be heated and afterwards cooled - no re-circulation system.
•
The lignin technology will mature more - the lignin yields are assumed to be as high as the current cellulose yield in sachariﬁcation of biomass
derived from Davis et al. (2015).
•
The amount of methanol was scaled down to 1:1 methanol to corn stover to simulate large scale process.
•
The amount of catalyst was scaled down to 0.01:1 catalyst to corn stover to simulate large scale process.
•
The amount of phosphoric acid was scaled down to 0.01:1 phosphoric acid to corn stover to simulate large scale process.
•
The amount of hydrogen is assumed to be the same as described in Corona et al. (2018b), due to lack of data. It is scaled to the amount of methyl
coumaric acid instead of adipic acid.
•
Lignin extraction yield is assumed to be 90% assuming that the process can be further optimised reaching levels of the cellulose extraction yield
during sachariﬁcation.
•
38% of the lignin oil is methyl coumaric acid. The aromatic compound to lignin oil ratio is assumed to be the same as in small scale experiment of
Anderson et al. (2016b).
•
The residual solids of the lignin process is consists of the remaining mass of the corn stover after subtracting the mass of the lignin oil.
•
Methanol can be recovered and recycled.
•
90% of the nickel catalyst is recovered.
•
The energy consumption of the methanol recovery is assumed to be the same as for ethanol recovery from Roy and Dutta (2013) adapted to the
density of methanol.
•
The recovery rate of ethanol in the catalytic hydrogenation step was used for the methanol recovery.
Bio-ADA: Fermentation
•
Sodium phosphate is used as proxy for disodium hydrogen phosphate, since disodium hydrogen phosphate does not exist in ecoinvent v3.6.
•
Potassium sulphate is used as proxy for potassium dihydrogen phosphate, since potassium dihydrogen phosphate does not exist in ecoinvent v3.6.
•
Ammonium sulphate is used as proxy for diammonium sulphate, since diammonium sulphate does not exist in ecoinvent v3.6.
•
Glucose is used to ensure cell maintenance during fermentation.
•
The amount of water added to the fermentation process is calculated based on the titer.
•
Aeration of the fermentation process was disregarded.
•
The cultivation of fermentation seed was disregarded.
•
The by-products formed during fermentation is protocatechuate representing the metabolic intermediates.
==PAGE:151==
Table A.14.1 continued from previous page
List of assumptions
•
The geneation of carbon dioxide is neglected, since carbon dioxide originating from biomass is considered CO2 neutral.
Bio-ADA: Downstream processing and hydrogenation
•
20% of water is removed with cells after fermentation not affecting the products and by-product concentration.
•
The energy consumption of the separation is adapted from Corona et al. (2018b), covering the ﬁltration steps. The energy consumption was divided
equally between centrifugation and microﬁltration.
•
The energy consumption of the AC clean-up step is neglected assumed to run via gravitational force.
•
Activated carbon from AC clean-up is recycled using existing ecoinvent v3.6 process.
•
Amount of sulphuric acid added during crystallisation is calculated based on the pH difference from 7 in fermentation and 2 during crystallisation.
•
The concentration of the sulphuric acid is assumed to be 2M.
•
Chilled water is used for the cooling process within crystallisation since it must be cooled down to 5°C.
•
In Vardon et al. (2016) some of the MA is neither crystallised nor retained in the broth. In the present study it was assumed that the "lost" MA was
retained in the broth and sent to the WWTP.
•
The amount of ethanol used for the further puriﬁcation of the fermentation broth is estimated based on the solubility of MA in ethanol. 10% ethanol
was added as a buffer.
•
The energy consumption of the puriﬁcation using ethanol is assumed to be the same as for the catalystic hydrogenation derived from Corona et al.
(2018a), since both processes mainly consists of the heating of ethanol to 75°C. It was scaled to the amount of MA instead of ADA.
•
It is assumed that the ethanol used in the ethanol puriﬁcation step can be recovered and used in the catalytic hydrogenation step.
•
Salts extracted are sent to an inert landﬁll.
•
The catalyst of the catalytic hydrogenation process will be recovered.
•
Ethanol is recovered from the catalytic hydrogenation process.
•
Hydrogen is recovered from the catalytic hydrogenation process.
•
The fraction of catalyst and catalyst support of the Rh/C catalyst was assumed to be the same as for the Ni/C catalyst used in the lignin ﬁrst process
derived from Anderson et al. (2016a), being 5% active catalyst and 95% carbon support of the total mass.
Bio-ADA: Other processes
•
The residual solids from the lignin ﬁrst process is assumed to mainly consist of holocellulose.
•
The residual solids are combusted substituting heat.
•
A lower heating value was estimated based on the glucan, xylan, and lignin composition and literature values.
•
The lower heating value was assigned to the total mass of the residual solids of the lignin ﬁrst process.
•
A heat efﬁciency of 90% was assumed for the biomass combustion plant.
•
The integrated biomass combustion process in EASETECH v3.3.7 was assumed to be representative for the biomass combustion process.
•
The generated bottom ash of the biomass combustion plant is assumed to be treated at a bottom ash landﬁll.

•
Water was added to the WWTP to account for cleaning water from the bio-ADA production plant.

obtained by conventional glucose production and 2G glucose production.
==PAGE:153==
A.15
Sensitivity scenarios applicable to bio-based BDO and ADA
A.15.1
2G biomass scenarios
General
The procedure to model multifunctional processes varies a lot among LCAs in literature, including
the way the burden from 2G biomass is accounted for. The present study accounts for the system
expansion related to substituting the services that corn stover currently provides (fodder and bed-
ding). The fodder service was replaced by soybean meal production, while the bedding service was
replaced by straw production. To test the sensitivity of the results towards 2G biomass modelling
procedure a number of scenarios are deﬁned.
Full burden scenario: In the baseline scenarios for bio-based BDO and ADA it is assumed that 39%
of the corn stover is "burden free". In this scenario it is assumed that there is no "burden free" corn
stover. Instead the 39% are divided equally between fodder and bedding services. This results in
46% of the corn stover services being replaced by soybean meal production, and 54% of the corn
stover being replaced by straw production.
Burden free scenario: In the baseline scenarios for bio-based BDO and ADA it is assumed that 39%
of the corn stover is "burden free". In this scenario it is assumed that 100% of the corn stover is
"burden free". This entails that no soybean meal production or straw production is included in the
system boundaries to account for corn stover removed from fodder and bedding services.
Mass allocation scenario: In the baseline scenarios for bio-based BDO and ADA system expansion
is used to account for the corn stover used in production. In this scenario allocation on the basis
of mass is used to assign the burdens of corn production to corn stover and corn grain production.
According to Iowa Corn Promotion Board (2013) 47-56% of the corn plant is stover, while the rest
is grain. Hence, in this scenario it is assumed that 50% of the corn production burden is assigned
to corn grain and 50% of the burden is assigned to corn stover. The soybean meal and production
processes are disregarded, and instead the ecoinvent v3.6 process "maize grain production, US"
has been included for half of the corn stover required in bio-based BOD and ADA production,
respectively.
A.15.2
Renewable energy scenario
The energy grid mix deﬁned for the present study is based on natural gas, which is estimated as the
marginal technology in the US. Since the energy system may transition towards more renewable
energy, it is relevant to test the effects of a renewable energy mix on the production of bio-based
BDO and ADA. The renewable electricity mix was assumed consisting of equal parts wind, solar
and hydro power, while the renewable heat is derived from combustion of wood chips. These
renewable energy mixes are used as proxies for potential future grid mixes. The ecoinvent v3.6
processes used for the renewable electricity mix are "high voltage, electricity production, hydro,
pumped storage, US-MRO", "high voltage, electricity production, solar thermal parabolic trough,
==PAGE:154==
50 MW,US-WECC ", and "high voltage, electricity production, wind, 1-3MW turbine, onshore,
US-MRO". As the process used for solar energy was not speciﬁc to the region in this study, the
heat used in this process was replaced by natural gas heat deﬁned for the present study baseline.
Losses related to conversion from high to medium voltage were accounted for. The ecoinvent
v3.6 process used for the renewable heat is "heat and power co-generation, wood chips, 6667 kW,
state-of-the-art 2014, US-MRO".
A.15.3
LCIA methodology scenario
The LCIA method used in the present study is ReCiPe 2016 with 11 selected impact categories
out of 18. Since the LCIA methodology can be a source of uncertainty in the results, it is rele-
vant to test the sensitivity of the results to a change in LCIA method. The ILCD 2017 method,
which is a collection of impact categories from other methods, which are recommended by ILCD,
includes 14 impact categories. Five of these impact categories overlap with the impact categories
included in the present study from ReCiPe2016. These include climate change (IPCC 2013), fos-
sil resource scarcity (CML 2001), freshwater ecotoxicity (USEtox), and human carcinogenic and
non-carcinogenic toxicity (USEtox). The results are compared on a basis of person equivalents
obtained from each of the two methods, since the characterised impacts do not have equal units
between the two methods (except for climate change). This means that the scenario tests the sen-
sitivity of the results to differences in both characterisation and normalisation methods for ReCiPe
2016 and ILCD 2017.
A.16
Sensitivity scenarios for bio-based BDO
BDO
A.16.0.1
1G glucose scenario
The bio-BDO baseline scenario uses sugars obtained from pretreated corn stover as the feedstock
for the fermentation process, which is a 2G biomass (not a human food source). However, the
current bio-BDO production by Novamont’s Mater Biotech facility relies on glucose obtained from
1G biomass as the feedstock for fermentation. Hence, a scenario where glucose is obtained from
corn starch (1G biomass) was tested. All unit processes upstream of the fermentation process were
disregarded and instead the ecoinvent v3.6 process "market for glucose, GLO" was used. The
amount of glucose required to produce 1 kg BDO (taking into account downstream losses) is 2.7
kg. Additional water is added to the fermentation, since the 1G glucose contains less water than
the sugar water from 2G biomass.
A.16.0.2
No xylose conversion scenario
In Yim et al. (2011) it is described that the Genomatica engineered E. coli strain for bio-BDO pro-
duction is developed using glucose feedstock, although the strain has also been tested for conversion
of other carbohydrate feedstocks including xylose. Yim et al. (2011) reports "approximately equal
==PAGE:155==
efﬁciency of BDO production" from xylose as from glucose, however the test was only carried out
at laboratory scale, and xylose is not currently being used as feedstock in industrial scale production
of BDO by Novamont’s Mater Biotech. Hence, industrial production of bio-BDO may not reach
similar efﬁciency in converting xylose to bio-BDO, as was assumed in the current bio-BDO base-
line. Therefore it is tested what the effect would be of only converting glucose sugars to bio-BDO.
The amount of glucose required to produce 1 kg BDO (taking into account downstream losses) is
2.7 kg. This requires more corn stover in the pretreatment process, namely, 11.6 kg instead of 7
kg. The upstream processes is scaled up accordingly, while no water is added to the fermentation,
since there is already enough water in the sugar water.
A.16.0.3
Alternative wastewater scenario
The bio-BDO baseline includes two different types of WWT. The upstream WWT is based on
adapted Aspen simulation data from Davis et al. (2015), while there was no data found in literature
about the treatment of wastewater from downstream bio-BDO production. For the downstream
WWT in the bio-BDO baseline an ecoinvent v3.6 process for treatment of wastewater from veg-
etable oil reﬁnery was used (scaled to the estimated COD in the bio-BDO wastewater). Since the
upstream WWT data (obtained from Davis et al. (2015)) does not include concentration of pollu-
tants in the treated wastewater released to the environment, it is relevant to test the effect of using
a more complete WWT dataset both upstream and downstream. The COD content in the upstream
wastewater was estimated similarly to the COD content in the downstream wastewater (theoretical
oxygen demand based on elemental composition of impurities in the wastewater, See Appendix
A.1 (attached) for calculations). The upstream WWT for the bio-BDO baseline was disregarded,
including the biogas and sludge combustion.
A.16.0.4
No drying of biomass scenario
In the bio-BDO baseline the harvested corn stover is assumed to be dried from 30 to 9% water
content at a facility near the corn crops to reduce the mass and volume for efﬁcient transport to the
bio-BDO production plant. As the drying electricity contributes with 36% of the total electricity
demand of the bio-BDO production it is tested what the effect of not drying the biomass before
transport is. It should be noted that this will increase the mass of corn stover being transported,
which will increase the environmental impacts of this process, and also the cost of it. The electricity
demand for drying and water content reduction was disregarded leading to transport of ≈7 kg
biomass instead of ≈5 kg.
==PAGE:156==
A.17
Sensitivity scenarios for bio-based ADA
ADA
The following section provides descriptions of sensitivity analysis made for the bio-based ADA
production to simulate further optimised processes as well as assessing the effect of some modelling
choices. Three sensitivity scenarios are made speciﬁcally for the bio-based ADA production, which
will be described in the following. The mass balances of the described scenarios can be found in
Appendix A.1 (attached).
A.17.0.1
Increased titer scenario
To simulate further optimisation of the bio-based ADA production a sensitivity scenario is made
increasing the ﬁnal titer from 100 g/L to 150 g/L. Increasing the titer will have an effect on the
amount of water used for fermentation as well as the amount of nutrients used. When the volume
of the fermentation broth is reduced a reduction in the energy consumption is assumed. It will
furthermore affect the entire water use and amount of wastewater to treat. The titer is known to
have signiﬁcant importance regarding puriﬁcation steps since it reduces the operation cost of the
puriﬁcation steps increasing the feasibility of the production of bio-chemicals (Villadsen et al.,
2011).
The set up of the model was not optimal for assessing the titer, since the titer is not included
as a parameter in the model, but as a parameter in a calculation sheet. Ideally, the amount of
water, nutrients and electricity should be entered in the model being dependent on the titer or
to make parameter simulations for instance in Aspen to determine the different process inputs,
which was done in Corona et al. (2018b). In that way no affect of the titer will be neglected.
In the present study the titer changed the amount of water and addition of nutrient as well as
reduced the energy consumption using the same relative change as for the water reduction within
the puriﬁcation processes.
A.17.0.2
Full lignin oil conversion scenario
The further metabolic engineering sensitivity scenario of the bio-based ADA process will assume
that P. putida can be engineered to convert all the aromatic compounds. It is currently known that
metabolic engineered P. putida is able to convert p-coumaric acid and to some extent ferulic acid,
which are the two main constituents of the lignin oil accounting for 38% and 24%, respectively (see
Figure 6.6. Adding propyl guaiacol and propyl syringol to the convertible aromatic compounds
would lead to the conversion of 88% of the lignin oil. If a larger part of the lignin oil is converted
to MA the feedstock requirement is assumed to decrease drastically, affecting the pretreatment
process of the bio-based ADA production. Reducing the amount of solvent, and catalyst and hence
the energy consumption. This scenario can be considered a best case scenario, and whether it is
realistic to engineer an organism to convert all the aromatic compounds contained in the lignin oil
is not assessed. The sensitivity scenario will assume the same molar MA yield for the the other
==PAGE:157==
aromatic compound as for p-coumaric acid.
A.17.0.3
Glucose substitution scenario
In the present study the solids from the chemical pretreatment of the corn stover are assumed to be
combusted generating heat. However, this is an unrealistic scenario, since the holocellulose can be
converted to sugars, which can be used in different production processes (e.g bio-BDO). Therefore,
a sensitivity scenario will be performed to credit the avoided corn production by converting the
cellulose from corn stover into sugar. To do so a glucose potential of the holocellulose is estimated
based on the glucose yield from corn stover retrieved from Davis et al. (2015). Resulting in a glu-
cose yield of 1.18 kg glucose/kg cellulose. The amount of available cellulose is found assuming
a cellulose yield of 90% from Davis et al. (2015). The glucose potential of the residual solids is
estimated to be 9.1 kg. To credit the potential glucose production is modelled as avoided glucose
production by substituting regular glucose production from corn starch using the ecoinvent v3.6
process ’market for glucose, GLO’. A correction factor of 0.75 is assumed to account for the dif-
ference in the glucose quality. Producing glucose from 2G biomass also results in the production
of xylose and the water content differs from regular glucose, which is a C5 sugar opposing some
challenges when used as feedstock for other processes requiring glucose Davis et al. (2015).
==PAGE:158==

INL (2013) ,

(2015)
30% water content in harvested biomass according to
INL (2013) . The amount of biomass needed to
produce 1 kg of BDO is calculated based on the sugar
content in the sugar water obtained from upstream
pretreatment of the biomass in line with Davis et al.
(2015) (glucose 28% wt. and xylose 15% wt.) and the
fermentation yields of BDO from the sugars (0.4 g
BDO/g glucose and 0.468 g BDO/g xylose).
Furthermore, a loss of 7.5% of the BDO in the
downstream processing is accounted for.

Cellulase dry
0.018
kg
Davis et al.
(2015)
According to the Davis et al. (2015) design basis the
cellulase loading is 10 mg enzyme protein/g cellulose,
however in the mass balance it is 12 mg protein/g
cellulose. In this study 20 mg/g is used, since the
abovementioned values are considered too optimistic.
There is 80.778 g cellulose/kg conditioned slurry. In
Davis et al. (2015) the cellulase production takes place
on site, while in the present study the cellulase is
obtained from the market, and consumption of
materials and energy related to cellulase production in
Davis et al. (2015) have been excluded. Scaled from
pretreatment of 104167 kg biomass/hr in Davis et al.
(2015) to 6.172 kg/hr (equivalent to production of 1 kg
BDO in this study).

343.483
kg
Satam et al.
(2019)
It is assumed that 99.6% of the cooling water entering
the plant exits the plant to its original source (once
through water cooling system). The remaining 0.4% is
assumed evaporated in the process, and is not
modelled. The assumption is based on a parameter for
evaporation in once-through cooling systems of 0.004
used in the ecoinvent v3.6 process for chilled water
"cooling energy, from natural gas, at cogen unit with
absorption chiller 100kW, CH". Scaled from
production of 100000 tons BDO in Satam et al. (2019)
to 1 kg BDO in the present study.

the water distillation step (water distillation is one of
seven downstream processes). See mass balance for
more detailed composition.

through water cooling system). The remaining 0.4% is

used in the ecoinvent v3.6 process for chilled water

GBL waste stream
(to GBL

0.016
kg
In the BDO distillation step BDO is removed at the
column top, while GBL and other impurities are
removed as waste. Additionally, 7.5% of the BDO that
comes out of the bioconversion is lost in downstream
processing, and it is assumed that 1/7 of this is lost in
the BDO distillation step (BDO distillation is one of
seven downstream processes). The GBL waste stream
has a fuel value of 6.4 MJ/kg wet mass. See mass
balance for more detailed composition.

Table A.20.20: Life cycle inventory of the unit process "Upstream wastewater treatment" for the bio-BDO production.
Upstream wastewater treatment
For 1 kg BDO
Value
Unit
Source
Comments/assumptions
Database process
Reference product
Upstream
wastewater
treatment
1.00
unit process
The wastewater treatment plant described in Davis
et al. (2015) serves an integrated process for
pretreatment of biomass and catalytic conversion and
upgrading to hydrocarbons. The present study only
includes the wastewater treatment that is related to the
black liquor and ﬁlter retentate, and not to wastewater
from other unit processes. 77% of the wastewater
treatment has been allocated to the present study
(biomass pretreatment), while 23% has been excluded.
The allocation key is based on an average of the ﬂow
and impurities in the wastewater coming from included
(58% of the ﬂow, 96% of the impurities) and excluded
unit processes (42% of the ﬂow and 4% of the
impurities).

Inputs from other processes
Sludge (from
upstream
wastewater
treatment)
0.603
kg
Davis et al.
(2015)
The sludge has 40% dry matter, and a fuel value of
16.2 MJ/kg dry matter (Kim et al (2005),
Aragón-Briceño et al (2017)). However, the average
fuel value (including water) is only 6.3 MJ/kg, which
is low for combustion. This means that an auxiliary
fuel could be added to aid the process, however, it is
assumed in this study that no auxiliaries are added and
that the sludge is only burned for the sake of managing
the mass, and not for energy recovery. Scaled from
pretreatment of 104167 kg biomass/hr in Davis et al.
(2015) to 6.172 kg/hr (equivalent to production of 1 kg
BDO in this study).

EASETECH
v.3.3.7
The amount of energy in the lignin waste stream is
20.7 MJ/kg dry matter and from this value the heat of
vaporisation (2.453 MJ/kg for water at 20°C) is
subtracted for each kg of water in the lignin waste
stream (62% water content). The remaining energy is
transformed to heat energy with an efﬁciency of 90%.
The generated heat is substituted 1:1 with the marginal
energy deﬁned for the US in the present study.

Table A.20.24: Life cycle inventory of the unit process "Upstream electricity utilities" for the bio-BDO production.
Utilities electricity upstream
For 1 kg BDO
Value
Unit
Source
Comments/assumptions
Database process
Reference product
Utilities upstream
1.00
unit process
Delivery of utilities to the pretreatment plant (air and
process water).
Inputs from other processes
Inputs from technosphere
Electricity
0.104
kWh
Davis et al.
(2015)
Scaled from pretreatment of 104167 kg biomass/hr in
Davis et al. (2015) to 6.172 kg/hr (equivalent to
production of 1 kg BDO in this study). The utilities in
Davis et al. (2015) serve an integrated process for
pretreatment of biomass and catalytic conversion and
upgrading to hydrocarbons. The present study only
includes the utilities that are related to the air and
process water delivery to unit processes included in the
present study, and not from excluded unit processes.
48% of the utilities have been allocated to the present
study (biomass pretreatment), while 52% has been
excluded. The allocation key is based on an average of
air and process water delivered to included unit
processes (35% of the air, 60% of the process water)
and excluded unit processes (65% of the air and 40%
of the process water).

GBL waste stream
0.0157
kg
Satam et al.
(2019)
(adapted
mass
balance)
The GBL waste stream has 100% dry matter, and a
fuel value of 6.4 MJ/kg (Satam et al., 2019), which is
low for combustion. This means that an auxiliary fuel
could be added to aid the process, however, it is
assumed in this study that no auxiliaries are added and
that the GBL waste stream is only burned for the sake
of managing the mass, and not for energy recovery.
Scaled from production of 100000 tons BDO in Satam
et al. (2019) to 1 kg BDO in the present study. See
mass balance for more detailed composition.

Estimation

v3.6 "wastewater from vegetable oil reﬁnery" used a
COD content of 42 kg/m3. Hence, the wastwater
treatment required to treat the amount of COD in the
current study was scaled based on the amount of COD
in the two wastewaters.

Fermentation broth
(to centrifugation)
13.196
kg
The ﬁnal cis,cis-muconic acid is 100 g/L. The yield is
0.94 mol/mol Salvachúa et al. (2018).
This stream also includes by-products, not converted
aromatic compounds of the lignin oil, cells, water,
sodium hydroxide ect.
The by-products are modelled as being
protocatechuate, since the by-products consists of
intermediates within the metabolic reaction, which
typically are protocatechuate and catechol. The
protocatechuate yield is assumed to be 0.06 mol/mol.

Combustion of

Inputs from other processes
Solids -
holocellulose
(from lignin ﬁrst)
20.502
kg
Assuming the remaining corn stover after lignin
extraction mainly consists of holocellulose.
Assuming the solids from the lignin ﬁrst process are
burnt. In reality the holocellulose would very likely be
used for the production of 2G sugar.
Inputs from technosphere
Biomass
combustion plant
The biomass combustion plant includes a theoretical
process, where the energy content is changed due to
the evaporation of water, when the wet biomass is
heated before it starts burning.
A lower heating value of the solids was estimated
based on lower heating values found in literature for
non-wood lignin, cellulose and hemicellulose and a
weighted average was made based on the composition
of corn stover.
EASETECH v3.3.7
"Combustion plant,
manufacturing industry
(boiler between 50 and 300
MW), wood and similar
(updated in 2016), DK".
EASETECH v3.3.7 "WTE
- Change of energy content,
generic, DK, 2012".
The substituted heat
generated was changed
from DK to the marginal
energy of US; the
composition of which can
be seen in Section 6.3.

processes.

Water
137.977
kg
Water was added to account for not accounted water in
the back-ﬂush of the activated carbon ﬁlter in AC
clean-up and other cleaning processes. The amount of
water added is calculated based on the COD of the
wastewater stream and is decreased to 42 kg/m3,
which is equivalent to the COD of wastewater from
vegetable oil reﬁnery.
See section 6.3 for marginal
energy process description.
Wastewater
treatment of
149.724
kg
The lignin oil results in high COD. The WWTP found
in ecoinvent with the highest COD was "wastewater
from vegetable oil reﬁnery". The COD of the
wastewater stream was scaled to have a COD of 42
kg/m3.

of wastewater from
vegetable oil reﬁnery,
